URUGUAY
NATURAL RESOURCES MANAGEMENT AND IRRIGATION
DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
LOAN AND PROJECT SUMMARY
Borrower                Republic of Uruguay
Loan Amount:            U'.$41.0 million equivalent
Terms:                  Repayable in 15 years, including five years of grace at the
Bank's standard variable interest rate.
Project Objectives:     The main objective of the proposed natural resource oriented
project would be to develop and implement a soil and water
management strategy. The latter would target irrigation
development, as a way to increase, diversify, and sustain
agricultural output and exports. The strategy would specifically
focus on: (a) supporting environmentally sound investments in
rehabilitation and development of irrigation/drainage schemes
and related service infrastructure; (b) improving surace and
ground water use efficiency; (c) establishing a well-balanced
operation and maintenance (O&M) and capital cost recovery
policy; (d) setting the stage for improved natural resource
management practices by supporting water and soil
management/conservation, and priority applied research and
technology transfer subprojects; and (e) providing technical
assistance for forestry development and non-traditional
agricultural export activities.
Project Description:    Components include: (a) Natural Resource Management,
comprising: (i) pilot development schemes in micro-catchment
areas of the Santa Lucia River Basin to gain experience in the
management of ecologically fragile zones; (ii) demonstration
farms in selected agro-ecological areas suffering soil degradation
and erosion problems; (iii) a Geographical Information System
to provide the groundwork for improved land use classification
aid management in the country; (iv) applied research and
technology transfer activities focussed on modern techniques of
soil and water management; (v) special studies; (vi)
strengthening of policy planning, evaluation, and monitoring
capacity at the level of the Directorates of Natural Resources at
MGAP, and Hydrography at MTOP, respectively; and (vii)
technical assistance for forestry development. (b) rrigation
Development, comprising: (i) the rehabilittion and



- ii -
modernization of existing public irrigation and drainage (I&D)
schemes where use, maintenance and admistration would be
transferred to users under appropriate capital cost recovery and
O&M arrangements; (ii) construction of new medium-size
collective irrigation and drainage schemes for the benefit of user
groups under appropriate capital cost re-.overy and O&M
arrangements; (iii) construction of selected small farmer
irrigation infrastucture (i.e., small-scale earthills, irrigation
and drainage canals and tubewells); (iv) water harvesting 'pilot"
program at the farm level to help develop an optimal surface
water use strategy; and (v) technical assistance for feasibility
studies of irrigation investment projects, and for production,
processing, and marketing of (mostly irrigated) non-traditional
expor, products to be produced under these I&D schemes.
Implementation of the two components would be suppored by
technical assistance to be provided by the project through the
Project Executing Unit (UEP).
Benefits:               The project would serve as a vehicle to assist the Government of
Uruguay to develop and implement a soil and water management
strategy leading to irrigation subsector growth. The main
project benefits would include: (a) expansion of the irrigated
area benefittmng mostly medium and small farmers in rainfed
areas; (b) increased productivity and investment in higher-value
export crops resulting from an expansion in the availability of
secure irrigation water; (c) enhanced small farmer income and
welfare; (d) improved public and private resource allocation by
introducing a comprehensive capital cost recovery and O&M
policy; and (e) improved natural resource management and
conservation practices leading to sustained agriculural sector
growth.
Povrty Category:        Not applicable.
Risks:                  The institutional capacity to execute and coordinate the project,
especially the naural resources assessment and management
aspects, may prove inadequate. This risk would be mitigated by
a strong focus on institutional strengthening and development,
and the inclusion of specific, programmed actions to fill
identified capacity gaps.

DEVEL O PROJEI
I. PROJECT AND SECOR BACKGROUND
A. Agh.j~eIn the Ecoomy
The A4riculural Sector's Performance
1.1   The agricultural sector in Umguay contributes about 10% of GDP, direcdy employs
16% of the work force, and makes a major contribution to foreign exchange earnings.
Sectoral exports, including processed agricultural raw materials, with an annual average
value of nearly US$1,000 million, represented over 80% of toul export value in the late
1980s. During 1970-81, the sector grew at less than 1% per annum, whereas GDP as a
whole grew at nearly 3% per annum. Following the recession of 1982-85, which resulted in
a sector output decline of over 12% in real terms, thd agricultural sector and the rest of the
economy have yet to reun to the output level of 1980/81 despite the positive grwth rates
shown since 1985. During 1985-1990, agricultural GDP grew at an average rate of 2% per
1.2 Although the composition of agricultural output and exports has been changing during
the 1980s, given the natural resource base of the country, the agriculural sector is sfill
largely dependent on the performance of the livestock subsector, which has traditionally
accounted for nearly two-thirds of agricultural output and 70% of agricultural exports. Thus,
the stagnation of the livestock subsector explains to a large degree the poor performance of
the sector as a whole. The large decline in area under cultivation of traditional crops, due to
the removal of artificial price supports, has also adversely affected the sector's performance.
1.3   The decline in area has been particularly sharp in the case of wheat and maize, which
decreased by 44% each over the last decade, although total wheat output fell by only 4%
dur'ig that period as marginal producers were eliminated. More recently a new
enuitymiaeurial class, more inclined to capital inteusive techniques than traditional producers,
has been marketing non-traditonal agricultural exports such as rice, soya beans, barley,
citrus and some horticultural products, whose combined exports were nearly US$160 million
in 1990. In recen years wood products have also been added to these non-traditional
exports. The relatve importance of wood production in Uruguay was rather marginal in the
past, with total exports totalling no more than US$250,000 per year. However, with the new
stimulus to expand forestation and forest euports, supported under the Bank's Second
Agricultural Development Project (Ln. 3131-UR; US$132.4 million total cost), total annnal
forestry exports ineased to nearly US$4 million in 1990/91.



-2-
Cumett kbUc ERYkRumm    c41aw
1.4   The present Government has reaffirmed its commiment to the process of structual
reform and an open economy as the basis for economic development. Along those lines,
Uruguay has taken decisive action in support of free capital markets, market determined
exchange and interest rates, freedom of exchange transactions, and reducing or eliminatwg
price controls or subsidies. Furthermore, agriculturl policy is now focussed on capitalizing
the codntry's comparative advantage. Whether this will result in accelerated agricull
investment and output will igely dtpend on the private sector's confidence in the
ustainability of the reforms.
1.5   The primary objective of the Goverment's agricultural strategy is to increase
productvity, output and exports, while preserving the country's natural resource base and
achieving a balanced distribution of benefits across the economy. In recent years,
encuraging results from a number of agricultural export products, including dairy, citrus,
malting barley, rice and fish, indicate potential for significant diversification and that the
-priae sector is capable of responding to a favorable economic climate. The explicit long-
term strategy is diversification. The progressive removal of remaining market distortions,
the generation of public goods through improved research-based technology, technical
assistance, and infras     development, plus Government support for private investment
should reinforce this trend.
1.6   The Govermment financial sector reforms have not yet mcceeded in extending the
maturity of domestic instuments; the capital market remains underdeveloped and there is
itte long-ter  ilnancing. Limited availability of term financing could jeopardize the private
sector drive towards diversificat; )n and the development of new exports. However, term
financing constrai  would be significantly mitigated through the Private Sector
Development Project to be cofinanced with IDB, which is expected to become effective by
December 1993 (prior to effectiveness of the proposed Naural Resource Management and
Irrigation Development Project). The Global Credit Project would provide the necessary
financial resources to the banking system to ensure that sufficient long-term credit would be
available to producers to realize the export potential that has opened up as a result of trade
and policy reforms.
B. Natural Resource Use In Agriculture
1.7 Uruguay has a total land area of 15.6 million ha of which about 86% are under
permane  pasture, 4% under forest cover and only 1.3 million ha (8%) are presendy under
cultivation. Non agricultural land amounts to only 2% of the territory. About 4 million ha
are potenftally suitable for cash crops. However, there is a need to rotate with pasture to
avoid soil degradation, thus ihniting cropping to 1.6 million ha in any one year. Most of the
agricultural land is currently cultivated under rainfed conditons but could benefit from
supplementary irrigation if water became available.
1.8   Although extensive production systems predominate, paridularly in the livestock
areas, and average fanm sizes are increasg, there is evidence of localized over-use of land
in crop prodction and some overgz , with the consequent degradation of soils and



-3-
erosion. About one-third of the counry's total area, including some land under pasure, is
affected by some degree of soil erosion. But the more severely affected areas, showing
moderate to severe soil erosion problems, are those devoted to intensive agricuture in the
crescent around Montevideo, which includes parts of the Santa Lucia River Basin (i.e., the
D-partments of Canelones, Florida, and San Jose) representing nearly 9% of the country's
total area. In these areas, but particularly in Canelones, the smaller size of holdings forces
fanners to cultivate land intensively, with little regard for soil conservation. Consequently,
despite the country's gentle topography, it would seem that inappropriate land-use and
cultivation practices were responsible for loss of soil fertility and structure. While the extent
of soil degradation has not yet reached alarming proportions, the problems are likely to
become more serious as current sectoral policies succeed in encouraging a more intensive and
diversified agricultural production.
1.9   The main rainfed cropping area, the Litoral, while not constrained by the small size
of the average farm, is an area of relatively intensive crop production that has also seen a
shortening of crop rotation over time and, consequendy, is also suffering from soil
degradation. The short life of improved pastures has imposed a short crop rotation of one or
two years of crops alternating with only tree or four years of pastures in the region. The
outcome of this practice is accelerated land degradation, with high risk of some aeas aroud
the country soon becoming unproductive (which has already occurred around Montevideo) if
measures are not taken to reverse the trend.
1. 10 There are about 4.5 million ha suitable for forestry production in Uruguay; however,
in the late 1980s, of the 620,000 ha under forest cover there were only about 170,000 ha
planted with Eucalyptus, pine trees, and other industrial species and the average rate of
planting was about 3,000 ha a year. This figure has increased to about 14,000 ha a year
shice 1991, and a preliminary estimate of approximately 20,000 ha in 1992, mainly as a
result of a new incentive framework implemented by Government.
1 .11 In terms of relative importance, irrigated land covered about 160,000 ba in 1992, or
less than 1% of the county's land used for crops or livestock. Although marginal compared
to rainfed agricultre and livestock production, the irrigated area has grown in recent years
largely in response to eWort oppornmities for rice and nontraditional export products, such
as fresh fruits and vegetables, whose combined area increased from 150,000 ha to 200,000
ha between 1980 and 1989. Growth, however, has taken place without much regard for
appropriate natural resources management.
C. Main Characterisfics of Irrigated Agricultur
Backmound
1.12 Mean annual rainfall exceeds 1,000 mm in most regions of Uruguay. However, due
to a combination of eraic and irregular distibution, high potental evapotranspiration during
the summer period (November-February), and predominantly shallow soils with limited wat
holding capacity, the productivity of rainfed agriculture is frequently limited by water deficits
of variable duration, affecting both yields and quality of crops. As a result, supplemeny
irrigation is generally reqired to obtain adequate production levels of rice, sugarcane, and



- 4 -
horticultural and fruit crops, with rainfed agriculture mostly limited to extensive, low
investment crops such as winter cereals, maize, sorghum and fodder crops.
1.13  The irrigated area has grown from about 50,000 ha in 1970 to an estimated 160,000
ha in 1992 (para. 1.1 1), as a result of the private sector's response to import substitution and
new export opportunities. Rice expanded significantly since 1990 and is the major irrigated
crop, representing almost 80% of the irrigated area. Following in importance are sugarcane
with 8%, orchards with 6%, and horticultural crops with 4% of the irrigated area. The
remaining 2% refers to maize and fodder production. Export value of irrigated crops totalled
about US$120 million in 1990, or just over 10% of the agricultural sector's total exports.
1.14 The private sector has been the driving force in irrigation development in Uruguay.
Public sector participation has been limited to the construction of some small schemes for
land settlement projects (i.e., Colonia Espafia: 815 ha, and Tomes Bertetta 360 ha) and some
small- and medium-sized irrigation works (e.g., India Muerta 10,000 ha; Canel6n Grande,
1,100 ha potentially irnigable; Aguas Blancas, 120 ha potentially irrigable). Some of these
schemes are dilapidated due to poor maintenance, while others have never been used for lack
of conveyance infrastructure. However, compared to the country's potential, growth in
irrigation investment has been disappointing and restricted mainly to large farmers. The
main reason behind this seems to be the relatively high risk of irrigation investment as
perceived by farmers in general-especially by small-and medium-sized producers-and the
organizational problems attached to the execution of irrigation works that would serve several
producers.
1.15 The Government is keen to continue diversifying the agricultural sector's output away
from livestock and extensive crop production and to intensify the use of arable land. To that
end, the modernization of the sector, with emphasis on the country's comparative
advantages, appears a key element. Under this framework, the promotion of private
investment in irrigation and adequate natural resources management is seen by the
Government as a priority. Furthermore, irrigation is perceived as the main tool to encourage
both a more intensive and enviromnentally sound use of agricultural land and the adoption of
modern technologies by producers. The notion is to profit from the export opportunities that
the non-traditional crops seem to have. With the same objective in mind, the Government is
also proposing to rehabilitate or complete the existing state-owned irrigation schemes,
transferring their use and administration (i.e., operation and maintenance) to the
beneficiaries.
Development Potential
1.16  Despite the lack of precise estimates of the potentially irrigable lands in Uruguay,
frere would seem to be agreement that a significant proportion of the 1.6 million ha currently
cultivated under rainfed conditions could benefit from supplementary irrigation if water were
made available and adequate natural resources management techniques were applied. The
type of works required to develop new sources of irrigation varies from region to region and
from product to product. In the north western part of the country, for example, where small
farms prevail, irrigation development is likely to be based on the use of both ground water
from the Salto aquifer and water from rivers and streams. In the north-eastern regions,
where the size of the farms is larger and the topography is adequate for dam constuction,
gravity-based irrigation schemes are likely to be the norm. In the southern part of the



- 5 -
country, the Raig6n aquifer will be the most important source of irrigation water for the
expansion of intensive production such as deciduous fruits and horticulture based on smal
farm strucure. In the eastern legion there are no major aquifers and irrigation expansion
would have to be based on pumping water from regulated nvers and steams and the
construction of new small water catchments (i.e., earthfills) for gravity based i kgation.
Farm structure and topography both work in favor of tis kind of irrigation system. An
overview of the irrigation subsector is presentd in Annex A.
1.17 In the past, profitable investment opporunities in irrigation could be found in those
crops in which the country had clear comparative advantages (rice, citrus), or when domestic
marketing oppormnities for off-season products existed (horticulure), c; in those crops for
which high protection was offered by govement policies (sugarcane). Given the
Govermen's export oriented development policy, future growth of irrigated agricultur is
likely to contue based on private initiative. This growth would be largely oriented towards
the development of those crops that have already established themselves in the export market,
e.g. rice, citrus and some deciduous fruits and horticultural products. With the required
infrastrucum (particularly irrigation) to ensure quality and timely delivery of the final
product, many commercial farmers could become out-growers to supply the unsatisfied
demand of exporters of nontraditional horticultural crops. A number of such exporters are
crently offering assistance to potential suppliers, by providing agricultural inputs (i.e.,
seeds, chemicals, and ferdlizers) and technical advice on crop management practices. The
pace at which this development takes place will depend on the Government's ability to
maintain an incentive structure conducive to expanded agricultural exports as well as a
commitmen to support expanded private investment in agriculture.
1.18  Irrigation development has been spearheaded by a small group of entrepreneurial
amers, particularly rce growers, and some agro-indusril companies. Co-existing with
these entrepreneurial farmers, however, is a wide range of more traditional farmers, who
usuafly have limited experience with irrigated crops, but see irrigated agriculture as the only
means of escaping the constraints of the domestic market. However, these farmers seem
unable to develop their farms into a modem commercial operation due to technical and
financial limitations. Their production practices and constraints vary by region and type of
crop.
Maor Constraints to r3igation Derelopment
1.19 One of the main technical constraints to greater and more intensive land use by
agriculture and the development of nontraditional exports is the lack of irrigation. However,
investment in irrigation is constrained by the negative effects that the private sector's lack of
confidence about the permanent nature of Government policies and the uncertainties about the
future course of the economy have on investment, aggravated by their perception of the high
risk of irrigation investment under those conditions.
1.20 There will be cases where direct access to rivers or lakes is not possible and a
common infraucture to supply water to a group of several small-and medium-size farms
would be required. While the areas involved in each case would be relatively small, the cost
of the investment would be high from the individual farmer's viewpoint. Further, the
medium-term maturity of investment for some of these nontraditonal exports (e.g. frit-
trees) and the need to have some kind of producer organization for the execution and



-6 -
subqun operation and maintce of the irrigation works, combined with the
uncertaiies typical of a new activity, would make such an investment a high risk venture
for small-and medium-size frmers. Under thse conditions, the Government can act as a
catalytic element for the oranization of producers, providing an adequate regulatory
framework, facilitating access to financial resources, and even sharing part of the risk of the
proposed investment with the water users.
1.21 Much of the past evolution towards improved irrigation and water distribution
methods (e.g. drip/trickle systems) was stimulated by private, commercial companies
intested in sales of specialized equipment. While this move towards a more sophisticated
irrigation system will continue, a large part of future advancement in irrigation could come
from improvement in gravity distribution systems, which are unlikely to be of interest to
private industry. Hence, there is a need for Government support to develop technologies
suitable for this type of irrigaton in those areas where it is economically viable.
Additionally, some of the existing irrigation schemes were designed for sugar production
(e.g., Colonia Espafia) and would require redesigning to diversify production into new
cropping systems. Furthermore, future irrigation developments should also involve farmers
with limited skldls, who would have no access to private consulting firms to develop
appWriate irrigation technologies.
1.22 Finally, while production techniques are well known by large producers of export
crops, this is not the case with most medium- and small-size producers. There are some
independen agronomists who are familiar with these technologies and are already providing
techical assistance services to producers, but their number is limited. Similarly, although
progressive fairmers, mainly dedicated to high-value crops for export, use sophisticated and
highly efficient on-farm irrigation methods, a large mmber of them use traditional methods
and overall efficiency is low.
1.23 Moreover, in the case of most farmers, natural resource management tschniques have
not been incorporated into their production packages, thus, not all agricultural production
ssms are sustainable under the curent cultural practices. Reconversion of traditional
monoculture agriculture in the West 1itoral to cultivated pasture-crop rotation systems is an
improvement in terms of soil conservation, as are the diversification programs leading
towards a change from monoculture sugarbeet cropping to cultivated pastre-horticulture
stms in the soueast of the country, as well as the expansion of forest plantation, which
are contng to better land use. The more widespread introduction of improved land and
water managent practices, however, is currently constained by the limited knowledge
about crop rotations adapted to the specific needs and development potential of small
fmes. Another limiting factor is the lack of information about existing economically
viable production systems that link soil and water conservation and irrigation or raifed
development to increase productivity and income.
nemkomo Conrint
1.24  In Uruay, there are indications that a process of soil degradation, erosion and
frtilky decline is taking place because of intensive cropping and some overgrazing. While
the problem has not yet reached alarming proportions, MGAP estimates nevertheless indicate
that some 1.5 million ha, or 9% of the total land in the country, is affected by moderate to
severe soil erosion. Additionally, in the development and operation and maintenance (O&M)



-7-
of the irrigation subsector, a number of standard environmental issues have been identified,
such as soil degradation and water contamination from agrochemicals under intensified
agriculture, wasteful use of water, deteriorating quality of irrigation water, some
sedimentation of reservoirs, and damage to biodiversity. Through environmental assessment
and the application of the required correction measures, the proposed project would address
such issues.
1.25 Over the past two decades there has been accelerating pressure on water resources
from the development demands of agriculture (rice in particular), hydroelectricity, urban
water supply, industry and tourism. The projected expansion in small and medium water
catchments for irrigation, hydroelectricity and pota. le water supply, and the generation of
sewage and other wastes discharged to water bodies may lead to severe competition for
available water supplies and to increasing demands for investment to reduce growing levels
of contamination. In the case of the eastern wetlands, expansion of irrigated rice is also seen
as threatening a unique ecosystem and an important flyway for migratory birds. These issues
have recently become a matter of international concern, largely as a result of the Ramsar
Treaty, which officially protects about 435,000 ha of wetlands. The proposed project would
not support in any way irrigation activities within the Ramsar Treaty area or other reserves
as they stand now or as they may be modified in the futnre. Further, in the envirnmenl
assessments of subprojects proposed in the departments of Rocha and Treinta y Tres,
attention wil be given to potential downstream impacts on aUl officiaRy reserved wetland
areas and those proposed for reserves.
1.26 The current capacity of central government agencies and local authorities to carry out
envirmental impact assessments and monitor the application of corrective measures that
might be suggested by those assessments is rather weak. To mitigate the idenified
insttutional constraints, a wide range of technical assistance activities has been programmed
under the proposed project to strengthen the capacity of public and private
institutions/organizations to effectively and efficiently analyze and manage environmental
issues (paas. 2.17 and 2.67).
LAW Framework
1.27 Water Cod. Uruguay's Water Code deals mainly with the norms that regulate water
rights and ownership. It also defines the respo -sibilities of the Water Authority (Ministry of
Transport and Public Works) that inter alia issues private water use rights. Although, in its
last two sections, the Water Code refers to rules and regulations related to water and
riverbank conservation and exploitation, it does not have any specific reference to irrigation
water.
1.28 Existing norms (i.e., Water Code, and Accounting and Financial Administration
Law), are sufficiently broad to allow an adequate implementation of an irrigation
development program as the one proposed under the project. Nevertheless, the Govermment
is currently promoting a new irrigation law in an attempt to modernize the existing legal
frmework for irrigation development in the country. The new Irrigation Law, once
approved, would not change the principles of the existing Water Code, but rather it would
focus on and strengthen operational aspects, introduced under the project, aimed at
improving the efficiency and effectiveness of water use in the irrigation subsector. Under the
proposed project, specialized technical assistance would be provided to assist the Govermment



-8-
in evaluating the need for any possible adjustments to the proposed new Irrigation Law,
which may be required to ensure future equitable and well-balanced distribution of available
water resources (para. 2.36).
1.29 Soil and Water Conservation Law. This law defines the responsibilities of the
Ministry of Agriculture in relation to soil and water management and conservation, as well as
the economic sanctions applicable to farmers who do not comply with the soil and water
conservation norms. The technical norms for soil conservation by type of lands, and the
norms for land reclamation and water use and management are included in the regulations
approved by Parliament in 1990. The existing legislation (Ley 15,239) regulating the use,
management, and conservation of soil and water for agricultural purposes, provides the
legal/administrative framework to strengthen the technical foundation and help incorporate as
common agricultural practices, soil management and conservation activities to be carried out
under the project. Institutional capacity would be strengthened through technical assistance
to the Directorates of Natural Resources at MGAP, and Hydrology at MTOP (para. 2.17).
D. Agricultural Sector Institutional Framework
1.30  Public Sector Institions The Ministry of Agriculture (MGAP) through its
Department of Natural Resources (DGRNR), which includes the Division of Soil and
Water (DSA), is responsible for soil conservation and irrigation development. In practice,
however, very little has been done in both fields. Apart from soil maps for the whole of the
country prepared in the 1970s, the Division's main activities have been: (i) the registry of
land under cropping; (ii) the evaluation of erosion problems in cropping areas and the
organization of a soil and water conservation campaign; (iii) some research on water
management; and (iv) awarding of irrigation permits, and extension activities, mainly related
to soil conservation and soil and water management, undertaken through T&V programs,
field days, and short seminars. Additionally, DGRNR runs a laboratory for soil, water, and
plant analysis.
1.31 The Natdonal Directorate of Hydrography (DNH) of the Ministry of Transport and
Public Works (MTOP) is responsible for issuing water rights (with the prior approval of
MGAP's DGRNR) and for running an information network through its hydrological stations
located throughout the country. The Directorate is well equipped and staffed, and is
effective in its functions. The control of water quality used to be under the jurisdiction of
this Directorate but has now been transferred to the recently created Ministry of Housing and
Environment (MVOTIMA).
1.32 The National Dirctorate for the Environment (DINAMA), within MVOTMA, is
the agency responsible for the control of water quality and industrial liquid effluent. Given
its relatively short life it is difficult to assess its efficiency. Most recently, responsibility for
the management of environmental issues associated with the agricultural sector has been
assigned to DGRNR within MGAP. The National Directorate of Mining and Geology
(DINAMIGE) located within the Ministry of Industry and Energy (MIE), is responsible for
groundwater survey and exploitation and other geological studies and drilling activities.
Another institution relevant to the proposed project is the National Commission for the
Development of the Santa Lucia Basin (CHDCSL). CHDCSL is an honorary commission



-9-
created in December 1990 to promote sustainable development and coordinate the activities
of the various public offices operating within the Santa Lucia River Basin; encourage the
protection and development of min-watersheds; and promote the introduction of appropriate
land management practices within this basin, which includes some areas with irrigation
development potential. The Planning and Budget Offlee (OPP), dependent on the
Presidency, is responsible for the overall programming and public budget allocations across
sectors. Additionally, OPP coordinates the various goverunent bodies responsible for
monitoring of environmental issues. A detailed description of the agricultural sector
institutional framework is given in Annex I.
1.33  Agricultural Support Services. Several public institutions are involved in providing
support services to agricultural production in Uruguay. In terms of crop and soil Research,
the semi-autonomous National Institute for Agricultural Research (INIA) is the most
important with five research centers, namely: La Estanzuela, Treinta y Tres, Las Brujas,
Salto Grande and Tucarembo. Although more limited in scope, the Faculty of Rural Science
of the University of the Republic also conducts resarch programs in its three research
centers located in Salto, Cerro Largo, and Paysandu. Forestry Research has been mainly
concentrated in MGAP's Forestry Directorate (DF), in cooperation with the Faculty of Rural
Science. Although Extension and technical assistance services are mainly provided by the
private sector, a number of public agencies operate extension programs aimed mostly at
small- scale operations, including Plan Agropecuario, JUNAGRA, Plan Citricola, Animal
Health, Plant Health, and the Directorate for Promotion and Development (all within
MGAP), the Bank of the Republic (BROU), the Colonization Institute, and local
municipalities.
1.34 Regional irrgatlon Boards. These boards were created by Decree' to promote
better coordination between farmers and the Water Authority and resolve any conflicts that
might arise in relation to water distibution for irrigation purposes. Water use rights
applications first must be presented to these Regional Irrigation Boards and then suDmitted to
the Government Water Authority (DNH within MTOP). Regional Irrigation Boards
traditionally included an official from the Ministry of Transport and Public Works (who
presides), two farmers' representatives, and two representatives of the land-owners.
Recently, the Ministry of Livestock, Agriculture and Fisheries also has a representative in
each of the Regional Irrigation Boards. There are five official Regional Irrigation Boards
located in the main rice production regions: Artigas, Tacuarembo, Treinta y Tres, Cerro
Largo, and Rocla. The Regional Irrigation Board of the southern region was established in
1992.
1.35 The main objective of the Regional Irrigation Boards is the allocation of farmers'
rights to use river water for irrigation. Rivers are usually not regulated, therefore, during
summer, competition for water use increases significantly. Under these circumstances,
although these Regional Ifrigation Boards have a wide range of responsibilities, such as
identification of projects and promotion of technology for increasing water use efficiency,
their major activity is the distribution of water permits during dry periods.
1.36 Private Sector Institutions. The role of the private sector in the delivery of
production support services is primarily related to extension and technical assistance,
1.    Decree 442/970 of the Mimstry of Transort and Public Works, daed 1970.



- 10-
although certain specific research programs are also carried out by private organizations.
These include barley, sugarcane, and rice research progams either conducted or contracted
with INIA. Private extension services, accounting for over 60% of total assistance received
by farmers, is either provided by individual consultants or by specialized institutions. With
the exception of Cooperatives and FUCREA (the umbrella organization for some 75
extension groups) these institutions generally provide commodity-specific assistance. The
most important ones are the National Cooperative of Dairy Producers (CONAPROLE), the
Rice Mills Shareholding Company (SAMAN), the Wool Secretariat (SUL), the National
Wime Institute (INAVI), and the recently established Zero Tillage Association (AUSID).
1.37  Water Users Associons. Irrigation in Uruguay has been developed mostly by
individual farmers. Collective irrigation systems are usually found only in the sugarcane
area and in the eastern rice region; these include a small number of farmers and in general
do not have any kind of organization. The exception is the Colonia Espafia Irrigation
System, which has an Irrigation Board to operate and maintain the irrigation facilities,
including pumping stations and canal networks. This irrigation system is state-owned but is
completely managed by private farmers.
E. Bankwide Enerience in Agricultural Sector Lending
1.38 Natural Resources Management. It was frequently assumed in the 1960s, that large
stocks of under-used land, water and forest resources existed and should be brought into
production quickly, to accelerate economic development. The issue of sustainability came to
the fore in the 1970s as borrowing countries increasingly queried such patterns of resource
use. However, this has had little impact on the selection and/or design of agricultural
projects. Economic growth, and economic efficiency with equity, continued to be tne main
tuhst of project design, sometimes at the expense of an explicit concern about conservation
of the natural resource base, and the multi-sectoral, long-range, strategic and operational
context.
1.39 An OED study of some 335 completed projects concluded that natural resource advice
should be a central element in the design of country strategies and that the Bank should help
countries incorporate resource management concerns into macro-economic planning. This
implies considerable time and effort in dialogue, consensus building, and database
development as a pre-requisite for systematic resource management actions which often
traverse social, institutional and political sensitivities. The proposed project recognizes the
problem of soil degradation and conservation, focussing on its potential impact on: loss of
productivity of agricultural land ("on-site" effects), and damage to reservoirs and waterways
off the farm ("off-site' effects).
1.40 IrrIgation Development. A vital component of agricultural diversification is
technological improvement of which irrigation systems are a key example. From 1974 to
1986, the Bank lent almost US$40 billion for agricultural projects and adjustment actions.
Around one-third, or US$1.0 billion per year, was invested in irrigation. After 1986,
lending for irrigation declined sharply, to an average 16% of agricultural lending. However,
in FY91, irrigation and drainage lending had risen again to about 29% and appear to be
returning to traditional levels.



- 11-
1.41   OED impact evaluations of completed irrigation projects worldwide show that
although many had beneficial effects, especially on food producton and improved incomes,
their operational performance fell short of expectations particularly at full development, and
their sustainability in many cases is doubtfl due to poor quality construction and inadequate
maintenance. The physical i tucture has often deteriorated leading to long-term declines
in production and low water use efficiencies. nadequate O&M funding and institutional and
organizatonal problems have contributed to poor performance.
1.42 Borrowers often disengaged too early from projects, leaving irrigation and drainage
systems incomplete and land development unfinished, while complementary services have
been inadequate and/or seriously delayed. In addition, some projects suffered from under-
estmated costs because engineng designs were only partdally completed at appraisal; cost
overruns due to delays in completing these designs, difficulties in preparing tender documents
and evaluating bids; and slower than estimated civil works construction and delivery of
equipment. The proposed Natural Resource Management and Irrigation Development Project
stresses the need for project planners (i.e., government and water users) to carefully assess
water resources within a comprehensive subsector framework before the design and
implementation stages.
1.43 Finally, cost recovery from irrigation projects has not been satisfactory due to lack of
Government commitment, unreliable water supply, and poor O&M. This situation has often
been worsened by the heavy burden of direct and indirect taxes already imposed by
governments on the farming sector. The main lessons on cost recovery indicate the need for
continuous dialogue with Government through sector work and lending which may be a
better instrument than project covenants to ensure that cost recovery objectives harmonize
with socio-economic conditions and national policy on cost recovery. Second, profitable
farming should be a precondition for cost recovery; and unless water supply is reliable, it is
unlikely that improved cost recovery can be achieved and sustained. The proposed project
works towards financial and technical sustinability by transferring many responsibilities
from public irrigation authorities to irrigation water users.
1.44 The Bank's response to the lessons arising from its experience has been to emphasize
institution-strengthening, the lower-cost option of project rehabilitation, adaptive research and
technology transfer, consensus building on cost recovery policy among interested parties, and
improved agronomic performance. Interventions are fiequently national or regional in scope.
An increasing number of loans concentrate on organizational, policy, and financial reforms to
improve institutions and reduce the burdens on government.
1.45 Along these lines, major project activities include more detailed guidelines, training
and information (at all levels), and the creation and strengthening of water user bodies.
Important resource managt components in association with irrigation actions include
controlling erosion, siltatioat, waterlogging, and salinity. Because technological
improvements in irrigation systems have expanded production opportunities and facilitated
agricultral diversification, technological development and innovation were emphasized in
project design.



- 12 -
Preous Bank LAwding in Urugma
1.46 The current lending portfolio in Uruguay includes 13 projects and US$345.0 million
in commitments as of July 1, 1992 with infrastrucue projects predominating. Bank lending
to the agricultural sector has been almost continuous since 1960, including seven loans for
livestock improvement through supervised credit for pasture improvement and livestock
production, the last of which closed in 1981.
1.47 The Second Agriculture Development Project (Ln.. 3131-UR) was the most recent
agriculture sector loan becoming effective in 1990. Its primary objective is to increase
agricultural output, especially that of export products, by providing long-term financing for
farm development and agro-industry, and institutional support in key areas. In addition, the
project promotes increased efficiency in agncultural lending and the provision of techimcal
assistance. Financial resources under the credit component of the loan are overcommitted
and approximately 90% disbursed. Institutional support financed under the project is slighdy
behind appraial forecasts mainly due to minor adjustents made durng implementation.
1.48 Lessons form Bank past experience in the agricultural sector indicates inter aia that:
(a) agricultural technology generation had been reasonably successful, but its transfer fell
short of expectation; (b) pasture management, natural resource conservation and institutional
obstacles merited greater attention: (c) existing technology generation and transfer insdtution
(the Agriculture and Livestock Commission) was not capable of solving increasingly complex
technological problems; (d) subloan indexing was imperfect and had not fulfilled its
objectives; and (e) the choice of a credit subsidy, rather than a fertilizer subsidy, had placed
emphasis on pasture establishment at the expense of maintenance. Many of the gains made
earlier in technology generation (i.e., introduction of improved pasture technology) were lost
due to overgazing and inadequate ferdlizer use. Slow progress ard inadequate coverage
were believed to be precipitated by unstable sectoral policies and adverse international
commodity prices.
1.49 Based on this experience, special attention was given during project design to: (a) the
need for oversight of the content, suitability and momentum of technical packages lest they
acally contribute to natural resource degradation; (b) core implementing institutions which
need sustained Govermnent support while suffering the impact of part-time workers; and (c)
(by implication) poverty alleviation and agricultural diversification. Likewise, the proposed
project recognizes the need for technical packages envisaged for widescale application, to be
tested and proven at the farm level. Consistent with this premise, the proposed project
would support the adoption of improved technology (including natural resource management
practices) through on-farm research and technology validation, integrating research and
extension teams with producers who will play an important role in testing and selecting
appropriate technologies adjusted to their needs.
1.50 In general, Bank projects across diverse sectors in Uruguay (eight projects have
PPARs) have revealed that low Government salaries, institutional deficiencies in state
enrprise, a scarcity of well-tained entrepreneurial staff and their frequently only part-time
availability due to having to work at several jobs, all affect project initation and execution.
The recent Country Implementation Review (CIR) noted the beneficial effects of full-time
special units for project execution, with managers for each component. Further, very slow
implementation of infrsruc   projects due to complicated bidding and award processes



- 13 -
indicates that bidding procedures for major works should be agreed upon before loan
approval.
1.51 In light of this experience, during project preparation emphasis was given to the need
for improved coordination between Govermnent agencies, clearly defining project objectives,
and eliminating overlapping institutional responsibilities. Overall project management will be
the responsibility of a small Project Executing Unit (JEP) made up of specialized local
consultants. Technical assistance will be proviaed through UEP to improve implementation
and evaluation capacity of the line participating sector agencies (para. 2.67). In addition, the
Government of Uruguay has made a special effort to improve its project implementation
capacity. Specifically, current legislation (Art. 42, Law 15,903) stipulates that procurement
procedures established in international contractual agreenents will prevail over local
procurement methods.
1.52 Finally, an important issue for future resource conservation actions in Uruguay is the
weakness of the institutional, policy and legal framework for addressing environmental
issues. Multiple agencies, often contradictory regulations, lack of a strategic perspective or
national policy and poor enforcement could complicate future project planning. To mitigate
this limitation, durng project preparation the Government has taken administrative action to
regulate and consolidate existing legislation (paras. 1.28 and 2.33), thus providing the basis
for enviromnentally sound irrigation subsector growth. The existence of many environmental
NGOs, some large enough to execute substantal programs, and a moderate research and
aning capability in academia, may prove integral to the success of ftumre Bank efforts.
kLeding Stategy in the  i lral Sector
1.53 There has been only limited awareness of resource management issues in the
agricultural sector in Uruguay until recendy. However, the trend towards a diversified
agriculture and more intensified use of cropland has stimulated a greater appreciation of soil
degradation and erosion problems resulting from inappropriate cultivation practices,
especially in rainfed areas. As some of these areas are potentially irrigable, the resource
management issue will arise in any future irrigation development program. Therefore, future
agricultural development efforts will have to include appropriate technical packages to
address erosion along with incentives for farmers to adopt soil conservation measures in both
rainfed and irrigated agriculture. The proposed project would combine actions to address
degradation while expanding the irrigated area. By linking the protection of key water
catchment areas with the downstream development of sustainable rainfed and irrigated
agriculture, the project would test technical packages and gain experience that could form the
basis for a nationwide land management program.
1.54 To reduce the risk of negative effects from irrigation investment, a precondition for
their financing would be the completion of a feasibility study, combining appropriate naturl
resources management techniques and environmental impact assessment to demonstrate
technical, economic and environmental viability. Further, as this is intended to become
standard procedure for the approval of all irrigation investment in the futre, the Ministry of
Agriculture would be strengthened to enable it to supervise environmental impact studies of
proposed sub-projects and monitor their enviromnental effects during execution (para. 2.17).



- 14-
1.55 Although the private sector would remain the driving force behind any irrigation
development program, Govermnent actions to promote expanded private investment in
irrigation would be essential to achieve a rapid and significant response from producers, and
to ensure the participation of small and medium-sized farmers. While irrigation investment
is expected to continue largely in rice-growing areas, the public sector financing envisaged
would attract small and medium-sized farmers willing to grow other export-oriented irrigated
crops, but for whom organization is difficult and the initial risks too great. Moreover, as a
result of the Government policy reforms, sugarcane fatmers who operate state-owned
irrigation schemes would require technical and financial assistance to diversify their
production by rehabilitating their irrigation infrastructure. The project would support these
diversification efforts.
1.56 The proposed project would also support the Govermnent's expressed wish to
rehabilitate irrigation works currently under state control and transfer their use, operation,
and maintenance to the private sector. As a means to ensure that investments do not actually
subsidize and promote inadequate O&M practices by private users, agreements would be
reached with beneficiaries specifying standards for O&M and remMdies for non-compliance
(paras. 2.24, 2.28, and 2.29). Government would be required to set overall parameters for
cost recovery and implement a credible system for collection (i.e., through decentralized
agencies such as the Bank of the Republic of Uruguay).
1.57 As an essential complement to investments in irrigation infrastructure, the project
would also include specific components to support the sustainable development of irrigated
agriculture. This will include the generation and transfer of technology by developing a
comprehensive applied research program and strengthening extension services through
training and technical assistance (para. 2.12). In addition. the project is envisaged to include
technical assistance support to farmers in post-harvest and marketing aspects of-mainly
irrigated-nontraditional products (para. 2.38). Government involvement at this stage would
help achieve more effective management of water resources through a balanced set of
policies and institutional reforms that will both harness the efficiency of market forces and
strngthen public sector agencies' capacity to efficiently carry out its essential regulatory
role.
H. THE
Orin and Rationale for Bank Involvement
2.1   Qgin.  The proposed project has its origins in Uruguay's Irrigation Subsector
Report prepared by the FAO/World Bank Cooperative Program (FAO/CP) in December
1990 and the Resource Management and Irrigation Development Program Report of the
Mistry of Agriculture (MGAP) in May 1992. The scope of the project was defined during
a preparation mission carried out in September 1992, after the sector authorities confirmed
the priority they assigned to improved water and soil management and conservation practices,
and enaced irrigation potetal in the country. During a pre-appraisal mission in May
1993, the Government's proposal to modernize the irrigation subsector and strengthen natural
resource management/conservation was reviewed. The project was appraised in June 1993.



- 15 -
Negotiations with a Uruguayan delegation headed by the Minister of Agriculture, took place
in Washington from October 12 to 21, 1993.
2.2   Ratbnal. The proposed project would be the Bank's first in natural resource
management and irrigation development in Uruguay. It conforms to the Bank's lending
strategy for Uruguay, especially in its support for infrastructure and services designed to
enhance productive potential and export growth. Further, it adheres to the recommendations
of the Bank's 1988 Agricultural Sector Review, wbich called for a greater public role in
promoting agricultural diversification and exports. The project would enable the Government
to act as both catalyst and overseer in irrigation expansion within the confines of a rational
resource management program.
2.3         While the primary objective of the Government's agricultural strategy is
increased productivity, output, and exports, the long-term goal is diversification. The
challenge is to intensify cropland use, while developing and encouraging farmers to adopt
appropriate, sustainable farming systems that would raise farm income without compromising
the preservation of the country's soil and water resources. The existence in Uruguay of
water resources that are not adequately harnessed means that there needs to be a prudent
strategy for irrigation, which could provide greater flexibiity and control than a purely
rainfed system. Such a strategy is therefore a key tool for sectoral diversification and export
expansion.
2.4         Bank involvement would: contribute to a more orderly development of, and
transition to private sector management of irrigation; stngthen private sector productive
capabilities through sustainable soil and water resource practices; support improved
environmental impact assessment in the sector; and improve public resource allocation.
Finally, Bank financing would help country dialogue and assist the Government to implement
a sound and consistent cost recovery policy. Overall, the proposed project would provide a
vehicle to consolidate public investment prograing and management, which in the past has
failed to take account of the interdependencies among agencies, jurisdictions, and sectors.
Prolj    Objectives
2.5   The main objective of the proposed project would be to develop and implement a soil
and water management strategy focussed on irrigation development in order to increase,
diversify, and sustain agricultural output and exports. This long-term objective would be
achieved by: (a) increasing the level of sector investment in rehabilitation and development of
new irrigation/drainage schemes and related service infrastucture following sound technical
and environmental pracdces; (b) strengthening the technical foundation and regulatory
framework to improve surface and ground water use efficiency; (c) establishing a well-
balanced operation and maintenance (O&M) and capital cost recovery policy; (d) supportng
agricutural diversification, and setting the stage for improved natural resource management
practices by financing priority applied research and technology transfer subprojects as well as
soil and water management/conservadon "pilot" subprojects, thereby assisting the
government in developing an overall sector strategy in Uruguay; and (e) providing technical
assistance for forestry development and non-traditional agricultral export activities.



- 16-
aProject Descrtmion
2.6    The two components would be: Natural Resource Management and Irrigation
Development.
(a)   The Natural Resource Management component (US$20.7 million) would: (i)
support the development of "pilot" micro-catchment areas in the Santa Lucia
River Basin to gain experience in the management of ecologically fragile
zones; (ii) establish soil and water management demonstration farms in
selected agro-ecological zones where potential soil degradation problems have
been identified, (iii) develop a Geographical Information System (GIS) to
improve land use classification and management in the country; (iv) support
project related applied research and technology transfer activities; (v) prepare
natural resource management/conservation studies; (vi) strengthen sector policy
planning, evaluation, and monitoring capacity at the level of the Directorates
of Naural Resources, and Hydrography, respectively; and (vii) provide
technical assistance for forestry development.
(b)   The Irrigation Development component (US$50.8 million) would: (i) support
the rehabilitation and modernization of existing public irrigation and drainage
(I&D) schemes where use, maintenance and administration would be
transferred to the users under appropriate capital cost recovery and O&M
arrangements; (ii) provide for the construction of new medium-size collective
irrigation schemes (civil works and communal pumping equipment) for the
benefit of user groups; (iii) support the construction of selected small farmer
irrigation infrastructure (i.e., small-scale earthfills, irrigation and drainage
canals and tubewells) at the farm level; (iv) introduce a water harvesting
"pilot" program at the farm level to help develop an optimal surface water use
strategy; and (v) provide technical assistance for irrigation and drainage
feasibility studies, and production, processing, and marketing of (mainly
irrigated) nontraditional export crops. Implementation of these two
components would be supported by technical assistance provided by the project
through the Project Executing Unit (UEP) (US$2.5 million).
Natural Resources Management (US$20.7 million, 28% of total cost)
2.7 There is already a substantial volume of data on appropriate natural resources
managemen practices available in the country. Nevertheless, most of it has not been fully
tested on a commercial scale. The proposed project would provide the required technical
assistance to process and analyze the available information, with the view to develop
technical packages suitable for the country's various agro-ecological zones. The main
objectives of the actions to be executed under this component are to encourage the adoption
of adequate natural resources management and promote sustinable agricultural production.
To achieve its objective the component would provide both the Government and producers
with essential information through the development of pilot programs in soil and water
management/conservation, demonstration fanns, applied research, the establishment of a
Geographical Information System, forestry development, institutional strengthening and
studies. This component would represent the first phase of a natural resources initiative.



- 17 -
2.8   "Pilot" Micro-Catchmnt Progr_M (US$3.1 mllnion). The project would provide
financial resources in the form of a grant to encourage farmers to undertake on-firm
investments and agricultural production practices designed to reduce soil degradation and
erosion. About 350 small farmers are expected to benefit from these micro-catchment
development programs within the Santa Lucia River Basin. Four micro-catchment areas
within the Santa Lucia River Basin, were selected during project preparation for the
implementation of pilot natural resources management development schemes. These are: (i)
Cafiada los Alamos, 1,188 ha, and about 60 producers; (ii) La Feliciana, 1,518 ha, and 11
producers; (iii) Canel6n Grande I covering 9,028 ha, and approximately 220 producers; and
(iv) Tala, 6,257 ha, and 58 producers.
2.9 Areas have been selected to be representative of the various farming and socio-
economic conditions prevailing in the region and thus have a potential demonstration effect.
The format and content of a draft grant agreement to be signed between the Govermnent and
beneficiaries of the "pilot" program was discussed and agreed during appraisal. Specific
technolr-jical packages would be recommended for the various groups of producers, aimed at
increasing their income while making current management practices more conservation-
effective. A detailed description of these actions is given in Annex B.
2.10   The strategy to be adopted would involve the organization of farmers in the selected
micro-catchment areas within the Santa Lucia Basin, who would addition.lly receive training
and technical assistance (US$2.0 million) for the promotion of appropriate technological
packages designed to conserve natural resources and sustain agricultural production. These
packages would include the establishment of adequate erosion control measures, conservation
tillage methods, improved land use planning, soil and water management, and diversification
of production systems. The project would also finance civil works and equipment
(US$300,000) to carry out the pilot program. On-farm soil conservation and I&D works will
be packaged and procured through service contracts. On-farm irrigation, tillage, and other
equipment (with the exception of small water treatment supplies) at the farm level, as well as
recurrent costs (US$800,000) will be financed by the Govermnent. Draft contracual
arrangements between UEP and CHDCSL for the execution of the pilot micro-catchment
program were agreed during appraisal and submitted to the Bank for final review, prior to
negotiations. Also during negotiations agreement was reached that, ar a condition of
disbursement, the Government and beneficiaries would enter into subgrant agreements
satisfactory to the Bank (para. 3.3).
2.11  Demonstraion Farm in Selected AgroUEcological Zones (US$1.1 million).
Complementing the actions to be promoted within the Santa Lucia micro-catchment pilot
program and the small farmer I&D schemes (para. 2.8 - 2.10), the project would also
support the establishnent of about five soil conservaton/management demonstration farms
(of approximately 900 ha to 1,000 ha each) in each agro-ecological region charactrized by
existng or potential soil degradation problems. Based on the experience gained through the
demonstration farms financed under Loan 3131-UR, the units selected for demonstration
purposes would receive technical assistance and training (US$600,000), and soil conservation
tillage equipment (US$320,000), the latter from DGRNR, to incorporate improved soil, crop
and livestock management technologies aimed at developing sustaminable rotations and
production systems on a commercial scale. Through both mass media and tareted extension
mechanisms, the results obtained are expected to be disseminated to the entire farming
community of the region. Recurrent costs (US$200,000) for the implementation of the



- 18 -
demonstration farms would be financed by the Government. Draft contractual arrangements
for the establishment and operation of the demonstration farms were agreed during appraisal
and submitted to the Bank for final review prior to negotiations. Eligibility criteria for the
selection of the soil management/conservation demonstration farms would be included in a
Project Manual to be attached to the Loan Agreement (par. 2.69 and 3.2 (f)). As a
condition of disbursement against the soil and water management/conservation
Demonstration Farms, the Govenmment (through UEP) will enter into a service agreement
with the corresponding farmer under terms and conditions satisfactory to the Bank (para.
3.4). The scope and operation arrangements for the demonstration farms are described in
Annex C.
2.12 Applied Research and Technology Transfer (US$10.0 million). The project would
finance high priority research proposals drawing from national and international experience
to adapt at the farm level existing technologies, focussed on natural resource management
and conservation, and irrigated agriculture (Annex D). The Government, with the assistance
of INIA would carry out the applied research program. The project would support an
ongoing demand-driven approach to technology generation and transfer which begins with
applied research and ends with the adoption of a given validated technology adapted to the
farmers' needs. The applied research and technology transfer program would also provide
the technical bases for encouraging appropriate land use and soil and water conservation
practices. INIA would be responsible for the implementation of about 70% of the proposed
applied research program. For this purpose, the Government would enter into a subsidiary
grant agreement with INLA to transfer the necessary funds for the execution of its part of the
program (US$4.2 million). The remaining 30% of the applied research subprojects (US$2.0
miflion), preselected during project preparation, would be contracted out to specialized public
and private institutions (i.e., Faculty of Rural Science, DSA, and AUSID). The project
would finance 50% of the total cost of these research contracts. Recurrent and incremental
operaing costs for the Applied Research and Technology Transfer Program (US$3.8
million), would be financed by the Government. Signing of the subsidiary grant agreement
eween the Government and INIA for the implementation of part of the Applied Research
and Technology Transfer Program would be a condition of Loan Effectiveness (para. 3.2
(a)). Research contracts would also be signed between the Government and the Faculty of
Rual Science and AUSDI, in terms satisfactory to the Bank.
2.13 The program is expected to provide technological recommendations to specific
requirements of farmers regarding the efficient utilization of water resources and
management of irrigated crops. Further to the research projects already identified and
budgeted, for which detailed terms of reference have been prepared for initiation during Year
1 of the project, additional funds have been included to undertake specific research projects
to address particular technological problems encountered dunng the implementation of the
ifrigation schemes and the natural resource management initiatives (micro-catchment
development and demonstradon farms). Technical assistance (US$400,000) will be provided
to support the establistment of a small soil conservation unit within INIA, with a view
towards introducing a ntw program oriented approach to technology generation. As a
condition of Loan Effectiveness, the referred soil conservation unit would be established no
later than June 30, 1994 (para. 3.2 (a)).
2.14 A total of 16 projects have been initially proposed by the different institutions, that
are primarily oriented toward the adoption of resource (mainly soil) management and



- 19-
conservation techniques in the various agro-ecological conditions of the country. Regarding
crop and irrigation teehnology, a preliminary program comprising an additional 24 individual
projects has been prepared. The final inventory of research subprojects was reviewed and
agreed during negotiations (para. 3.1 (a)). TORs and individual, pre-selected research
proposals will have prior review by the Bank.
2.15  Technology transfer in cropping techniques under imgated conditions would be
mainly provided by the existing private institutions and cooperatives. The applied research
and technology transfer program would nevertheless assist in improving the quality of
extension services to farmers by supporting the proposed technical assistance and training
program to INIA as well as technology transfer activities related to the applied research,
micro-catchment development (para. 2.8), and demonstration farm (para. 2.11) activities.
The project would also provide sinilar support to the beneficiaries of all irnigadon schemes
to be developed (para. 2.26) or rehabilitated (para. 2.22). In all cases, technical assistance
would focus on resource management, crop diversification, and irrigation technology issues.
2.16 Geographical Infomation System (USS1.0 miliion}. The Project would finance the
development of a computerized data base of the geographical distribution of renewable
natural resources and their characteristics that would provide the basis for agro-ecological
zoning and recommendations for appropriate land use in Uruguay (Annex E). The Program
would rationalize the existing data collection centers and provide an integratd geographical
information system, including updated hardware and software (US$480,000), to be used as a
naural resource management tool by all interested institutions and private investors, in
addition to the required trainig and technical assistance (US$420,000). Reurrent costs for
the implementation of the GIS (US$100,000) would be financed by the Government. During
negotiations, agreement was reached on the action plan for the implementation of the GIS
(para. 3.1 (b)).
2.17  Institutional Strengthenine (US$ 1.7 million}. The main responsibility for project
implementation would be within MGAP and MTOP (through its Directorate of Hydrography)
which in turn will play an important role in the allocation of water rights and the
construction of main irrigation works. An analysis of the capacity of the various institons
was carried out as part of project preparation to determine the ability to implement the
various activities. As a result, some deficiencies were identified in instittional capacity,
particularly in the General Directorate of Renewable Natural Resources within MGAP, for
which technical assistance and training programs have been defined (Annex P).
2.18 To improve coordination between the main ministries involved in project execution,
an Inter-Ministerial Council (CCI), headed by the Minister of Agriculture, was created and
supported by a strong Project Executing Unit (UEP) (para. 2.65). The project would
strengthen implementation and project monitoring capacity of the Forestry Directoate (D)F)
and the Directorate of Natual Resources within MGAP. Further, the project would also
provide technical assistance and training on soil and water management/conservation  the
water users' associations that would be organized or benefit under the project. With these
objectives in mind, the project would provide about 150 person/months of technical
assistance and training (US$1.4 million)-including 40 person/months of local consultancy
(or US$140,000)-to improve planning and monitoring capacity at the level of the Minister
of Agriculture, as well as the neary communication equipment and vehicles
(US$200,000) to disseminate the results of the various pilot and research subprojects to be

financed under the project. Recurrent costs (US$100,000) would be financed by the
Government.
2.19 Natural Resource Management Studies (US$1.6 millionl. As part of its efforts to
improve management and conservation of the country's natural resource base, the project
would finance special studies in the following areas: (i) assessment of the resource
availability and exploitation potential of the aquifers of Raig6n and Salto; final TORs for
these groundwater studies were agreed during negotiations (para 3.1 (c)); (ii) evaluation of
the quality of water for irrigation; (iii) water management plans at the level of the various
nicro-catchment areas where private producers intend to finance small earthfills; (iv)
evaluation of water use efficiency under different irrigation practices; (v)
environmental/economic assessment of the water harvesting pilot program (para. 2.33); final
TORs for this study were also agreed during negotiations (para. 3.1 (c)); and (vi)
preparation of a possible follow-up agricultural sector/natural resources management project.
2.20 Forestry Development (US$ 2.2 million). The project would provide technical
assistance and training (US$1.6 million) in forestry development for the Directorate of
Forestry (DF) to continue the activities that are currently being developed under
Loan 3131-UR, and thus contribute to mitigating potential soil degradation problems througb
better utilization of forest resources. The main areas of interest under the proposed project
would be those related to the introduction of new species and to the management of existing
plantations, including: (i) forest inventory, fire control, pest control and logging; (ii) wood
drying and processing; and (iii) management of agro-silvopastoral systems. The project
would also finance the necessary field and laboratory equipment (US$220,000) to support the
proposed forestry development activities. Recurrent costs, including incremental operating
costs (US$380,000) would be financed by the Government. The action plan for forestry
development activities, is presented in Annex F.
kiation Development (US$ 50.8 million, 69% of total cost)
2.21 Under this component, the project would finance: (a) rehabilitation and modernization
of existing State-owned I&D schemes; (b) construction of new medium- and small-size
irrigation schemes and on-farm works (i.e., small-scale earthfills, irrigation and drainage
canals and tubewells); (c) development of water harvesting "pilot" schemes at the farm level;
and (d) technical assistance to assist producers to identify new market prospects for-mainly
irrigated-nontraditional export crops. The project would also finance the required feasibility
and engineering studies, design and construction supervision, and environmental impact
assessments. The level of investment in the three categories of irrigation projects to be

the project, indicative investment figures are given below for each projected activity.

transfer, however, these subprojects would have to be rehabilitated to improve the efficiency

2.23 Rehabilitation works to be financed under this component would include: (i) deferred
maintenance and repairs of main structures, such as headworks or pumping station facilities
and main water conveyance structures; (ii) improvement of water distribution and drainage
structures in the secondary and tertiary networks at the communal level; and (iii) farm outlets
as well as any additional-up to the farm gate-investment required to adapt the existing
irrigation works to the requirements of a diversified agricultural production.
2.24 Under Uruguayan law, the use, operation and maintenance of public irrigation
schemes could be transferred to the users through a concession. Prior to the beginning of
rehabilitation works, and as a condition of disbursement against this project activity,
agreements for the transfer of water use, administration and O&M satisfactory to the Bank,
would be signed between the Government and the project beneficiaries (para. 3.5). During
its first two years of execudon, the project would finance the rehabilitation of I&D
infrastruture in Canel6n Grande I (1,100 ha) and Colonia Espafia (750 ha). In the
subsequent years and subject to the outcome of the respective feasibility studies, the proposed
project would finance the rehabilitation of the other state-owned irrigation projects (para.
2.22). Rehabilitation of the Laguna Negra and India Muerta state-owned I&D schemes, have
not been considered in the project because they are located within an environmentally fragile
area (Rocha Wetlands).
2.25 As a condition of disbursement against the construction of I&D schemes financed
under the project (paras. 2.26 to 2.29), the Government would issue and publish in the
official gazette, two regulatory decrees of the Water Code guaanteeing: (i) water availability
(during construction and thereafter) to beneficiaries of I&D schemes included in the project;
and (ii) that all I&D works would be carried out according to project objectives and priorities
as established in the Loan Agreement (para. 3.6). Drafts of both regulatory decrees were
agreed during negotiations and attached as Annexes 2 and 3 to the Minutes of Negotiations
(para. 3.1 (e)).

consideration by an Intenninisterial Coordinating Council (para. 2.65) (CCI). The

million). Land acquisition (US$2.5 million), on-farm equipment, and implements would be

2.28 Prior to the initiation of the civil works, and as a condition of disbursement against
each new medium-size I&D scheme, a capital cost recovery and O&M agreement satisfactory
