prototype contract agreement was dicussed and agreed during negotiations (para. 3.1 (f)).
Eligibility criteria for the selection of proposed I&D subprojects, including capital cost
recovery decision matrix are described in a Project Mlanul to be attached to the Loan
Agreement (para. 2.69).
2.29 Small Farmer Irrigation Infrastructure (US$5.8 milion), would entail on-farm
small civil works (US$2.0 million) and communal equipment (US$3.8 million) (i.e., small-
scale eartbfills, irrigation and drainage canals, and small pumping stations). The
construction of tubewells at the farm level or communal pumping stations plus conveyance,
distribution and drainage networks would benefit an area of about 5,000 ha. Satisfactory
evidence that the conclusions/recommendations of the groundwater studies of the Raig6n and
Salto aquifers (para. 2.29) are being implemented, would be a condition of disbursement
(pan. 3.8 (a)). Also, as a condition of disbursement against this project activity, a capital
cost recovery and O&M agreement satisfactory to the Bank, would be signed between the
Govermment and small farmers (para. 3.8 (b)). A prototype contract agrement was
disussed and agreed during negotiations (pan. 3.1 (g)).
2.30  Water Harrest  "Pilot" Proag   (US$ 6.9 million). This pilot program would
include earth movements for water catchments (i.e., earthfills) at the farm level, mainly to
benefit farmers operating in rice growing areas. It intends to develop the groundwork to
improve surface water use efficiency and effectiveness in the country following sound
environmental practices. Along these lines, aU works to be financed under this "pilot"
program would be screened according to natural resource management/conservation technical
criteria at the selected river basin level, which in turn would not exceed approximately 8,000
ha of irrigable land.
2.31 The proposed subprojects within the "pilot" program would include an environmental
impact assessment and a water management plan at the level of the micro-catchment area
where these irrigation schemes would be located (para. 2.33). In addition, regardless of
size, subprojects would be eligible for financing under the project only if they comply with
the agreed selection critena (Annex H) inckded in the Project Manual to be atached to the
Lean Agreement (para. 2.69). All eligible subprojects would have to have a minimum of
12% economic rate of return (ERR).



- 23 -
2.32 Average size of these small projects will vary depending on the private producers'
interest and production potential. Given the relatively small size of these schemes, no full
feasibility studies would be required, but a technical description of the project and some cost-
benefit analysis would be carried out.
2.33 As a condition of disbursment against the Water Harvesting "Pilot" Program, the
Government would complete, in terms satisfactory to the Bank, an environmental/economic
assessment study (paras. 2.19 and 2.31) quantifying the enviromnental externalities associated
with a systematic development of small water catchments (earthfills) (para. 3.9). Final TORs
for the referred study were agreed during negotiations (paras. 2.19 and 3.1 (c)).
2.34 I&D Feasibility Studies (US$1.9 millionD. The project would also finance feasibility
studies to be undertaken during implementation, with resources allocated mainly to potential
new I&D schemes (paras. 2.26, 2.29, and 2.30) on a point system that would inter-alia favor
subprojects that would benefit small and medium-sized farmers and have strong beneficiary
support. Engineering studies and final design would be financed as part of the irrigation
schemes' investment costs.
2.35 All I&D subprojects would be environmentally screened (para. 2.82) and, if required,
would have to have the respective environmental impact assessment carried out and
mitigating measures identified. In addition to the application of the agreed selection criteria,
the selected investment proposals would be ranked by the net benefit-investment ratio derived
from the net benefit stream used for estimatng the internal rate of return. Water Harvesting
I&D sub-projects would be submitted by private producers to the UEP for screening and
forwarding to CCI (para. 2.66 - 2.67), and subject to capital cost recovery agreements
satisfactory to the Bank.
2.36 The project would also finance a study of the current legal/administrative framework
regulating irrigation water use in Uruguay. The objective of this study would be to review
proposed legislation for the irrigation subsector, currently under discussion at the level of the
Senate's Agriculture Committee, and ensure its conformity with the regulatory framework
supported by the project (para. 1.28). The referenced study would provide the stage for the
Government to identify possible adjustments to the proposed legislation which could be
introduced, as necessary, prior to its approval by the General Assembly. The project would
also finance a more comprehensive watershed management study in Uruguay, aimed at
establishing the groundwork for modernizing and rationalizing the Government's overall
water policy.
2.37 To reduce the risk of assigning public funds for the development of economically
marginal I&D schemes and to ensure that the assumptions regarding expected benefits from
proposed investments meet the test of the market, the proposed project would stress the
participation of beneficiaries in the preparation, selection and execution of proposed sub-
projects, as was done with the irrigation schemes proposals that have already been identified
during preparation for execution during the first year of project implementation (para. 2.25).
2.38  Non-Traditional Export Crops Promotion (US$2.2 million). Subsequent to an
assessment of the ongoing Non-Traditional Export Program being implemented by PENTA
under Loan 3131-UR, the project would provide technical assistance and training (US$1.1
million) to carry out marketing surveys and feasibility studies. MAG's PENTA program



- 24 -
would be responsible for the implementation of this subcomponent. The proposed technical
assistance and training activities are intended to help farmers and private potential investors
interested in either adopting improved post-harvest technologies or expanding exports of non-
traditional (mostly irrigated) agricultural products. Administrative and other recurrent costs
(US$1.1 million) would be financed by the Government. The assessment of the PENTA
program would be carried out during the project mid-term review (para 2.73 - 2.74) planned
for the third year of project implementation (September 1996). As a condition of
disbursement against this activity, the Goverunent and the Bank would agree during the
project mid-term review, on a specific action plan based on the assessment of the PENTA
program financed under Loan 3131-UR (para. 3.10).
Project Costs and Financlug
Costs
2.39 The total cost of the project, including contingencies, taxes and duties, is estimated at
US$74.0 million, of which about US$32.9 million (44%) represents foreign exchange costs.
Price contingencies, estimated at US$5.8 million (or 9% of baseline costs), have been
included at a rate of 3.1% per annum between 1993-1999. Physical contingencies, except
for the applied research component and protected I&D works, have been estimated at
approximately USU400,000 based on 5% contingencies on all components. According to the
data available for the subprojects to be executed during the first year of implementation, the
cost of rehabilitation of I&D infastructure would fluctuate between US$1,000 and US$2,000
per hectare; small rice irrigation earthfills would represent an investment of about US$ 1,000
per hectare, while the costs for the development of medium- and small-sized new irrigation
schemes for horticulture or fruit tree production (Jumecal) are estimated at US$3,500 4,500
per hectare, and for grain and forage production (Fraile Muerto and Roman Grande) at
US$700 to US$1,300 per hectare. A project cost summary, and total project costs by
component and disbursement category, are presented in Annex R.
2.40 The proposed US$41 million Bank loan would be made to the Govermnent of
Uruguay at the standard variable interest rate for a term of 15 years, including five years of
grace. The proposed loan for US$41 million equivalent would finance about 62% of total
costs, net of taxes and duties. Bank financing would cover: (a) 60% of eligible civil works;
(b) 80% for eligible equipment for all components; (c) 100% for vehicles and eligible office
technology equipment; (d) 100% of technical assistance, (i.e., feasibility and enviromnental
assessment studies and engineering design), training, and special studies; (e) 50% of research
contracts; and (f) 100% of the costs of administering the UEP during the life of the project.
Eligible expenditures for the early phases of project implementation from July 1, 1993 to the
date of loan signing would be considered for retroactive financing up to a maximum of
US$3.0 million to maintain the momentum gained during preparation.
2.41 All land acquisition (US$2.8 million), recrrent and incremental expenditures (US$
5.8 million) (for project-related staff and activities) as well as duties and taxes (US$7.8
million) would be financed solely by the Govermment of Uruguay. During negotiations the
Government confirmed that adequate and timely counterpart flmding will be made available
to MGAP, MTOP, INIA, and UEP for the implementation of the project (para. 3.1 (h)).



- 25 -
Project beneficiaries would finance the costs of on-farm investnents including inputs, and
irrigation and tillage equipment (estimated at US$8.5 nw.-.ion equivalent).
Plwcrement
2.42  During negotiations it was agreed that the proclement of works, goods and
services financed under the project would be undertaken ih accordance with Bank guidelines
and using Bank standard bidding documents and contract fonn for ICB and consulting
services (para. 3.2 (i)). Similarly, LCB procedures acceptable to the Bank were discussed
and agreed during negotiations (para. 3.2 (i)). A favorable opinion on the format of
standard Bank bidding documents, furnished to the Government prior to negotiations, was
submitted to the Bank by the competent Uruguayan administative court (Tribunal de
Cuentas) during negotiations. All procurement of goods, works and consulting services
financed by the Bank would be carried out by the Government and INIA, as the case may be,
with the assistance of UEP (para. 2.68).
2.43 During negotiations, the Govermnent confirmed that in case of conflict between local
legislation and the Bank's procurement policy, the latter would prevail if appropriate
provisions are included in the Loan Agreement (para. 3.1 (j)). Such special provisions
would be included in the procurement schedule to the Loan Agreement.
2.44 The special provisions would be the following:
(a)   neither foreign firms nor consultants would be obliged to enter into joint
ventures with local firms for carrying out civil works, supply, selection of
equipment, or consulting services;
(b)   contracts will be awarded to the "lowest evaluated biddert;
(c)   foreign bidders would be allowed to participate in Local Competitive Bidding
(LCB) for goods and works;
(d)   no prior registration of bidders would be required for ICB, and if so required
by local legislation, the information to be requested will be of such nature that
will not discourage foreign bidder participation;
(e)   price adjustments for equipment would be allowed even if these changes in
prices are lower than 15%;
(f)   bidding process will not be cancelled even in the case that less than three bids
are received, unless otherwise agreed with the Bank;
(g)   bidders would not be required to legalize bid documents with consulates,
except when needed prior to signing the contract; and
(h)   the use of local personnel would not be imposed on foreign contractors, except
for unskilled labor.



- 26 -
2.45  aiLWorks, including irtigation schemes and micro-catchment development, would
generally be small, remote, and dispersed, both geographically and over time, and are
unlikely to be of interest to foreign bidders. Civil works consist mainly of: construcion of
irrigation and drainage networks and their related stuctr, service roads, dikes, pumping
station infrastructures, water harvest  infraructure (i.e., earthfills), and soil conservation
(soil movement) works and infrastructure.
2.46 Contmacts for civil works estimated to cost at least US$250,000 but not more than
US$3.0 million each, and for a total amount of about US$ 10.8 million equivalent, would be
awarded on the basis of Local Competitve Bidding (LCB) procedures acceptable to the
Bank. In all cases LCB procedures would be open to foreign bidders. Civil works contracts
for less than US$250,000 would be procured on the basis of shopping procedures acceptable
to the Bank, including a minimum of three quotadons from qualified firms, subject to an
aggregate limit of US$7.7 million. These would consist of investments in individual
schemes, to be carried out by the private sector, which would generally include water
harvestng infrastruture, soil movements, small pumping stations, on-farm infrasucture and
wells, and would be tendered on the basis of the normal procedures of the beneficiaries. To
m=nimize adverse impact on the environment during construction, specifications in the
bidding documents for the major works would include clauses under which contractors would
be required to prevent, miimize, or mitigate environmental damage.
2.47       . Goods would include pumnping and irrigation equipment, vehicles,
computers (including software) and other office equipment, laboratory equipment, extension
aid equipment, agricultural equipment, spare parts and training materials. Most of the
pumping and irrigation equipment would be procured in the first two years of the project,
and could be grouped into packages of at least US$250,000 each and procured by
iternational competitive bidding (ICB) in accordane with Guidelines for Procrement
under IBRD Loans and IDA Credits (May 1992). Contracts for goods procured under ICB
would total US$10.7 million. Where ICB procedures are used, and for bid evaluation
purposes, a preferential margin of 15% would be given to domestic manufacturers in
accordance with Bank Guidelines. Goods procured under contracts esdmated to cost less
than US$250,000 equivalent each, which in the aggregate would not exceed US$9.3 million,
would be suitable for procurement through LCB under procedures acceptable to the Bank.
The mmaining goods, which cannot be grouped into bid packages of US$50,000 equivalent,
would be procured on the basis of price quotations from at least three qualified suppliers,
provided that the aggregate amount of such procurement does not exceed US$0.7 million
equivalent.
2.48  Consulffng Services. Preparation of studies and designs, institudonal strengtenin,
technical assistance and training activities, amounfing to about US$17.5 million, would be
carried out by nadonal and foreign consultants, contracted in accordance with Guideines on
the Use of Consulants by World Bank Borrowers and by the World Bank as Executing
Agency (Augst 1981). Of this amount, approximately US$8.0 million would be transferred
to INIA for the implementation of its part of the Applied Resch and Technology Trasfer
Program (para. 2.12). FA, DSA and AUSID would be contracted directly by MGAP to
cary out the agreed research subprojects (Annex D) on grounds of their past and current
involvement in soil and water management/conservation activides, and their satisfactory
performance.

2.49 Review of Procurement. Civil works contracts to cost US$0.5 million or more, and
goods contracts under ICB, and LCB estmated to cost US$200,000 or more, as well as the
first contract for works and the first for goods below the aforementioned respective limits,
would be subject to the Bank's prior review of procurement documentation before invitations
to bid were issued and contracts awarded.
2.50 Because of the nature of the Irrigation Schemes to be financed under this loan, it
would be difficult to estimate the precise number and value of contracts that would fall above
the prior review threshold. Of the available data on possible schemes, roughly 60% would
be subject to prior review. Additionally, the review will result in a coverage of about 60%
of the total estimated value of goods contracts. The balance of contracts would be subject to
post-review by the Bank after contract award applying a sampling of at least 5%.
2.51 The Bank would review all documentation for consulting services included in the
project. For consultant contracts with firms costing less than US$100,000, and up to an
aggregate amount of about US$2.6 million, the Bank prior review would cover only the
corresponding terms of reference before contracting and conduct a selective post-review of
support contract documents during supervision.

from an official legal authority in the Uruguayan executive branch stating that the terms of
the Loan Agreement with the Bank (including terms and conditions for the proement of
works, goods, and consultant services (para. 2.42)) are valid and binding in Uruguay
(para. 3.2 (c)).

Cost Recovery in Irrigaion Projects
2.54  Operation and Maitenance. Given the limited level of I&D infras
development in Uruguay, there are few multi-rm irrigation schemes operating with water
users' associations. However, in the case of those in existence, user groups cover the full
cost of O&M and in some cases are directly responsible for these operations (e.g., Colonia
Espaia). In other I&D schemes, O&M is carried out by a private company (e.g., India
Muerta). The project emphasize financial autonomy and water user managemet of the
proposed I&D schemes. The rationale for public sector involvement, and less than full
recovery of capital investment, is mainly based on environmental extrnalities. The main
objective is to assist the Government to design and implement an irrigation subsector strategy
aimed at improving public resource allocation, and discouraging wasteful use of water and
degradation of the natural resource base.
2.55 For investments financed under the project, beneficiaries would be responsible for
O&M and would cover the full cost of these operations through the collecuon of water
charges based on irrigable area. To ensure against providing negative incenives for poor
O&M, standards for operation and maitenance would be defined and would form an integral
part of the agreement to be signed between the water users and the state regarding the
construcion of the respective irrigation and drainage subproject (paras. 2.24, 2.28, and
2.29). For both state-owned and new projects, a trnsition period would be defined during



- 28 -
which water users' associations wou4d be given technical assistance in organization and in
O&M activities (para. 2.67).

2.56   The exclusive reliance on taxation of incremental benefits to achieve cost recovery is
not a reliable system because farmers bear little risk if the investment fails to achieve its
objectives. The project would stress collection-through a decentralized agency, i.e.,
BROU-of O&M and 30% to 40% of direct new investment costs. The percentage of capital
cost recovery would be determined on a case-by-case basis according to the users' real
repayment capacity. A draft of the final decision matrix for determining levels of capital
cost recovery, was discused and agreed during negotiaftons (para 3.1 (k)), and
incorporated to the Project Manual (para. 2.69). An estimate of the fiscal impact of selected
subprojects is presented in Annex L.
2.57   Inv.estmnt  Direct capital cost recovery would be based on the cost of investment,
eimated after final design has been completed. This amount would be divided by the
number of beneficiaries in proportion to the irrigated area allocated to them. Grace and



- 29 -
repayment periods would be negotiated on a project-by-project basis, but would not exceed
four years grace in the case of farners involved in fruit tree production and two years for
other farmers. A maximum of 25 years repayment would apply depending on farmers'
capacity to pay. Repayment would be indexed by the wholesale price index. The rate of
interest would be determined by the Interministerial Coordinating Council (para. 2.65), but it
will not be less than the interest rate on medium-term certificates of deposit (in Pesos)
calculated by the Central Bank of Uruguay. The Coimnission would also establish the level
of payment during the grace period and would define a discount rate on total debt to
encourage pre-payment.
2.58 The level of direct capital cost recovery would be negotiated with the beneficiaries for
each individual investment. It would be between approximately 30% and 40% of the total
cost; except in the case of the Water Harvesting "Pilot" Program (para. 2.30) or other
private on-farm I&D schemes (paras. 2.26-2.28), which may reach up to 70%. If the
irrigation investment were to benefit small farmers in marginal areas (e.g., Santa Lucia
Basin) (para. 2.8), the level of direct cost recovery could be below 30%, subject to Bank
approval.
2.59 Negotiation packages with users, including direct cost recovery targets, for proposed
investments to be financed under the project would have to be reviewed by the Bank and
approved subsequent to negotiations with beneficiaries. If the overall :evel of direct cost
recovery differs more than 15% from proposed targets, the change would have to be justified
and agreed by the Bank before the agreement could be signed with the beneficiaries. Major
divergence would suggest that assumptions used in internal rate of return calculations were
overly-optimistic and that the net incremental benefit-cost ratio was incorrect. Consequently,
the ERR would have to be recalculated and the irrgation schemes ranked again.
2.60 For the market to ration demand effectively, beneficiaries must believe that
government will recover its investment costs. In the case of Uruguay, the risk of non-
compliance on direct capital cost recovery would be minimized by having a decentralized
agency (i.e., Banco Republica Oriental del Uruguay) collect the debt of water users.
2.61 Efforts would also be made to recover some of the investment costs indirectly through
taxes on incremenal gross agricultural output (the value-added tax rate is currently 22%) and
through the property tax set at 1.3% to 2.7% of assessed value, depending on the range
within which the property falls. In addition to the property tax, there are municipal rates,
that are about one half of the property tax. If no tax exceptions applied, up to approximately
42% of investment costs, on average, could be recovered indirectly through taxation (see
Annex L). In the case of large- and medium-sized farmers, the combination of direct and
indirect cost recovery could increase the overall capital cost recovery figure to between 70
and 80% (30% to 40% direct, plus approximately 42% indirect). For beneficiaries of the
Water Harvesting "Pilot" Program, direct plus indirect cost recovery could reach 100%.
2.62 As indicated in para. 2.54 above, less than full direct capital cost recovery for on-
farm investments to be carried out under the Water Harvesting "Pilot" Program (para. 2.30),
is mainly based on environmental externalities. These externalities would be quantified on an
environmental/economic assessment (paras. 2.19 and 2.33) of the water harvesting pilot area,
thus contributing to integrate land use policies and practices with water management strategy.
The Government of Umguay increasingly recognizes that environmental considerations need



- 30-
to be built into agricultural development projects, particularly to those issues related to soil
and water resource conservation. To accomplish this, a set of clearly defined policy actions
combined with a well balanced public investment program is needed to take advantage of the
efficiency provided by market forces while strengthening the users' commitment to natural
resource conservation.
2.63 Proper management of surface water is critical for the sustainability of agricultural
production systems, water quality, and related eco-systems. These include a combination of
market-i.e., financial-incentives, regulatory policies, and enforcement capability. At this
early stage of irrigation development in Uruguay, when a subsector regulatory framework
needs to be secured and the legal/administrative foundation and incentive framework for
sustainable agricultural conversion established, the Government's active participation
becomes essential. Under the project, the mainly regulatory role of the public sector would
be stressed, while the users' involvement in the design, financing, and administration of the
proposed I&D schemes would provide a cost-effective means of improving the limited
monitoring and enforcement capacity of public agencies.
Disbursements
2.64 Loan proceeds are expected to be disbursed over 6.5 years (Annex Q), consistent with
the standard disbursement profile for Bank-financed agricultural projects in Uruguay. Up to
US$3.0 million in eligible project expenditures would be financed retroactively to July 1,
1993, to maintain momentum gained during preparation and to permit the initiation of works
during the off-season for agriculture. The closing date will be December 31, 1999. Prior to
Loan Effectiveness, the Government would establish a special account in the Central Bank
(BCU) to cover expenditures eligible for Bank financing under the project to be managed by
UEP (para. 2.67). As a condition of Loan Effectiveness, the Government would establish,
operate and maintain in a commercial bank a project account or accounts for purposes of
depositing the Borrower's counterpart funds. Such account or accounts would maintain at all
times a minimum balance of US$400,000 equivalent (para. 3.2 (d)). The Bank would
initially deposit up to US$2.5 million, which is equivalent to an estimated four months of
average disbursements. Disbursements would be made on the basis of Statements of
Expenditure (SOE) for all expenses except those covered by contracts requiring prior review
by the Bank. Disbursements for contracts requiring prior review would be fully documented.
Relevant documentation in suppor. of SOEs would not be submitted to the Bank, but would
be retained by the implementing agencies. Copies of such documents and disbursement
applications would be supplied to the project executing unit (para. 2.65) by the implementing
agencies and would be made available to Bank supervision missions. The special account
would be replenished through normal reimbursement of project expenditures.
Organization and Implementation
2.65 Prolject Mnagement. MGAP would be the implementing agency for the natural
resource management component, and MTOP for the irrigation development component of
the project. INIA would execute part of the Applied Research and Technology Transfer
Program (para. 2.12). Overall project management would be the responsibility of a Project
Executing Unit (UEP) that would provide technical back-up, and execute the CCI decisions.
Since the proposed project would have actions involving various sub-sectors and agricultural
institutions, an Intenninisterial Coordinating Council (CCI), headed by the Minister of



- 31 -
Agriculture and comprised of the Minister of Transport and Public Works (MTOP) and the
Director of OPP, was established by executive decree No. 529 of 6/29/1993. CCI would
define policies and adopt the decisions related to implementation and supervision, including
the execution of the Annual Work Program (POA) and related activities. Agreement was
reached during negotations on the POA for the first year of project implementation
(attached as Annex 2 to the Implementation Letter (paras. 2.74 and 3.1(1)). During
negotations, it was also agreed that draft POAs for each succeeding year would have to be
presented to the Bank for approval by September of each preceding year, and carried out
thereafter (para. 3.1 (1)).
2.66 CCI would review annual operating programs and semi-annual reports and would
make proposals for modifications, as necessary. In addition, CCI would arbitrate those
issues which are either of an inter-institutional nature or are not the responsibility of any
particular executing agency. It would meet a minimum of four times a year.
2.67 CCI would be assisted by UEP (pars. 2.65), headed by an Executive Secretary who
would report directly to CCI and participate in its meetngs, acting as its secretary, and
would liaise directly with the Bank at the operational and technical levels. The head of UEP,
who would be an Agricultural Economist with ample experience in project preparation and
manrgement, would be assisted by a multidisciplinary team comprising an agronomist
specialized in irrigated agriculture, an irrigation (civil) engineer with proven experience in
procurement of goods and services, a specialist in natual resources management, and an
accountant plus support staff. The Unit would require two vehicles for field travel, three
computers, communication and office equipment. Additionally, the UEP would have funds
for hiring an estinatd 29 months of specialist consultants for EAs, technical backstopping,
transfer of technology, applied researh assistance and other implementaion-related matters
including operating and tavel expenses of the consultants. Asuans were obtained
during negotiations, that the operating budget for the UEP would be submitted to the Bank
for review and approval by September of the preedig Umguayan fiscal year (para. 3.1
(m)). The UEP was formally established by MGAP (Minsterial Resolution No. 450/93 of
7/7/93). Prior to negotiations, the head of UEP, and core technical and support staff were
appointed. Assurances were obtained during nego     that the Unit's staff would
continue to be contracted in terms satisfactory to the Bank, and that funding for the UEP
would be made available in the amounts necessary and in time to ensure appropriate project
administration (para. 3.1 (n)).
2.68 The UEP would: (a) implement and coordinate the agreements of the CCI; (b) arrange
for the preparation of irrigation investment proposals imcludg environmental impact
assessment, their review and the preparation of the annual investment programs; (c) prepare
project background information so that CCI can define annual implementation programs; (d)
facilitate the efficient coordination of components among the various implementing agencies;
(e) consolidate the POAs of implementing agencies, monitor program implementation and
provide periodic reports to the CCI; (f) recommend measures to resolve divergences of
project components from the program; (g) monitor the use of program funds; (h) assist the
executing agencies in the preparation of bidding documents for the procurement of goods,
works and consulting services to be financed under the project; and (i) centralize the
information regarding natural resource management that the various sub-component under
that hading will generate, and publish jointly with DGRNR and CHDCSL, periodic reports



- 32 -
with this information. A summary of the project organization and management, is presented
in Annex J.
2.69 As a condition of Loan Effectiveness, the Governnent would cause UEP to sign
cooperative technical agreements with the line agencies (i.e., DGRNR, DNH, DF,
CHDCSL), in terms satisfactory to the Bank (para. 3.2 (e)). Assurances were obtained
during negotiations that MTOP would provide the necessary technical support on
procurement and supervision of civil works to be financed by the project (para. 3.1 (o)).
Investment proposals would be gathered at the regional level by the Regional Irrigation
Boards which would submit the respective project profiles to UEP. This Project Executing
Unit would analyze and prioritize the proposed project ideas according to agreed selection
criteria and then submit them to CCI for a decision on whether financing for feasibility
studies is warranted. The feasibility studies would be carried out by private consulting firms
and be supervised by UEP. After completion of corresponding feasibility studies, the UEP,
in collaboration with DGRNR and DNH would review the draft studies and provide
comments on technical, economical, institutional and environmental aspects prior to issuing
their final version. As a condition of Loan Effectiveness, the Government would prepare
and approve, in terms satisfactory to the Bank, a Project Manual describing the eligibility,
and capital cost recovery criteria as well as the environmental screening procedures to be
followed in the selection of I&D subprojects (paras. 2.26 and 2.29), and demonstration farms
(para. 2.11) (para. 3.2 (f)). UEP would analyze the final results of the studies and would
rank those projects that meet the minimum economic criteria and environmental standards,
and submit them to CCI for approval prior to final design and construction. The list of
selected projects would be discussed with the Bank during the Annual Reviews (para. 2.73)
and would then be integrated into the follcwing year's project implementation program
(POA). These reviews would be held no later than June 30 of each year, three months
before the Government discussions on their annual budget.
2.70 Environmental impact assessment would be contracted by UEP in collaboration with
DGRNR and DINAMA. DGRNR (within MGAP) would be responsible for monitoring the
implementation of recommended actions regarding neutralizing possible negative
environmental aspects in any single irrigation project. Project activities in the Santa Lucia
Basin will be coordinated by UEP and CHDCSL (para 2.8), which would be the overall
coordinator with the various governmental institutions present in the region. Technical
assistance activities related to forestry development and promotion of nontraditional export
crops (paras. 2.20 and 2.38) would be executed by the Forestry Directorate, and the PENTA
Committee within MGAP, with the support of UPCT (Unidad de Proyectos y Cooperaci6n
TWcnica of MGAP) and the overall coordination of UEP.
Accounts and Audits
2.71 Executing agencies (MGAP and MTOP) and UEP would maintain separate accounts
to record their expenditures under the project using an agreed plan of project accounts. A
plan of accounts for overall project administration was discussed during negotiations (3.1
(p)). For each respective component of the project each implementing agency and the UEP
would have to establish an accounting procedure acceptable to the Bank. Each executing
agency will maintain records of project expenditures and send copies of those records to the
Project Executing Unit (UEP), which will maintain consolidated accounts. Statements of
Expenditures (SOEs) will be prepared by the implementing agencies coordinated by UEP.



- 33 -
These SOEs will be signed by the authorized representative of the respective implementing
agency. Copies of supporting documentation for SOEs provided by the executing agencies
would be maintained by UEP and made available to visiting Bank missions and independent
auditors, who at their discretion would make direct visits to the executing agencies for the
purposes of the overall audit. UEP would be responsible for final preparation of
disbursement applications to be signed by the Government's authorized representative. A
complete statement of disbursements made against each project component and activity would
be submitted to the Bank on a quarterly basis. Agreement was reached during negotiations
that project accounts, including the special account, and SOEs would be audited annually by
independent auditors acceptable to the Bank following terms of reference approved by the
Bank (para. 3.1 (q)). As a condition of Loan Effectiveness the Borrower would
demonstrate that MTOP, MGAP and the UEP have the capability to adapt accounting
procedures, and to maintain and control project accounts in a manner satisfactory to the Bank
(para. 3.2 (g)).
Monitoring and Evaluation
2.72 Monitoring would be based on the objectives and targets defined in the POAs, an
instrument that integrates all management functions of the proposed project into one single
tool: programming, budgeting, work organization and distribution of tasks, personnel
management and monitoring and evaluation. The UEP would coordinate the annual
development of the POA to be reviewed by the Bank and approved by the Borrower (i.e.,
CCI). It would have the responsibility for issuing, not later than three months after the end
of each respective period, semi-annual reports on progress of implementation of each project
component, which would be submitted to CCI, and through the Minister of Agriculture, to
the Bank. If implementation progress is not satisfactory for any of the components, UEP
would propose remedial action to be adopted by CCI in agreement with the Bank.
2.73 Annual reviews to be held no later than June 30 of every year, starting in 1994,
would be used for setting investment plans and establishing requirements for complementary
services. Overall project performance would also be assessed during the annual reviews. An
in-depth mid-term review would be carried out at the end of the third year of project
implementation in FY96 (June 1996) to: (a) assess progress in implementation measured by
performance objectives; (b) verify the continued validity of the project design in light of
implemenDtation experience; and (c) undertake necessary adjustments in project objectives,
design, and implementation arrangements. This review would be based on a mid-term
evaluation to be carried out by independent consultants at least two months prior to the
arrival of the Bank mission. Scope and content of the annual and mid-term reviews were
discussed and agreed during negotiations (para. 3.2 (r)) and incorporated as Annex 3 to a
draft Implementation Letter to the Loan Agreement.
2.74 The mid-term review will be used to take stock formally of the lessons from the
various pilot activities (paras. 2.8, 2.11, and 2.30) and studies (paras. 2.19 and 2.34) to be
supported by the project. This mid-term review would provide the stage for the Government
to articulate the subsector development strategy supported by the project, including possible
adjustments in the institutional/policy framework which might be advisable. Project targets
and follow-up actions to be discussed during the annual and mid-term review, will be defined
in accordance with the Project Performance Indicators (Annex 0). Such Project Perfonnance
Indicators, the GIS Action Plan, the Annual Work program (POA), and matters to be



- 34 -
discussed during the anmal and mid-term reviews were included in an Implementation Letter
to the Loan Agreement, discussed and agreed during negotiations (para. 3.1 (s)). A
detailed description of the areas to be addressed in the annual and mid-term reviews, is
presented in the Supervision Plan (Annex N).
2.75 Ex-post evaluation of rehabilitated and new irrigation projects, and of the pilot
program for the introduction of natural resources management techniques, would require
studies of physical performance of the investment and production systems and their socio-
economic impact. These evaluations would be contracted out to consulting firms or
university research centers. A preliminary evaluzJon would be carried out dunng the third
year of the project and presented to the mid-term review meeting. The terms of reference of
the study would be prepared by UEP and agreed with CCI and the Bank by the end of the
first year of project implementation. A summary of the monitoring program on water use
efficiency and soil conservation under the proposed I&D schemes, is presented in Annex K.
2.76 A major effect on sustainable agricultural growth will stem from alternative
production technologies that focus on more efficient and adequately designed natural resource
management practices. The introduction of a demand-driven approach to the rehabilitation
and expansion of the country's I&D infrastructure will stimulate vertical and horizontal
linkages across the public and private sectors, which will sustain long-term investment, rural
employment, and natural resource conservation.
2.77 While providing the means to support the development of a regulatory framework and
technical foundation to reduce potential agro-ecological imbalances and preserve the
country's natural resource base, the project would help to diversify production and increase
the competitiveness of the agricultural sector. The proposed investment program is expected
to benefit some 15,000 farmers or 25 percent of the entire farming community of Uruguay.
The main project benefits include: (a) increased production as a result of the expansiot of the
irrigated area by up to approximately 30,000 ha cultivated mostly by small and medium-size
farmers; (b) increased productivity based on higher crop yields due to the availability of
secure irrigation water, higher quality, and shifting investment to higher-value export crops;
(c) enhanced small-fhrmer income and welfare; (d) introduction of technical criteria that
would increase the efficiency and cost-effectiveness of water resource use; (e) improved
public and private resource allocation, introducing a comprehensive and consistent capital
cost recovery and O&M mechanism; and (f) improved soil and water management and
conservation practices leading to sustained agricultural sector growth. To examine the
potentidal impact of the project at the farm level, four feasibility studies of irrigation
investment proposals were analyzed during project preparation, including nine illustrative
farm models representing: (i) horticulture farms ranging from 4 ha to 30 ha; (ii) fruit tree
fms (17 ba); and (iii) a lage rice/livestock farm. Results-presented in working paper No.
7 (Muestra de Proyectos de Inversion de Riego)-showed a robust return on investment.



- 35 -
Economic ANsI
2.78 An overall economic rate of return has not been calculated due to the programmatic
nature of the project. However, the project would have a major impact on medium and
small-sized-farmers in the irigated areas and would improve the quality of life of a
significant proportion of small farmers located in the marginal areas of the Santa Lucia
Basin. A 12% rate of return is the minimum required for public investment; however, most
projects are likely to have substantially higher returns. Most potential investments currently
under review have estimated ERRs above 20%. The main assumptions adopted for the
calculation of these ERRs were the same as those used in the study on competitiveness of
Uruguayan agricultural products prepared by the MGAP with the assistance of the
Gesellschaft fir Technische Zusammenarbeit (GTZ), the German Govenmment Technical
Assistance Agency.
Environmental Impact
2.79 Most of the project's components are geared to improve resource management to
reduce degradation of soils, enhance productivity and efficiency in agriculure, and preserve
biodiversity (wetlands). In the development and operation and maintenance (O&M) of
irrigation projects, a number of standard enviromnental issues have been identified, such as
soil degradation and water contamination from agrochemicals under intensified agriculture,
wasteful use of water, deteriorating quality of irrigation water, some sedimentation of
reservoirs, and damage to biodiversity.
2.80 Through environmental assessment, steps will be taken within the project to address
the above issues at each stage of the project cycle; during identification of options to expand
agricultural output, initial project selection, feasibility analysis (with design modification to
mitigate negative impact where necessary), constuction, and monitoring and evaluation
(M&E) of the operational phase. In this process, particular attention will be given to
institutional questions in dealing with long-run intersectoral conflicts in the management of
water resources.
2.81 Activities would include strengthening local irrigation organizations and government
agencies directly associated with these organizations, promotion of beneficiary participation
in subproject design and implementation, and clarification through research, field testing and
extension of the equity, efficiency, and sustinability dimensions of intensification of land
and water through multiple uses.
Environmental Scree
2.82 Under the proposed project, all subprojects eligible for financing would be subject to
an environmental impact assessment to detect current and potential environmental problems
and recommend appropriate changes in design or mitigating measures (para. 2.70).
Assurances were obtained at negotiations that necessary steps would be taken by the
Government to ensure that environmental issues would be adequately considered in the
selection, design, construction, and O&M of irrigation schemes, as well as all other works
financed under the project, including agreement on enviromnental screening procedures as
outlined in Annex M, paras. 10 and 11 (para. 3.2 (t)).



- 36-
2.83 The potential for conflict between irrigation and preservation of wetlands represents a
special case in the managing of environmental aspects of the project. Under the 1971
Ramsar Treaty, the Govermnent of Uruguay agreed to set aside 435,000 ha as a wetlands
reserve. This was ratified in 1984 as a 325,000 ha reserve, excluding 110,000 ha of the
water area in the Uruguayan sector of Laguna Mann. By that date, much of this area had
already been modified by flood control and irrigation works, and additional areas were
modified subsequently. Further, significant wetland areas outside the Ramsar Treaty limits
have been similarly modified over the past two decades. The result has been an escalation of
conflicts arising witiin agriculture (upstream versus downstream users) and between
agriculture and environmental protection, both inside and outside the Ramsar reserve.
2.84 In response to this situation, three initiatives have been taken in an attempt to resolve
the conflict. First, DINAMA was charged in 1991 with leading an interagency task force to
define the precise area of undamaged or recoverable wetlands to be reserved. This is
expected to provide the basis for an eventual request to modify the Ramsar Treaty. The
revised area has been set at 140,000 has which falls mainly within the original Treaty limits.
Second, an inter-agency commission was established in 1991 to study the various conflicts in
a 360,000 ha area along the right bank of the Rio Cebollati in the Department of Rocha.
This study encompassed most, if not all, of the Ramsar Treaty area within the department
and a significant area outside the Treaty limits. This commission, consistent with the
findings of the above task force, will make a series of recommendations on hydraulic works
to resolve current conflicts among farmers, enable further irrigation development, and protect
certain natural areas that may be witiin or outside previously defined reserve areas. Third,
parallel with state efforts to specify and resolve the areas of conflict, a group of NGOs, with
external fumding, have a project underway to map all wetland areas that may be considered
in a "reasonable" state of preservation or worthy of reclamation. This map should be
completed by mid 1994, at which time it is expected that the group would lobby for official
designation of the whole area as reserve.
2.85 The project would not finance any irrigation or drainage acdvity within the present or
future Ramsar Treaty wetland areas nor in other protected wetland areas of the country.
Environmental criteria to be followed in the selection of I&D subprojects financed under the
proposed project are outlined in Annex M. In addition, any proposed I&D subproject
located in important wetland or riverine areas would be submitted to the Bank for prior
review and approval.
2.86 If the environmental aspects associated with the intensification and expansion of
irrigated agriculture are to be effectively managed, the institutional capacity must be
strngthened to generate information on the social, economic and ecological trade-offs
involved, and to facilitate dialogue among the vested interests in reaching decisions on water
policy and policy implementation. In this regard, fte studies of the Salto and Raig6n
aquifers to be funded under the project are expected to make a contribution. The capacity of
central government agencies, regional offices, and regional irrigation boards to apply
enviromnental procedures in the subproject cycle will be critical to the implementation of the
irrigation component.
2.87 UEP, in consultation with DGR1 R, would have the prime responsibility for screning
and recommending irrigation and drainage development proposals and overseeing the
feasibility, design, and constuction phases (paras. 2.69 and 2.70). The beneficiary groups



- 37 -
and the respective irrigation boards will have a major role in defining subproject proposals
and will be responsible for O&M after construction. The National Directorate of
Hydrography (DNH) would recommend the use of water for irrigation after evaluation of
existig and potential multiple uses. It is expected that under the new environrmental law,
DINAMA will have oversight authority in the implementadon of environmental assessment
(EA) requirements, quality of EA work, and setting water quality standards. The Minister of
Transport and Public Works, on recommendation of DNH, is ultimately responsible for
approving water resource allocation and related civil works according to a priority
frmework pre-established by law (i.e., potable water supply, followed by hydroelectricity,
then irrigation). OPP will have final authority over the adequacy of EA as a prerequisite to
approval of publicly financed subprojects and works.
2.88 A wide range of activities is underway and programmed to strengthen the capacity of
public and private entities to deal with enviromnental questions. DINAMA will receive a
US$1.1 million grant from the Inter American Development Bank (IDB) and a US$0.3
million grant from the European Economnc Community (EEC). The UNDP and UNEP are
also active and the Global Enviromnent Fund (GEF) recently approved a US$3.2 million
project on wetlands that includes institutional strengthening. Many bilateral agencies are also
funding insitutional support activities in various government agencies and NGOs. In
addition, NGOs have received extensive support from domestic sources as well as external
foundations and NGOs. A significant increase in technical capacity within the country can be
expected over the next two years.
Ridk
2.89 The institutional capacity to execute and coordinate the project, especially the natural
resources assessment and management aspects, may prove inadequate. This risk would be
mitigated by a strong focus on institutional strengthening and development, and the inclusion
of specific, programmed actions to solve capacity gaps.

A. GENERALBCfiQ
The Need for IhAtio
1.   Although the mean anmnal precipitation is above 1,000 mm in most parts of Uruguay,
potenal monthly evapotranspiration exceeds mean monthly ainfall during at least the four
summer months (November to Febary) of the year. This deficit grows from North to
South and its impact is furdher increased by the irregularity of mean monthly rainfall
patterns. Dry periods are common, especially during the main crop growing swason betwe
October and April. Under these conditions rained agriculture with winter and/or early,
short season crops is possible, but supplemeny irrigation is reqired for production of rice,
sugarcane, and most summer crops, such as maize and horticulture species. Although fnrit
trees grow under natural rainifall, irrgation is requred to ensure productivity, timely
production and the quality demanded by local and foreign markets.
2.   Irrigaton demands vary with climatic conditions durng the growg season and
prevailing soils. Deep soils of good water holding capacity demand less freqent irgaim
an use rainl more efficiently. However, most cultivated land in Uruguay have low water
storage capacities due to shallow soils (soils with a sballow A horizon underlied by a heavy
texured layer with little or no root peneraon), or light texures, which futher increases the
benefits of spplementary irrigation.
I ad and Water iI
3.    Unrguay has a total land area of 15,6 million hecares of which about 86% are under
permanent pau, 4% under forest and only 1,3 million heres (8%) are presently under
cultivation. Non agricultural land amounts to only 2% land area. About 4 million hectares
are potentially suitable for cultivation, but need to rotate pasure widt crops to avoid soil
degradation which would limit cropping to 1,6 million hectares in any one year. Most of
this land is currently culuvated under rainfed conditions but could be iringated if water was
available.
4.    Uruguay's main watersheds are: (a) I-a  Merin, (b) Rio Negro, (c) Rio de la
Plata, (d) Rio Santa Lucia, (e) Oc6ano Aindco and (f) Rio Uruguay. Most of the
watersheds are covered with pasture, have gently sloping topographies, with slopes of less
than 15%, and soils of low water storage capacities and infiltration rates because of
impervious layers close to the soil  ufce or lght textr  over basaltic matial. These
conditions combined with high innsity raifl lead to high mn-off and consequey to
laIge fluctations of river water levels and river flows.



-44-                                 Annex A
5.    Surface water resources require storage facilities for efficient use. Large reservoirs
on the Uruguay river (Salto Grande) and the Rio Negro (Dr. G. Terra, Rinc6n de Baygorria
and Constituci6n) supply hydro-electric power plants. Publicly financed medium sized
storage facilities include the Canel6n Grande reservoir (23 million cubic meters) in the Santa
Lucia river basin, originally constructed for urban and industrial water supply but recently
freed for irrigation; India Muerta (128 million cubic meters), which irrigates about 10,000 ha
in the rice producing areas of the Department of Rocha; and Minas de Corrales (45 million
cubic meters) with a potentially irrigable area of 3,500 hetares. Additionally, there are
about six reservoirs financed by private producers (Casarone, Bueno y Galgena, Castellu y
Barrios) with a total storage capacity of 117 million cubic meters built specifically for
irrigation purposes.

6.    An additonal number of small water catchments (earthfills) for irrigation have been
comstructed mainly under pnvate initiative. The exact number is not known but the
Directorate of Hydrology (DH) of MTOP registers 315 earthflls with a total reservoir
capacity of 782 million cubic meters.
7.    Irrigation in Uruguay is, and future expansions will be, mainly based on surface water
resources and new irrigation developments wiU require medium and small size storage
facilities to meet the increasing water demands during the main crop growing season. A
strict contol of water use would be required for an optimum use of the available surface
water resources and to avoid interference between different irrigation developments.
8.    Information on ground water is scarce. Hydrogeologic studies are limited to a 1:2
million hydrogeological cartography and the country lacks a systematic control of well
construction and ground water extraction. Although the use of groundwater for irrigation is



- 45-                                        AnnexA
of secondary importance on a nationwide basis, it could have a major local impact in case of
uncontrolled exploitation of the Raig6n and Salto aquifers, which are widely used for
irrigation of horticulture crops. These aquifers should be studied and controlled to avoid
interference of wells, contamination and salt water intrusion due to over-exploitation.
Extent of Irrigation
9.      The private sector has been the main driving force in irrigation development in
Uruguay. Public sector developments have been limited to small projects (i.e. Colonia
Espafia 750 ha, Tomas Berretta 360 ha, Aguas Blanas 125 ha) and to the India Muerta
project originally conceived for 12,000 hectares. Public sector projects generally suffer from
deferred maintenance and require rehabilitation. It is the Govermnent's intention to further
encourage private initiatives in irrigation development and to rehabilitate state owned
schemes and transfer use and administration of them to the beneficiaries.
10.    The irrigated area has grown from about 50,000 hectares in 1970 to almost 160,000
hectares in 1992. Rice is the major irrigated crop and represents 78.8% of the irrigated
area, followed by sugar cane with 6.1%, horticulture crops with 4%, top seeded and
cultivated pastures with 3.2%, citrus with 3.2%, and deciduous fruit trees with 3% . The
remaining 1.7%  refer to summer crops such as maize. The development of the irrigated area
since 1970 is shown in the table below.

Soil and Water Conservation
11. The prevailing irrigated soils have a tendency to lose soil structure and porosity under
intensive cultivation, which leads to insufficient root aeration, lower infiltration rates and soil
moisue storage capacities, increased run-off and erosion and surface crusting. The loss of
organic matter also affects soil fertility. Irrigation development should therefore consider
crop rotations, which include pastures and the incorporation of organic matter in the fonn of
green manure, to reduce the above-mentioned effects.
12.   About 30% of the Uruguayan crop and pasture land is affected by slight, moderate or
severe erosion, mainly due to overgrazing of natural pastures, intensive cultivation, use of
inadequate rotations and poor tillage practices. The destruction of watersheds leads to more
accentuated peak and low flows in the natural water courses, increased erosion, silting of
reservoirs and loss of pasture lands. The Ministry of Livestock and Agriculture recognized
the problem and started in 1970, an ambitious soil conservation program oriented towards
construction of terraces and introduction of credit for grain production subject to the use of
contour cultivation methods (strip cropping).
13.   Within ten years the program constructed terraces on almost 10,000 hectares, and
inroduced contour leveling and strip cropping on about 82,000 hectares. However, the
rsult was disappointing as the program did not address the main problem and did not include
the introduction of agronomic practices which would secure soil structure stability and avoid
deterioration of infiltration rates. Today, extended crop - pasure rotations for conservation
of the organic content and physical characteristics of the soils are widespread. Vertical and
minimum or zero tillae practices are being introduced in the western part of the country
along the Uruguay river. Intensive horticulture uses atimal manure and general cropping
green manures. Soil and water conservation practices in orchards include contour plantations
on wide terraces and reduced tillage by using herbicides on the rows and establishing of
sodded grasses in the inter-rows.
14.   Substantial volumes of water are lost to the Rio de la Plata, Laguna Merin and the
Atlantic Ocean during periods of heavy rainfall. Topographic conditions would allow for a
country-wide small and medium reservoir construction program to increase the overall water
use efficiency. However, an improvement in the efficiency of surface water conservation
and use requires an effective control of available water resources, water use and rights. DH
issues water use rights, mantains an inventory on surface water resources and issues annual
publications with statistical data. Information on riverflows are systematically collected and
immediately processed. However, DH has been unable to include all storage reservoirs and
pumping sations along the rivers in its program. The difficulty arses when the private
sector builds dams and/or pumping stations without the use of official credit from BROU,
who would request a priori approval of DH. In this case DH lacks information and as a
result, conflicts due to interference between irrigation infrastructure are becoming more
frquent.
15.   The National Directorate of Mining and Geology (DINAMIGE), located within the
Ministry of Industry and Energy, is responsible for ground water surveys and exploitation
and also carries out other geological studies and drilling activities. DINAMIGE should also
control the instllation of wells, monitor ground water exploitation, and forward the



-47-                                  Annx A
statistical data to DH for inclusion in its data bank. However, this is not the case and at
present there is no control on groundwater extraction and interference between wells,
contamination and salt water intusion constitute a real danger in the heavily exploited
Raig6n and Salto aquifers.
B. DESCRIPTION OF THE MAIN IIRIGATED AREAS
16.   At present irigation is mainly limited to the norhern, eastern and southern regions,
which are characterized and differentiated mainly by prevailing soils and water resources,
type of prodcers and producer organztions and existing infrastructre, rather than by
climatic conditions. The three regions account for 95% of the national irrigated area. In the
westem litoral and center regions, agriculture involves livestock production through extensive
grazing of natural pastures and mixed rainfed grain and livestock production. However, part
of these areas and specifically the western litoral has some potential for development of
irrigated rice and citrus plantations.

The Northern Region
17. The northern region includes part of the departments of Artigas, Salto, Tacuarembo
and Rivera and covers almost 25% of the national territory. The area has a mean annual
rainfall between 1100 mm and 1300 mm and mean monthly temperatres range from 13-C to
270C. Annual potential evapotranspiradon averages 1300 mm. Most of the area has shallow
clay soils over basalto and is suitable for extensive grazng only. Irrigation developed mainly
on the better soils along the Uruguay, Cuarem and Tacuarembo rivers. The total irrigated
area amounts to about 44,000 hecrs, with rice accounting for 70%, sugarcan  for 22%
and citrs and horticulture crops for dt  remaining area.
18.   The private sector developed the flat lands in the Tauarembo and Uruguay river
basins, where about 200 prducers farm some 30,000 hectares with rice. Small- and
medium-sized dams and pumping statons on river banks supply earth canal conveyance and
distribution networks and irrigate 9,000 and 21,000 hectares, respectively. Field irrigation
consists of the flooding of paddy fields. The rice area of the northern region is expected to
increase in the near futre because of availability of suitable land, well-textured and easy to
till soils, and an adequt  _opq_      *e constmcion of medium- and small-sized
tv ;St>,-t:.'   .;



- 48 -                             Annex A
reservoirs. An estimated additional 30,000 hectares of rice in rotation with pasture could be
developed.
19.   Ahnost 400 farmers and one state owned enterprise (ANCAP) cultivate about 9,600
ha of sugar cane on complex light to heavy textured soils along the Uruguay river.
Individual farms average about 20 ha in size. ANCAP manages 1400 ha of sugar cane for
alcohol production. Government plans call for the privatization of ANCAP, including its
industrial facilities. The main irrigation water source for this sugar growing area is the
Uruguay river. Water is pumped and distributed through earth canal networks and fields are
irrigated by gravity, in contour furrows. About 60% of the sugar cane area has collective
irrigation networks and important areas are forced to diversify their production as a result of
Government's open market policies (e.g. Colonia Espafia).
20.  Irrigated citrus plantations occupy about 1800 ha of mostly sandy soils in the
department of Salto. Citrus farmers require irrigation to meet the high quality demands of
the nternational markets. Most plantations are private developments which draw water from
small- and medium-sized reservoirs or through pumping from natural water courses and
irrigate with localized irrigation methods.
21.   Private farmers developed about 1,000 ha for production of pre-seasonal horticulture
crops for local markets near Salto and Beila Union. Exports of mainly berries to Buenos
Aires started on an experimental basis. Cultivation takes place in greenhouses or under low
cost frost protection systems (quinchos). Production units are normally small, with farmers
drawing their irrigation water from wells and irrigating by localized irrigation methods. The
only collective system is CALAGUA, located in Bella Union, which was originally designed
for production of 3,000 ha of sugar cane and is now diversifying towards field crops and
horticulture. Expansion of irrigation for citrus and horticulture production in the northern
regions will be based on development of water resources for irrigation of presently rainfed
farms, rather than bringing new land into cultivation.
The Eastern Re ion
22.   The eastern region comprises parts of the departments of Cerro Largo, Treinta y
Tres, Rocha and Lavalleja and covers about 12% of the national territory. It includes the
Laguna Merin and part of the Rio Negro and Atlantic Ocean basins. The area has an
average annual rainfall of between 1,000 mm and 1,200 mm and the mean annual potential
evapotranspiration ranges between 900 mm and 1,200 mm. Mean monthly temperatures vary
from 120C during the coldest and 24C during the hottest month. Private sector initiative
developed the region into the main rice growing area of the country where about 500 farmers
cultivate some 95,000 hectares of rice annually on the flat lands along the Yaguaron,
Tacuari, Olimar, and Cebollati rivers. An average producer farms about 200 ha of paddy.
Because of the type of soils, irrigated cropping is limited almost exclusively to rice
production and only about 1,000 ha are dedicated to maize, soybeans and irrigated pasture.
23.   Heavy and poorly drained gleysols with a high organic matter content dominate in the
southern part and lighter textured plansols and solods with a low organic content in the north
of the region. About 63,000 hectares are served by pumping stations located on non-
regulated rivers and lagoons and the remaining 32,000 hectares by small, and medium-sized



-49 -                              Annex A
dams and by the large India Muerta reservoir. The latter is a public sector project which
allows for annual cultivation of 10,000 ha of paddy in rotation with pastures. The public
sector also constructed drainage works to benefit some 100,000 hectares. Almost 40% of the
irrigation systems are collective, each serving between 5 to 20 producers.
24.   There is sufficient land available to incorporate an additional 30,000 hectares of rice
under a 2 year rice - 6 year pasture rotation. However, the availability of water at
reasonable distances from potential rice producing areas is a major constraint for new
developments. Construction of small- and medium-sized storage facilities and regulation of
the main rivers would be a possible solution. Rice production could also be increased by
shortening the prevailing 2 year rice - 6 year pasture rotation to a 2 year rice - 4 year pasture
rotation.
The Southern Region
25.   The southern region covers the departments of Colonia, San Jose, Canelones and
Montevideo. The average annual rainfall amounts to about 900 mm, potential
evapotranspiration varies from 1,000 mm to 1,200 mm per year and mean monthly
temperatures range between I1 C and 230C. Most soils are medium textured and of limited
depth, underlain by mostly silty material. The topography is smoothly undulated with under
4% slopes.
26.   For many years this area has supplied the Montevideo market with cereals,
vegetables, fruit, and milk products. However, about 100 years of intensive land use caused
degradation of physical soil properties and subsequent erosion of extensive areas in the
northeast of this region. The total irrigated' area amounts to 11,000 hectares including 5,300
ha of horticulture crops, 4,000 ha of orchards with deciduous fruit trees and 250 ha of citrus
plantations. Irrigated fruit and horticulure farms are generally small. About 2,000 fruit and
vegetable producers farm an average of 5 ha each under irrigation. The one exception is
"Mi Granja", a large private development, dedicated, among others, to production of apples
and peaches under drip irrigation.
27.   Private initiative accounts for most of the irrigation development in this region and
presently sophisticated localized irngatton methods are replacing more traditional furrow and
sprinlder irrigation methods. About 50% of the irrigated area uses ground water; for the
remaining area farmers collect water from small streams and waterways. During the last ten
years irrigation of deciduous orchards expanded significantly, mainly to increase productivity
and quality of fruit and enable farmers to compete in the local and international markets.
28.   Water shortages and lack of capital are the main constraints for further irrigation
development in this region. The main water source is the heavily exploited Raig6n aquifer.
There is a real danger that, in the absence of an effective control of ground water
exploitation, interference between individual wells may increase and ground water
contamination and salt water intrusion develop into a main constraint to agricultural
production. Expansion of irrigation with surface water must be approached on an overall
basis, as most holdings are too small to offer adequate sites for construction of a storage
facility or the latter would occupy a large proportion of the agricultural land.



-50 -                              Annex A
C. MAIN FEATURES OF IMRIGATED CROPS
29. Uruguay's area under rice increased from 31,000 to 125,000 hectares since 1971 in
response to relatively low cost irrigation water, availability of suitable land and a strong
demand in the inutnational markets. Similarly, rice exports grew from US $ 7 million to
US $ 114 million since 1971 and today represent between 5% and 10% of all agriculturad
exports. Most of the Urguayan rice exports (94%) are sent to Brazil.
30.   Uruguayan rice is known for its quality; about 95% of all plantings are of the long
grain Patna type. Yields average around 4.5 Ton. of paddy per hectare and depend mainly
on climatic conditions. Delays in land preparation due to rainfall and excessive water in the
fields often result in late summer flowering and reduction of yields due to lower temperatures
and less sunlight.
31. Rice is normally grown in rotation with pastures, generally two years of rice followed
by six years of pasure. In many areas this rotation, whose main purpose is to avoid weed
control with expensive herbicides and soil conservation, could be shortened to 2 years of rice
followed by 4 years of pasture. Most rice growers are entrepreneur sharecroppers who
operate under anal contracts and pay about 8 to 10 bags of paddy (50 kg/bag) for land and
20 bags for water. Land and water may have different owners and their joint cost represents
about 30% of the producers' gross income. Rice growers normally farm 150 ha each, and
move their machinery and workers on a yearly basis. Farming operations are fully
mechanized. Water consumption varies widely from 7,000 mn3 to 20,000 rn3 per hectare.
Irrigation efficiencies are low as rice farmers pay a fixed amount and are not charged by
volume of water actually used.
SugE
32. In response to Government's past subsidies aimed at reducing sugar imports, the area
under sugar cane grew from 7,000 ha to 9,000 ha and yields from 4,8 to 6,8 tons of sugar
per hectare over the last decade. However, with the present open market policies, these
subsidies will disappear and farmers will be forced to diversify their prduction. Irrigation
systems consist normally of a main pumping station on the Uruguay river bank, and a
distribution system of earth canals, secondary pumping ulits and compensation reservoirs,
and have to be adapted to the needs of future crop diversification.
33. Sugar cane is a monoculture with replanting every 4 to 5 years. Generally, early (NA
6344) and late (TUC 6818, TUC 6819, FAM 6318) varieties are combined in order to exend
the harvestng period. Contour furrow iigaton is widespread. Individual farmers with an
average of 23 ha produce under comact with the main sugar cane cooperatve CALNU,
which also supplies the ncessary inputs, machinery services and technical assistance. The
state owned ANCAP company manages 1400 ha mainly for alcohol production and, in
response to prevailing open market policies, plans to privatze its planation and industrial
facilities.



- 51-                              Annex A
citrus
34. The area of irrigated citrus increased substantially during the last deca-4e as a result of
subsectorial policies aimed at improving the competitive position of Uruguayan products in
the international market, and now reaches some 5,000 ha. External market demands for high
quality fruit as well as the need for qualitative and quantitative stability and timely delivery
have encouraged farmers to invest in sophisticated localized irrigation systems. Citrus export
rocketed from US$ 0,5 million in 1972 to US$ 22 million in 1992. Today about 25% of the
country's citrus plantations have irrigation systems and according to the National Citrus Plan
Commission (CHPC) 6,000 ha will be brought under irrigation in the short term, and an
additional 5,000 ha in the near future.
35. Climate and soil conditions in the north western litoral and in the south west of the
country permit the production of high quality fruit which competes without major problems
on the European off-season market. Main constraints are the risk of frost and water deficits.
During the 1988/89 drought period citrus production dropped significantly and led large
farmers and companies who represent about 35% of the area under citrus, to install irrigation
systems in about 85% of their plantations. Small farmers (10 to 20 ha) in the traditional
citrus growing area around Salto are interested in improving their orchards to become
competitive. These improvements would include planting of new genetic material, irrigation
and introduction of advanced soil and water management practices.
36.   The impact of irrigation on yield and quality has been recorded by high technology
farmers and research carried out by the university under contract with private producers.
The study concluded that in case of Valencia oranges, increases of 20% to 40% in yields and
35% to 65% in the proportion of exportable fruit can be attributed to irrigation.
Deciduous Fruit Trees and Grapes
37. Since 1980 deciduous fruit trees occupied about 12,000 hectares, with apples
accounting for 51%, peaches 18%, and pears 16 % of the total production. In 1980 less than
10% of these plantations were irrigated. Today the area under irrigation has increased to 33
percent. Montewideo was the traditional market for deciduous fruit. Its demands were fairly
constant and prices related directly to volumes available. Thus years of large supplies and
low prices were compensated by years of low supply and high prices and production without
irrigation remained a reasonably profitable undertaking.
38.   Present open market policies imply a more aggressive competition with supplies from
other countries and farmers are encouraged to invest in irrigation to increase their
productivity and quality and therefore their competitiveness on the local market. Yield
increases due to irrigation of about 40% in the case of apples and peaches have been
recorded. Exports, mainly to Brazil, are still limited. The following table sununarizes
exports of deciduous fruit during the last 5 years.

39.   In general, export efforts will only become feasible if enough volumes are produced
and adequate packing, cool storage and marketing infasucture enter into operation. So far,
MI GRANJA has planted 577 ha under computerized drip irrigation, including 303 ha of
apple trees, 74 ha of pear trees, 42 ha of peach trees and other fruit trees, for export to
Europe and USA, and other cooperatives (e.g. JUMECAL and UFRUCA) plan to introduce
irrigation to improve their productivity and quality and compete in the intermational market.
40.   About 12,000 hectares of grapes are cultivated in Canelones and around Montevideo.
The total area has been decreasing over the last 20 years but farmers replaced the old
varieties by hybrids and officially certified virus free materials. These new plantations
doubled the yields of the old vineyards and thus production levels have been maintained and
price distortions avoided. In 1990 only about 6% of the area under grapes, mainly table
grapes in the Bella Union area, were equipped for irrigation. The production of table grapes
focusses on internaonal   rkets and production under high levels of cultivation and post
harvestig technology have led to promising export experiences.
41.   The main constraint to expansion of irrigation in deciduous orchards and vineyards is
the availability of water resources and limited investment capacity of the producers. Ground
water is scarce and the generally small production units do not offer adequate topographic
sites for individual storage facilities which in addition would occupy a significant part of the
farmers' land. Collective systems would improve the efficiency of land and water use, but
their design and organization are more complex and thus escape the farmers' implementation
capability.
Horticltue
42.   Horticultural products are mainly marketed locally. Major production areas extend
around Montevideo (Canelones and San Jose) and in the areas of Salto and Bella Union
which have a micro climate that is suitable for production of early tomatoes, sweet pepper
and melons ahead of any other part of the country. The latter producers apply modern and
sophisticated technologies and initiated exports of strawberries from Salto to Buenos Aires.
43.   The exisence of a vegetable packing, cold storage and freezing plant
(GREENFROZEN) finaced by IDB and presently under-utilized, would support any
