expansion of horticultural production in areas under sugar cane, and facilitate future exports
(i.e. Colonia Espaila). During the 1988 -1991 period average annual exports amounted to

44. The total area under horticultural production decreased between 1980 and 1990 from
58,000 to 40,000 hectares probably due to the replacement of small low technology
producers by more efficient farmers with improved cropping technologies. Volumes supplied
to the local market remained fairly constant. About 14% of the area dedicated to horticultwe
is under irrigation (sprinkler and drip) which, as in the case of orchards, improves the
productivity of the farm and the quality of the produce. Similarly, domestic market
consants and absence of adequate marketing infrastructure for exports limit the expansion
and more rapid adoption of irrigation in the case of horticultural crops. Research and
technical assistance organizations (INIA, JUNAGRA), jointly with farners, initiated pilot
production and export programs for melons to Europe and sweet onions to USA.
Corn and Forage
45. Maize occupies some 74,000 hectares of which only 3,000 hectares are under
irrigation. Ylelds of up to 10 tons of grain per hectare have been obtained under efflcient
irgation and with good cultivation practices. However, irrigation of maize is only
financially attractive if investment costs are low (less than US$ 1,000 per hectare) and yields
reach top levels. Furrow irrigation or low cost sprinkler equipment may make irrigation
attractive, but extended conveyance systems, large pumping lifts and/or the use of self
propelled irrigation equipment are seldom financially justified. Intensive daity farms are
experimentng with irrigation of corn for silage which would improve their feed basis during
the winter month and increase milk production when the milk prices reach their highest level.
46.   Maize for both grain and silage are grown in rotation with pastures, the latter either
for graing, hay production or seed production. Such a diversified system reduces the fixed
cost and therefore improves the financial viability of irrigation. Irigation of pastures may
also be increased if farmers take advantage of irrigation systems designed for rice production
under rice - pastre rotations.

NATURLAL RESQU&C1SM-MAAGEN   AN MIG1
IC:RHENPT DEVELOP   PROM
A. ITBQPD1UCIION
1.    Soil degradadon has become an issue of main concern, as it affects the country's
development potential in the long run. Among the most severely affected areas showing
moderate to severe soil erosion problems, are those which have been devoted to intensive
agriculture in the crescent around Montvideo, including parts of the Sana Lucfa River Basin
(i.e., Canelones, Florida and San JosO). In these areas, particularly in Canelones, small-size
holdings have forced farmers to cultivate land intensively in the past vith little regard for soil
cnservation. Consequeny, despite the country's gende topography, it would seem that
inappropriate land-use and cultivation practices have been mainly responsible for losses of
fility and soil stucre. While the extent of soil degradation has not yet reached anming
proportions, the problems are likely to be aggravated if agtural diversification policies
are not accompanied by a sound natural resource management strategy.
2.    There is already a substatial volume of information on appropriate naual resources
managm ent practices available in the county. Most of it has not left the research stations
and has not been fully tested on a commercial scale. The proposed project would provide
tecnical assistance to proces available infomation on natural resources management with a
view towards developing technical packages suitable for the country's various agro-ecological
zones. The project would have an intrdisciplinary approach aimed at comple     and
nforcing already-available research data and relevant reach projects already in
progress.'
3.    The most significant land management problems are somewhat similar in various parts
of Uruguay. They rewire iputs from several conventional disciplines in the search for
soiutions (such as basic and applied aspects of: physical, chemial and biological branches of
soil science; plant sciences; micro and macro-econocs). Their resolution merits takig an
overall view of soil and water conservation issues, putting together a coherent program of
applied research to be underaken by vaious specialized bodies. Along these lines, it would
be importnt to ensure a multidisciplinary approach to technology generation and transfer,
avoiding problems caused by different agencies wodring indenely of each other and
without attempting to inter-mesh their programs towards a common purpose.
4.    Principles and practices of better land use and management, validated within the
micro-catciet (SAR para. 2.8) and demonstration farms (SAR para. 2.11), are expected
to have wider applications, not only with respect to non-rigated production per se but also
wih respect to catcbment areas upstream of irrigation schemes in other parts of the country.
1.    See Donuto de Trabjo 4, uvestgaci6 en Maneo deSuelos y Riego (on fie).



- 56 -                             Annex B
It is expected that improvements in land use and management in accordance with adequately-
validated recommendations will increase farm output and income. This will allow an
increase in living standards for nrual families in the micro-catchnment areas, while reversing
the present trend of soil degradation.
5.    Given the severity of the poential soil degradation problem and the economic
significance of the Santa Lucia River Basin, it has been decided to select that area as the
main focus for a pilot program in natural resources management in nicro-catchment areas.
6.   In the Santa Lucia Micro-catcbment Progran, the main "iterdisciplinary coordinator"
will be the farmer, by whom all agricultural research results will be ultimately tested and
validated. In the technical context of the National Honorary Commission for the
Development of the Santa Lucia River Basin (CHDCSL), four micro-catchunents, which are
considered to be representative of differng conditions within the larger catchiment of the
Santa Lucia River, have been selected. It is proposed to focus on their recuperation,
stabilization and development by identing, testing and applying appropriate
recommendations for their proper use and management, so that they may in time become
self-demonstrating areas. From these initial areas, famer-validated iovements will then
be ifradiated outward into urrounding areas and into similar situons in more dista
localities.
7.    Some 350 farmers within the four micro-catchment pilot areas will benefit from the
program. The project would provide financial resources in the form of a grant to encourage
farmers to undertake on-farm investments designed to reduce soil degradation and erosion.
The selected areas are representative of the various farming and socio-economic conditions
prevailing in the region and thus have a pontal demonstration effect. The strategy to be
adopted would involve the organization of farmers in the selected miro-cachments within
the Santa Lucia basin, who would additionally receive taining and technical aistan  for
the promotion of appropriate technological packages designed to conserve natural resources
and sustain agricultural production.
8.    The conclusions expected to be reached from the implementation of these pilot
schemes, combined with the results from the demonstration farms (SAR para. 2.11) will
provide reliable and representative data which could be extrapolated to various other areas in
need of improved natural resources management, within and outside the Santa Lucia River
Basin. In tun, this experience will help the Govemnment to design and implement an
environmentally sound sector development strategy.



- 57 -                             Annex B
B. THE NATURAL RESOURCES MANAGEMENT IN SELECTED MICRO-
CATCHMENTS IN THE SANTA LUCIA RIVER BASIN
General Background on Santa Lucia
Ihvsical Characteristics
9.    Location. The Santa Lucia catchmnent as a whole lies between 55000'W and 57010'W,
33040'S and 3450'S, and covers 13.500 kin2. The four micro-catchment areas tha have
been identified witiin this basin as representative of the natural resources management issues
facing the whole River Basin are Cafiada de los Alamos, Arroyo la Feliciana, Arroyo Tala,
and Canel6n Grande.
10.   Climate. The climate of the Santa Lucia region is defined as humid temperate, with
mean annual rainfall of 1,I00mm irregularly distributed throughout the year. February and
September are the wettest months, both recording between 9% and 11% of total amnual
rainfall. April and December are the driest months, with generally less than 6% of rainfall
in each. The mean temperature of the coldest month is 1 C and of the hottest month 230C.
Potential evapotranspiration is around 860mm per year. In the northeast of the catchment it
is reported that the water deficit may reach 50-60mm in summer, while having an excess of
about 200mm in spring.
II. Runoff. Variations in streamflow are indicated by those reported for the main Santa
Lucia River - from 2.700 m3/sec to 5m3/sec, with a mean of 28m3/sec. By extrapolation of
data from gauging stations on the Rio Santa Lucia, Rio Santa Lucia Chico, Rio San Jos6 and
Arroyo Casupa, estimates have been made of maximnum rates of runoff across the whole
catchment, which range from 8.70-15.59 liters/sec/km2. Graphing of recorded streamfow
information indicates in very general terms that the smaller the catchment, the higher the
specific runoff.
12.   Estimates of runoff made at gauging stations in the Santa Lucia catchment indicate
that mean runoff from different parts of the catchment represents between 274 and 491mms.
- or up to almost 1/3rd. - of annual rainfall. Mean annual rainfall is not a good direct
indicator of quantities of soil moisture for crop growth, since allowance must be made both
for the fact of the mean being skewed by a few, very wet years, and for the effects of runoff
in diminishing potential soil moisture.
13. Topo2raphy. Most of the micro-catchments lie at altitudes below 130 meters above
sea level and have a gently undulating topography, with natural slopes very seldom in excess
of 8%. Micro-relief may locally be steeper due to gullying or to tillage practices which, not
following the contour, may have produced accentuated, artificial micro-fratures up- and
down-slope. Stream gradients are generally less than the lateral slopes, though may be
steeper in the furthermost reaches of the micro-catchments within each micro-cachment.



- 58-                              Annex B
14.   Soils. In the Santa Lucia catchment as a whole, soils have formed principally from
sediments. They are generally leached with varying severity of imperfect internal drainage.
They include Gleysols, Planosols, Solonetzes, Arenosols, in lower positions. Brunosols and
Vertisols, blackish in color and of silty clay loam texture, occupy upper positions. On side-
slopes, soils are Brunisols which are greyer and more leached. The upper soils are of good
fertility, with high nutrient-retention capacity, and pH neutral to moderately acid. Some
areas have phosphate deficiencies, and low content of organic matter.
Production Suport &Srvices
15.   There are some extension services currently provided by Government. Also, certain
technical advice may be obtained through membership of cooperatives or comparable
organizaons which may be product-focussed or providing overall agricultural service in a
particular geographical area.
16. Animal improvement services are offered by the Secretariado Uruguayo de la Lana,
the Asociaci6n Rural del Uruguay and other private institutions. Milk producers are
represented by the Asociaci6n Nacional de Productores de Leche. Vegetable, fruit and grape
producers associate in the Confederacion Granjera. There are several single-product small
associations. Research services are provided by the Instituto Nacional de Investigacion
Agropecuaria (INIA). The University's Agronomy Faculty in Montevideo is also prominent
in agricultural research of relevance to the Santa Lucia region.
M - d e
17.   Grapes of good quality for wine production are bought and processed by "bodegas"
handiing more than 250,000 liters/yr. About 15% of fiuits and vegetables are consumed on
farms; about 16% are sold to retailers or direct to consumers; the remaining 69% are
generaPy sold to wholesalers. Prices of fruit and vegetables are generally determined by the
transactions at the Mercado Modelo in Montevideo. Produce of excellent quality may be
sold under contract to supermarkets within the country, or to exporters through packing
companies.
18.  In the Santa Lucia area many farmers sell produce through intermediaries, who either
buy outright or undertake to sell the produce on behalf of the seller. Such a system is most
prevalent where farmers may not have means of transport to take their own produce to
markets, or where they are far or isolated from the main markets in Montevideo.
19.   Fmit and vegetable products for industializaon are either produced by the
processors themselves, or bought through contracts with farmers' groups, cooperatives and
individual growers, in most cases using the specialized varieties, dates of planting and
harvest, and indices of maturity which are specified by the processors. This specialized
production revolves chiefly around tomatoes, asparagus, peppers and oregano.
20.   Most of the milk production in the Santa Laia River Basin is sold to the national
dairy cooperative (CONAPROLE). Some of the milk is sold as "quota" milk at a bigher
price fixed by Govemment, and the rest of it as milk for iialiation at a lower price,
determined by market forces. Milk derivatives account for 43% of aU marketed milk.



- 59 -                             Annex B
Home-made cheeses account for 98% of milk processed on the farm. For lack of
standardization of quality and of labelling, market prices for such products are relatively low
when sold directly to consumers in local markets such as San Jose; those of better quality
may command higher prices from intermediaries for selling-on in other markets.
Socio-economic Aspects
21.  Information about the situation in the micro-catchments could only be inferred from
data for the Sector Census. By special arrangement, detailed infonmation about the four
individual catchment areas alone was extracted from the 1990 Censo Agropecuario.
However, as tihe complete results of the census have not yet been published, it is not yet
(March 1993) possible to make direct comparisons between 1980 and 1990 even at the level
of the larger Sector Census.
22.   Population. If national trends measured between 1961 and 1980 continue, and
assuming the same trends are representative of each of the four micro-catchbmt pilot areas,
then there may have been a reduction in the mumber of farm families, and a small increase in
the ratio of laborers to farmers. With the decrease in rural population, a larger proportion of
those leaving the farm were female. By the end of 1980, 58.1 % of the nation's agricultural
population was male, and 41.8% female.
23.   Education. Taking as a sample Caiiada de los Alamos, in 1990, 70% of farmers and
60% of their spouses had some primary education, 10% and 30% had some secondary
education, and 5% and 0%, respectively, had some university education. Levels of school
attendance by children were also considered to be relatively good. These levels are in fact
higher than those found in Canelones Department in 1980.
Farm Size and Land Tenur
24. At the time of the Census, in Sector Census 2021 - within which Cafnada de los
Alamos is located - 76.8% of farms were less than 50 ha. In the micro-catchment area of
the Santa Lucia River Basin in 1990, 86.6% of farms were less than 50 ha, and occupied
48.7% of the micro-catchment area. Conversely, farms over 100 ha in size comprised
13.3% of all farms, but occupied 51.3% of this area.
25.  In terms of numbers of farms, more than half occupy only 13% of the total area.
Conversely, over 60% of the area is divided between only 13% of the number of farms.
While more than 50% of farmers are owners, more than 50% of the land is held under a
"combination" form of ownership.
Land Use Patern
26.   About 40% of the Santa Lucia River Basin is under perennial vegetation with little or
no disturbance by tillage; about 9.2% is under fruits and vines; 30.3% is under annual crop
production; 17.3% is under planted pasture (which is of only short - 2 to 3 years' - duration
related to weed invasion), and 2.6% is considered non-productive.



- 60 -                             Annex B
27. Of perennial crops, 84% are vines and 16% fruits. Of tilled crops, 53% are annual
forages, 26% vegetables, 10% cereals and 11% industrial crops. Forests cover 35%S of the
area, and 65% is under natural vegetation. Of permanent pastures, 65% is natural pasture,
32% planted pasture, and 3% improved natural pasture.
28.   Wheat is the most widespread crop in the micro-catchment area, but mainly on farms
of more than 100 ha. Maize is generally confined to holdings of less than 50 ha. Potatoes
are grown on all sizes of farms. Cucumbers are grown only on larger farms, while other
horticultural crops (e.g., sweet potatoes, sweet corn, onions, sweet peppers and garlic) are
grown on farms less than 50 ha. Forage species are primarily oats, maize (for grazing and
silage) and grain sorghum. Permanent crops are table grapes, peaches, "membrillos",
apples, cherries and pears; all these are produced on farms less th  50 ha in size.
Eucalyptus spp. are preferred for forestry plantings.
29.   Three distinct groups of farmers occupy the micro-catchment area. The most
nmerous are grape-growers, cultivating a variety of low yielding and moderate quality
varieties. The second most numerous group is more heterogeneous, producing fruits and
vegetables. A range of crops are produced, predomnantly peaches, apples, membrillo, pear,
potatoes, cucumber, onions and garlic. There is some specialization in the last two crops.
The third group are dairy farmers.
30.   Characterization of farm systems is presented as a series of farm models derived from
individual-farm data in the micro-catchment area, collected during the 1990 census and
specially extracted for the project by the Direcci6n de Investigaciones Agropeuarias (DIEA
of MGAP). These data were updated and expanded with a survey in 1992 of 25% of
frmers in the micro-catchments (see Annex C). The accuracy of the data has been checked
with advisers from CONAPROLE and JUNAGRA.
31.   These updated models (1992) cover the following types of farm:
(a)   fruit and vegetables on a farm of 12.8 ha, representing 18.3% of producers
and 11% of the area;
(b)   grapes on a farm of 7.6 ha, representing 38.3% of producers and 13.8% of
area; and
(c)   milk production on a farm of 60 ha (with 55 head of cattle) representing
20.0% of producers.
32.   Models of the same farms have been constructed retrospectively for year 1980, so as
to provide some idea of the evolution which may have occurred in the period 1980-1992,
though it is recognized that data for 1992 are much more accurate than those for 1980 (taken
from grosser Sector Census units). The data, assumptions and methodology employed in
conswtctig the models - both to typify past trends and to make future projections - are
provided in earlier reports produced by CHDCSL (CHDCSL, Dec. 1990).



-61 -                              Annex B
33.   Statistical data from the survey indicate that:
(a)   In terms of soil conservation practices, only 5% of farmers questioned said
they cultivated on the contour and 5% turned ploughed soil upsiope;
(b)   15% of those with animals practice continuous grazing, implying that 85%
operate types of intermittent/rotational grazing; 45% do not "clean" their
pastures by burning; and
(c)   36% of the area received some fertilizer in 1990, the majority of it applied on
land under 'combination" type of tenure. Almost no farmers use organic
manures. "Occupants" of land did not use any fertilizer. Where fertilizer was
applied, the amount per ha varied between 30.2 and 72.7 kg./ha, the larger
amount being applied on farms over 100 ha.
Irrgation and Drainage
34.   5% of farmers surveyed have small dams capable of water storage, while 20% have
small structures to trap runoff for water supplies for animals.
35.   There are apparently 26 water-pumps capable of pumping 30,000 liters/hour, and 2
pumps with capacity between 30,000 and 100,000 I/hr., in the catchment.
36.   Apparently there are no agricultural drainage systems in the micro-catchment.
Farmao
37.   Only 70% of farmers devoted fill time to their farms, the remainder having off-fam
occupations in addition to farm work. On the other hand, only 30% of wives worked full
time on the farms, as did sons and daughters. Farm family members provided 75% of all
farm labor. Hired labor was entirely male, the majority over 35 years of age. The
implications are that off-farm income is important for maintaining a sufficient standard of
living by many farm families in the catchment.
Farm Income
38.   The farm models indicate that in 1990 farm incomes were low: on a 9.3 ha
grape/root-crop farm, using traditional technologies, the net margin was estimated at
US$3,166 per year, or US$341.5 per ha; on a 12.8 ha fruit/vegetable farm the net margin
was estimated at US$4,011, or US$313.3 per ha; on a 7.6 ha vine farm, the figures are
US$720 or US$94.7 per ha; and for a 60 ha dairy farm the figures are US$4,044, or US$67
per ha.
Farlmrs' Involvement in Organizations
39.  It is reported that in the micro-catchment there has been an increase in participation
between 1990 and 1992 in SALUCAL, the local agricultural cooperative concerned with
grapes and horticuluWal production. In 1992, it appears that 30% of farmers belong to some



- 62 -                             Annex B
producer group - 80% of these belong to CONAPROLE, the national dairy cooperative.
Some 55% apparently do not belong to any farmers' organization, and only 5% belong to
Savings and Credit cooperatives.
40.   Of those not yet belonging, 35% were interested in belonging to a producers'
cooperative, 10% to a general agricultural cooperative and 10% to farmers groups. 40% of
farmers said they wanted advice and technical assistance, 10% wanted credit, and 45% were
uncertain what type of service they would like.
C. MAIN PROBLEMS AND LIMITATIO_NS
Low Net Farm Income
41.   One of the key problems affecting vegetable, fruit and milk producers within the
Santa Lucia River Basin is their need for increased productivity and income, while improving
and stabilizing the ongoing water and soil degradation process. A second issue related to
farm income is the horticulture and fiuit producers' need for improved quality of their
produce in accordance with export market demands. In addition, production and income
stability for milk producers in particular are closely tied to their ability to improve
productivity of their currently degraded winter pastures. Financial incentives would have to
be introduced if natural resource management/conservation practices are to be implemented
and preserved on a long-term basis.
Natural Resource Degradation
42.   Under the soil, climate and land management conditions which characterize the area
of micro-catchments within the Santa Lucia River Basin, a key technical problem is believed
to be that of poor soil structure of the A horizon. This physical constraint is probably caused
by compaction derived from excessive grazing and use of heavy equipment under wet
conditions. Compaction of the surface few mms. of soil alone has been worsened by very
intense rainfall impact on uncovered and tilled soils.
43.   Compaction affects (a) the quality of the soil as a rooting environment (with particular
respect to aeration, to adequate storage and availability of soil moisture during rainless
periods, and to retention and release of plant nutrients); and (b) runoff and consequent soil
erosion (with particular respect to loss of potential soil moiste, to removal of soil
materials, to quality of water in streams and rivers, and to severity of floods downstm).
Excessive runoff and soil loss by erosion have been consequences of structural degradation
rather than a cause of it.
44.   A key problem in the general areas represented by the micro-catchments is how to
intensify production and avoid soil compaction derived from heavy machinery utilization.



- 63 -                             Annex B
D. PROPOSED DEVELOPMENT STRATEGY
45.   Continuing degradation of soil resources will lead simultously to increasing costs
and to declining yields. On smaller and less efficient farms, incomes may decline to such
low levels that the farms are sold or abandoned, having litfle residual productive value.
46.   One of the program's cbjectives is to develop a set of recommendations leading to
appropriate management and conservation of land resources, thus generating sustainable
improved standards of living of rural families in the Santa Lucia catchment.
47.   The proposed program would have three main characteristics:
(a)   Take a multi-disciplinary approach to farm management. This would involve
recommendations for improving net farm income while making the neessary
provisions for naural resource conservation;
(b)   Collaborate with the farmers in an iterative process of validation and
refnement of the technical recommendations being made; and
(c) Impiiove farm-families' understanding of processes involved in sustanable use
of the natural resource base.
The Four Micro-catchments as Agppled Research Areas
48.  It is envisaged that the micro-catchments selected within the Santa Lucia River Basin
will initially in fact be effective Applied Research areas, rather than pure demonstation
areas, since there are as yet many uncertaintes about how farmers are going to react to the
recommendations which the project staff may make. Some suggested improvements are new
and untested: e.g. re-aligmnent of fences to re-divide farms into areas which are
homogeneous with respect to land use capability. Other techniques, such as contoured grass
strips, while being known to be technically effective, may not be acceptable in economic
terms either as a fodder resource by farmers with animals or as a soil and water conservation
technique by those without animals. Yet again, other soil conservation practices have been
recommended in Uruguay in the past but with very limited adoption: e.g. contour banks, or
using land strictly according to its capability.
Farmers as Partners in Testin and Validation
49. In such a process of validation it is hoped that the fanrers in the micro-catchments
may be seen as equal partners with the specialists and advisory staff in searching for, tesig
and validating improved practices which will have sustainable benefits, rather than merely as
"target groups" to which recommendations will be directd. In this context, the importance
of developing two-way communication and feedback, in both directions, among farmers,
advisers and reserhers cannot be too strongly emphasized. The proposed project should
give careful consideraton to the best means of achieving this dynamic interaction.



- 64 -                              Annex B
Actions Proposed
50. Grape-growers. Quality improvenment is proposed to be achieved by the partial and
gradual replacement of current varieties with those of better wine-making characteristics,
suitable for processing and sale at better prices by commercial enterprises. It is also
suggested to introduce high-quality varieties of tradable grapes for sale on both the local and
the export markets. Irrigation is proposed for this enterprise.
51.   Growers of fruits and vegetables. Greater specialization in fruit production is
proposed. Emphasis will be placed on peaches, which have good market prospects in the
region, and for which both the technology and production costs are suitable for those not yet
very familiar with fruit production. It is proposed that 50% of the planted area should go
under localized irrigation. Growing of vegetables would be maintained, with increasing
emphasis on onions and garlic, much of it under sprinkler irrigation.
52.   Small-farm milk producers. It is proposed - for those with more than 30 ha of land -
that higher stocl ng rates and production per animal should be sachieved by increasing the
proportions of planted pasture and annual forage, and planting two ha of maize with furrow
irrigation. Better use of long-term pastures with summer crops and short-term planted
pasures will be encouraged. Maize, in substitution for sorghum, would be irrigated to add
to the available fodder. Milk production from less than 30 ha of land is considered
uneconomic, and present producers on these sub-economic farms will be encouraged to go
into improved production of fruits and vegetables.
53.   LaMe-farm milk oroducers. To allow increases in stocking rate and higher
production per animal, it is proposed that an increased proportion of the extensive areas of
natural grazing should be brought into rotations of planted pastures with winter crops and
maize. The latter would be produced under irrigation.
54.   Farm planning. It will be recommended to producers of crops and pastures, either
separately or in combination, that the most efficient use of their farms' soil resources can be
achieved by planning and managing the farms' uses according to a priori assessments of land
capability and suitability for proposed enterprises.
55.  Improved management practices. Complementing current conventional production
practices, improvements and additions would be recommended which improve the
conservation-effectiveness of current practices, as well as providing more specialized
conservation measures to act as "back-ups" against irregular climatic events - particularly
rainstorms - of excessive intensities.
56.  Imroved management practices. Grass strips would be recommended between rows
of perennial fruits for soil surface protection and for maintenance of water infiltration
capacity under rainfall and irrigation via soil structure maintenance and improvement.
Contour-planting would be recommended for both new plantings and the associated inter-row
grass strips. With incorporation of legumes in the mixtures (where appropriate) free addition
of nitrogen would improve growth of both the crop and the grass strips.



- 65 -                              Annex B
57.   Contoured grass strips would also be recommended where appropriate on cropping
land on all farm types, where according to width and spacing would act solely as sediment-
filters and runoff-infiltration strips or additionally as contoured segments of strip-crop
rotations.
58.   Regular additions of organic materials and associated plant nutrients would be
recommended via ploughing-in of green-manure/cover crops once every four years on farms
with vines, fruits and/or vegetables. Soil recuperation in systems for producing annual fruits
and vegetables will be emphasized with the use of 2-year breaks under alfalfa and possibly
other species.
59.   For both recuperation and maintenance of soil structure, and addition of recycled
nutrient materials, application to fields and orchards of organic manures (e.g., cattle dung
and slurry, other organic materials and composts), will be recommended for all production
systems.
60. The maintenance of soil structure will be emphasized with the formal rotation of
crops and planted pastures. For degraded pastures the cropping break permits soil loosening
and weed control, while for degraded cropped soils the pasture break, if sufficiently long and
effective, restores collapsed soil structure.
61.  In addition to recommendations for contour-tillage and contour-pltnting of both annual
and perennial crops on the contour, recommendations (preferably within the context of such
plans) would include also: the layouts of soil- and water conserving measures such as
contoured grass strips (already mentioned), contoured conservation banks ("terraces"), bench-
terraces, and properly managed grassed waterways for safe disposal of runoff. The plans
would provide recommendations for conservation-effective alignment of access routes,
measures for gully control, and for water conservation ponds and, at communal level, of
dams. Recommendations for improvements in fencing would relate to both delineation of
homogeneous areas for inclusion or exclusion of grazing animals and to protection of dam
walls and areas immediately upstream of tanks and dams to minimize degradation of water
quality. The associated provision of drinking points for cattle at predetermined sites
throughout the pastures would assist in pasture management and the minimization of
damaging concentrations of cattle.
62.  In collaboration with research and experienced farmers, recommendations will be
provided on systems of reduced tillage methods with a view to both reduction of costs,
reduction of damage to soil structure, maintenance of organic matter levels, and maintenance
of suitable porosity for infiltration of water from rain and irrigation.



- 67 -                            Annex C
MRUGMAY
NATURAL RESOURCES MANAGEMENT AND IRRIGATION
DEVLOPMET PRCT
DEMONSTRATION FARMS
1    Program Objective
(a)  to introduce soil management and conservation technological packages, adapted
to the five main agro-ecological regions of the country;
(b)  to select production systems that are economically feasible and environmentally
sound;
(c)  to recover lost fertility in the majority of soils under agricultural use;
(d)   to improve the efficiency of tilling in relation to soil characteristics and to
control/eliminate weeds that affect artificial pastures; and
(e)   to apply and develop production systems that improve the productive level of
degraded soils.
2.    Selection Criteria of Action Zones. The main criteria would be soil types,
vegetative, and topographic differences identified in the Soil Survey Chart of Uruguay. The
different zones in the country have their own characteristics regarding soils, vegetation and
climate which would determine the optimal management strategy adapted to such
characteristics. The selection criteria would also take into consideration the need to have
duly represented those areas of traditional intensive agricultural use, affected by different
degrees of erosion.
DiaMosis of Selected Areas Situation
3.    Natural Resources. Consistent with the general criteria for area selection, four units
of soils representing the different edaphologic conditions of the country have been identified.
The selected soil units are CHAPICUY, YOUNG, CANADA NIETO and IIBERTAD. The
total area of the selected units represents 6.36% of the national territory (1,119,360 ha),
representing 29.5% of the country when referred to the total potential agricultural land
(excluding rice planting areas). Based on the information contained in Uruguay's Soil
Classification Map, these are the main characteristics of the zones where the demonstration
farms will be:
(a)   Chapieny Unit: The soils within this unit, covering an area of 300,000 ha
(1.7% of the country), mainly cover the department of Paysandd. These are



- 68 -                             Annex C
soils formed over sandy clay and sandy sediments from cretaceous formations.
The landscape consists of strong and soft hills with sedimentary knolls and
associated bluffs. Vegetable cover is thick, with a predominance of summer
species and parks of thorn and carob trees in the plains associated with
drainage works.
Prevailing soils: Typical Brnmosoles Subeiutricos (litvicos) ArFr/Ar and Typical
Argisoles Subeiliticos Ocricos Ar.
Soil aptitude: winter and summer crops.
Main limitations are: (a) hydric erosion risk; (b) degradation of physical
properties, and prior erosion; and (c) fertility losses.
(b)   Young Unit: The soils of this unit, over an area of approximately 242,000 ha
(1.38% of the country), cover the departments of Paysandd and Rio Negro.
These are soils developed over medium texture quaternary sediments covering
the Fray Bentos Formation. The surface is characterized by soft and strong
hills. Vegetation consists of predominandy winter pasture with thick cover.
Prevailing soils: 7ypical Brwwsoles Eutricos Francos (ArAc).
Soil aptitude: winter and summer crops.
Main limitations are: (a) erosion risk; and (b) fertility losses.
(c)   Caflada Nieto Unit: Covering an area of about 131,800 ha (0.75% of the
country) this soil unit appears in the departments of Soriano and Colonia.
Soils are developed on sandy clay sediments related to the Fray Bentos
Formation, and limy clay sediments of the Libertad Formation. The surface
consists of hiUls and knolls with mixed pasture vegetation associated to park
forests. Prevailing soils are moderately deep typical Brunosoles SubOutricos
ArFr.
Soil aptitude: winter and summer crops.
Limitations: (a) erosion risk; and (b) fertility losses.
(d)   Libertad Unit: (Ombdes de Lavalle and Tarariras Regions). This unit is
found in the departments of Soriano, Colonia and San Jos6. The unit has an
area of 118,700 ha (0.67% of the country). Soils are developed over Libertad
Formation sediments. Surface consists of soft hills with large valleys and
associated high plains. Vegetation is winter/summer pasture with thick cover.
Prevailing soils are Brunosoles Eutricos/typical SuNutricos/L(*vicos LILAc.
Soil aptitude and limitations: These are soils with a high productive potential,
very apt for winter and summer crops. Their main limitation is excess
humidity in winter due to drainage problems associated with heavy textures.



- 69 -                              Annex C
Phsent Use of the Land
4.    The most important characteristic affecting land use in Uruguay is the reduction of
condmtous agriculture, replaced by agricultural crops which started the introduction of
pasture rotation. Therefore, land use is more directed towards livestock and dairy
production, with a reduction of the area devoted to winter and summer crops. As to the
Southern units, such as Libertad, there is an increase in production of annual fodder and
pasur  associated with milk production.
Identifcati  of Problems and their Possible Causes
5.    The prncipal problems affecting the physical properties and fertility of soils in the
selected areas are the following:
(a)   soil erosion evidenced by lost horizons and the formation of gullies, registered
at national level specially in the areas of more intensive agricultural use;
(b)   soil degradation with negative effects on structure, organic matter and fertility,
worsened by problems of compactness and crusting; this is a complex process
with an interrelationship of properties which change the soil hydric behavior
and its subsequent management; and
(c)   in many cases the degradation and erosion problem is accompanied by weed
invasion hard to control (Bermuda grass, thistles, etc.) which generally begin
to thrive when soil productivity decreases, making it more difficult yet to get
these areas back on production.
6.    According to diagnostic studies carried out in different areas of the country, the main
causes for the stated phenomena are:
(a)   unawareness on the part of the producer that erosion may cause serious
deterioration to the use and potential productvity of agricultural land under
production. In general, when the producer discovers the damage there is little
he can do to reverse the situation;
(b)   exhaustive use of the soil, without restoring or preventing the drop in
productive potential;
(c)   the sharecropper system has had a great incidence in soil degradation, although
presently the portion of agriculture under this system has decreased; besides,
today leaseholders are more controlled by agricultural credit supervision; and
(d)   in general, it was concluded that a high proportion of agricultural producers
have modernized their equipment and improved their technological level in
pest control and phytosanitation, as well as their use of modern varieties. In
spite of this, farmers in general have not yet advanced in adopting sound soil
management pracdces aiming at improving productivity within a framework to
preserve its properties and characteristics in the long term.



-70 -                               Annex C
nuagement practices aiming at improving productivity within a framework to
preserve its properties and characteristics in the long term.
7.    During the 1960's, efforts were made to promote the adoption of conservation
techniques inspired by the American systems of broad-base terraces. However, most of the
terraces built were abandoned, and erosion problems were worsened by the lack of
mnaintenance.
8.    CIAAB experiences in rotation trials and production systems replicated by La
Estanzuela in different areas of the country, provided the groundwork to incorporate local
experiences and rotation trials with improved pastures. Likewise, research trials in run-off
plots carried out in Aguas Blancas (D.S.y A.) and La Estanzuela (CIAAB), showed the
effects of appropriate tilling and rotation systems in reducing soil losses.
9.    Efforts have been made to include conservation measures in investment plans to be
financed with credit lines from BROU. These promoted vertical tilling tools, and the
dissemination of reduced tilling systems and some experiences with direct sowing carried out
by La Estanzuela and private organizations such as AUSID. Although these soil
conservation practices and methods have been introduced at different levels, in general they
have been characterized by the lack of sufficient applied research support.
Analysis and Conclusions of Succsses and Failures
10. It is evident that technology transfer programs applied in the past fell short of
expectations. It is also a reality that there has been a lack of continuity on the part of the
Ministry of Agriculture, Livestock and Fishery in focusing on natural resource conservation
as the mainseam of sustainable agricultural growth. According to the successful experience
of leading producers in different areas of the country, a group of practices may be defined
whose success in erosion control has been proven in the field. These practices must be
supervised and adapted to the particular situation by the consulting agriculturist. These
practices include:
(a)   contour tilling;
(b) spacious pasture-covered drainage;
(c)   elimination of disk plows and disk harrows;
(d) hierarchization of vertical tilling in accordance with soil characteristics;
(e)   reduction of tilling; and
(f)   planned crop and pasture rotations.

Organization and Operation of Demonstration Farns
S1ci op                . The selection of demonstration farms will be carried out through
public bidding among interested parties. A selection committee will be established, made up
by DGRNR and the Projects Executing Unit (UEP). The farm selection criteria and the
subsequent contractual arrangement with the farm owner will conform to the following
conditions:
12.   Producer and Far  Charactics. Preference will be given to producers
satisfying one or more of the following characteristics:
(a)   between 25 and 55 years of age;
(b)      direct responsibility in decision-making regarding daily management of the
farm;



- 72 -                             Annex C
(c)   income must come mostly from the agricultural activity;
(d)   producers residing in their farm; and
(e)   producers already keeping physical production and econonic registers of their
enterprises.
13.   Farm charac i:
(a)   Tenure. The producer must have the tenure of the farm confirmed for a
period of not less than six years, in order to accomplish the whole
development program.
(b)   Production system and minimum equipment. The potential agricultural area of
the farm should not be less than 50%. The model of present production
should include crops and yields representative of the area soils. With the
purpose of implementing specific soil management and conservation practices,
the selected farms must have tractors with 70 HP as a minimum, in addition to
the other implements normally required for tilling and sowing operations.
(c)   Borrowmna. The total debt of the producer (physical or juridical person) at the
time of presenting his proposal (principal plus earned interest) may not be
more than 25% of farm total equity, excluding land value and improvements,
and the payment schedule should not exceed 20% of the farm's annual gross
income at present production levels.
(d)   Location. To be able to fulfill the proposed demonstration purposes, the farm
must be located on a national highway, or a departmental or local road
accessible any time of the year.
14.   Documents to be submitted by applicants of the demonstration farms:
(a)   application form;
(b)   notary public certificate indicating the farm area and the juridical relationship
maintained with the property owner. If a leaseholder, copy of the lease
contract must be attached;
(c)   sworn affidavit of the producer's equity position on date of application;
(d)   copy of the sworn statement before DICOSE as of June 30, 1989;
(e)   CONEAT document with CONEAT Index and Real Value Index;
(t)   aerial photo on a 1:20,000 scale;
(g)   photocopy of owner's ID or that of the administrator in the case of a
corporation, as stated in point 5.1.7;



-73-                               Annex C
(h)   last Capital Tax payment receipt or negative;
(i)   last IRA or IMAGRO payment receipt;
(j)   last Social Security Bank payment receipt;
(k)   last Real Estate Tax payment receipt;
(1)   certificates on the farm and the owner(s) for:
(i) inhibitions record; and
(ii) mortgage;
(m)   sworn affidavit of the owner(s) about other incomes aside from those coming
from the farm; and
(n)   at the time of submitting the proposal, the owner, or administrator in the case
of companies, must present his Civic Document with evidence of having voted
or other evidence in substitution thereof, or payment receipt for the
corresponding fine.
15. Terms of the Agreement. The agreement will be signed between the owner of the
selected demonstraon farm and the UEP on behalf of MGAP. The agreement will define
each party's responsibilities and obligations, directed to guarantee the fulfillment of the
project objectives, and would include in addition to the terms and conditions set forth in
Section II of Schedule 6 to the Loan Agreement, the following:
(a) The General Directorate of National Resources (DGRN) within MGAP
assumes the obligation of delivering to the demonstration farm in consignment,
through the UEP, the set of goods determined in the Plan for the Development
of Demonstration Farms, to be used for demonstration purposes in soil
management and conservation practices. In general, the project will not
finance traction and harvesting machinery nor any equipment used in cattle
raising activities;
(b)   The project will finance the total cost of the specialized technical assistance, in
addition to the incremental cost of the consulting agriculturist of the farm up to
a maximum of 150 days/year;
(c)   The goods provided by the project will complement the machinery of the
demonstration farm and may include the following items: conservatonist tilling
(chisel-subsoiler plow, vertical tilling harrows), stubble chopper, special
planters (direct sowing), soil levelling, and layout and constructon of
stucus for erosion control (reversible plows, scrapers, etc.);



- 74-                             AnnxC
(d)   After the activities to be carried out in the demonstration farm in question
(farm subproject) have been finished, MGAP, if so decided, may transfer to
the demonstration farm the residual value of the acquired mnachinery;
(e)   Acquisition of the goods and the hiring of the consulting services must be done
in accordance with the conditions stated in the Loan Agreement signed
between the Bank and the Government of Uruguay;
(f)   If the Bank and the Government of Uruguay, jointly or unilaterally, decide to
cancel the disbursements under the project, or the Bank decides to suspend
disbursements under the project, the UEP and the DGRN on behalf of MGAP
will not assume any obligation for expenses either made or commited by the
producer to carry out the Development Program, whether they be included in
the UEP capital contributions or those of the producer himself;
(g)   The UEP will provide technical assistance jointly and in coordination with the
technicians responsible for the preparation and fulfillment of the Development
Plan; and
(h)   The UEP will carry out a semiannual evaluation of the Program development
and a final evaluation on its completion.
16.   Oblilons of the demonstration farm owner. Under the agreement, demonstration
farm owners would be committed to:
(a)   carry out a Development Program included in the Annex to the Services
Agreement and according to the stated timetable;
(b)   carry out the management and operations of the farm according to the
Development Program;
(c)   rely on the technical assistance of a duly qualified agriculturist for the duration
of the Agreement;
(d)   carry out the repairs and maintenance of the goods delivered in consignment
by DGRN;
(e)   comply with the responsibilities regarding sanitary, taxation and administrative
matters, and those of any other nature required by law and regulations;
(f)   keep in separate accounts all operations carried out to fulfill the Development
Program in accordance with UEP's instructions;
(g)   make available to the UEP all the technical and economic information
necessary for a correct evaluation of the Program development; and
(h) allow access to technicians, producers and any other persons appointed by the
UEP for demonstration events and/or to observe the Program results, and

carry out the required field experiences for their evaluation, as well as any
other scientific activities. These activities will be coordinated between the

results of the program for training and technological dissemination as it may
deem convenient.
17.   Resonble paries. Each party will appoint two persons who will be the
representatives of each party in the Services Agreement. Any changes would be notfied to
the other party in advance.
18.   Puration. Duration of this agreement will be five (5) years from the date it is
signed.
19.   Non.funlflent. Excepting a case of force majeure, failure of compliance by any
party of any of the obligations stated in the contract, will give the other party the right to
rescind it. If the producer has failed to comply with any of his obligations under the
Services Agreement, the producer will have to reimburse to MGAP the amounts received, its
value updated according to the consumer price index variation plus a 6% (six per cent)
annual interest earned from the date the amounts were delivered, as provided for in Decree
Law NO 14,500 of March 8, 1976. If the MGAP fails to comply with any of its obligations
under the Services Agreement, it will have to indemnify the producer for any damages he
may have suffered.
20. Changgs. For justified reasons from a technical or economic point of view, the
Contact and Program may be modified by agreement between the parties, prior approval by
the IBRD.
21.   Sbei domidles and notfcatdons. The parties establish as special domiciles for all
effects derived from the contract, those mentioned in the appearance to sign it, and they
accept as valid all notifications made by collated telegram to such addresses.

23.   Second priority tasks will-be:

characteristics;
(b)   fertility recovery (correction of nutrient level, specially phosphorus at a
minimum of 8 ppm, and correction of soil acidity where necessary); and
(c)   weeds eradication and control.
24.   Tentatve mans for demonstration farms management will be as follows:
(a)   Farm in Chapicuy: In general, annual crops are restricted to old pastures
which are renewed through agricultural crops. Technological level goes from
improved to high. As a complement, the Development Plan will include the
increase of fodder production and its utilization through direct grazing, silos
and haying, increasing, in this way, meat and wool production. The
Development Plan will be characterized by:
(i) Types of rotations: It is estimated, 2 to 3 years of pastures, followed
by two agricultural crops (one summer and one winter crop);
(ii) Soil management: Management oriented to minimize tilling and to the
utilization of tools contributing to the recovery of physical properties
and to simplify tilling (reversible plow, chisel-subsoiler,
vibrocultivator, etc.). Elimination of disk harrows and disc plows;
(iii) Recovery tasks: Levelling of cultivated fields to eliminate microrelief
and hills. Recovery of soil fertility levels; and
(iv)  Systematization: Parallel and level bands systems accompanied by
alternate crops. In situations of greater risk, the system of temporary
terraces may be used.
(b)   Farm in Young: According to the soil aptitude, the plan for this farm will be
based on increased grain production in the anmnal agricultural area, moving the
technological level from improved to high. The agricultural area will be
increased through a sequence of well planned crops. In addition, emphasis
will be given to increased fodder production, diversifying its utilization
through silos, hay and direct grazing, according to the needs and tim of the
year. The Development Plan will include:
(i)  Soil management characterized by:
a.   tendency to minimum tilling;
b.    efficient use of equipment;
c.    recovery of soil physical properties;



- 77 -                              Annex C
d.    use of direct tilling, in principle on the second crop in a double-
crop rotation; and
e.    elimination of disk harrows and disk plows.
(ii) Recovery tasks which will focus on:
a.    levelling of cultivated fields and elimination of microrelief;
b.    gradual eradication of weeds; and
c     recovery of fertility levels.
{iii) Systematization which will include:
a.    parallel and level bands systems;
b. alternate crops;
c.    special emphasis on the correct and ample design of pasture-
covered drainage; and
d.    eventual use of temporary terraces for the areas of greater
erosion risk.
(c)   Fanm of Cafnada Nieto: Medium rotations are being planned, with two years
of crops and three years of pastures. To the extent that weed problems created
by Bermuda grass are controlled, the tendency would be to extend the
rotations. The Development Plan will include:
(i)  Conservation systems of very wide pastre-covered bands are planned,
with careful management to avoid weed dissemination. It will be
complemented with a strict design of ample and pasture-covered drains.
Levelling of cultivated fields for microrelief elimination;
(ii) Soil management practices to reduce tilling and introduce direct sowing
will be organized. Strategic tlling with reversible plow as a means to
fight Bermuda grass will be applied. Summer crops will be managed in
alternate bands, and the same for the combination of long and short
cycle (late) winter fodder crops. In the case of high erosion risk, the
system of temporary terraces and wide bands will be used; and
(ii) Recovery tasks will include levelling of cultivated fields, Bermuda
grass control and fertility recovery.
(d)   Farm of Ombdes de Lavalle: It is planned to substantially increase grain and
fodder production. Enhance the technological level from moderate to high,
and the use of irrigation in the production of maize and fine seeds. The
Development Plan will include:
(i) Type of rotation: short rotation of grain crops (maize and barley), and
production of legume fodder and seeds;



- 78 -                               Annex C
(ii) Soil managemnt: There is a tendency to minimize tilling and perform
direct sowing. Every now and then and according to the evoluuion of
physical properties, the green mass will be buried, for instance, red
clover;
(iii) Recovery tasks: Levelling of cultivated fields, weed eradication and
fertility recovery; and
(iv) Svstematization: Since this is a low erosion risk area, it will be
sufficient to use pasture-covered bands.



-79 -                              Annex D
NATURAL RESOURCES MANAGEMENT AND IRRIGATION
DEVELOPEN PROJECT
APPLIED RESEARCH PROGRAM
A. INTRODUCTION
1.    The objective of PRENADER's research component is to support the existing
research lines and create a new technical basis for their transfer to the agricultural sector,
thus obtaining the development of a conservationist agriculture in rainfed areas as well as in
land under irrigation. Due to the fact that rainfed production prevails in most areas under
cultivation in Uruguay, modern methods are not applied in the use and management of soils
and irrigation water, with the exception of rice and sugar cane. Similarly, traditional
extensive agriculture (including vegetable and fruit production) has not prioritized soil
conservation and producivity despite the technical and political efforts made in that
direction.
2.    To prepare the present Project Research Program, a collection was made of the
technical proposals from the principal institutions capable of fulfilling the roles of applied
research and technology transfer, which were grouped in the following subjects:
(a)   Soil Management and Iripgation Research;
(b)   Soil Management and Soil Conservation Research;
(c)   Training; and
(d)   Technology Tranfer.
B. RESEARCH iNSTITUTIONS
School of Agronomy
3.    The School of Agronomy integrates the series of Schools of the University of the
Republic. It is a public organization with autonomous administration, teaching being its main
objective. Its activity is national in scope. Presently it has research agreements with other
public institutions and with private enterprises. Its main headquarters for teaching and
research are located in the city of Montevideo. It has a network of research and teaching
experimental stations in the departments of PaysandO, Salto and Cerro Largo. An
experimena area may soon be established in the locality of Joanic6, departme  of



- 80-                              Annex D
Canelones. uis organizational chart includes a Soils and Waters Area, integrated with
disciplines coordinated with teaching and research in both specialties.
National Institute for Ariculturad Research tINIA)
4.    The National Institute for Agricultural Research (Instituto Nacional de Investigaci6n
Agropecuaria - INIA) is the main agricultural research center in the country. It is a semi-
private institution, with Government representatives and producers' associations. Its
administration includes rules for domestic and external non-governmental relationships.
5.   Its principal duties are the generation and transfer of agricultural technologies. It
receives technological proposals from the Regional Advisory Councils (Consejos Asesores
Regionales - CAR), from the regional Working Groups, from the policy outlines of its Board
of Directors and from its technical staff. Within its organization, the Areas and Programs
sector has the responsibility for planning, follow-up and evaluation of research. It supports
other sectors in the survey of proposal and execution of projects.
6.    Its technical-financial organization is made up of the National Directorate, the
Administration and Finance Management and the Regional Directors. The latter have direct
responsibility in project execution at their respective experimental stations.
7.    It has an Information and Dissemination Management Unit acting as an advisory unit
to the National Directorate, with the responsibility of programming, executing, following up
and evaluating technology transfer. INIA has five Experimental Stations in the departments
of Canelones, Colonia, Salto, Tacuaremb6 and Treinta y Tres.
8.   In addition to its own projects, it participates in research projects with other public
and private institutions, with resources that represent 10% of its annual budget. It is called
the Agricultural Technology Promotion Fund (Fondo de Promoci6n de Tecnologia
Agropecuaria).
Soils and Waters Directorate (DSA) of MGAP
9.    The Soils and Waters Directorate (Direcci6n de Suelos y Aguas - DSA) is an agency
of MGAP's National Directorate of Renewable Natural Resources (Direcci6n Nacional de
Recursos Naturales Renovables). DSA has research agreements with public and private
institutions. It has Regional Offices in the departments of Paysandd, Salto and Tacuaremb6.
Uruguavan No Illage Association (AUSID)
10. AUSID (Asociaci6n Uruguaya Pro Siembra Directa) is a private organization for
research and transfer, established in June 1991. Its duties are experimentation and
technology transfer on soil management oriented to clarify the advantages and disadvantages
of sowing without cultivation. Their members explain that the establishment of AUSID was
prompted by displeasure over the poor technical assistance from other research institutions on
the subject of direct sowing. AUSID headquarters are located near the city of Mercedes, in
the department of Soriano.



- 81 -                             Annex D
C. llGE!RLQ
11.   Uruguay trains agricultural technicians at two levels: university professional level, and
technical or semi-technical level from other non-professional training institutions such as
Universidad del Trabajo del Uruguay (UTU), belonging to the Ministry of Culture, and one
or two private institutions.
12.   On the subject of irrigation, the School of Agronomy offers a very general and not
too intensive education program. The level is even lower in the other institutions. One of
the UTU schools in the city of Mercedes offers a non-professional short course on surface
irrigation.
13.   The private companies selling irrigation equipment normally offer technical support in
design preparation. However, they do not participate in the use of water nor in the
evaluation of the initial work conditions of the equipment after its installation in terms of
efficient and uniform application of the water-bearing stratum. The problem worsens when
the fertilization-irrigation systems are used with the resulting inadequate distribution of
nutrients.
14.   As a consequence of the foregoing, the economic evaluations on the use of irrigation
are not clear. While in some cases overirrigation occurs thus increasing costs and in many
instances reducing production due to an excess of water, on other occasions during critical
periods the sources of water are insufficient or the systems undersized. Even companies with
agriculturists as technicians and permanent advisers, also face the same previously described
problems. In the area of soil management and conservation, the situation is similar to that in
irrigation. The soil, this badly used natural resource, shows its gradual and less noticeable
degradation at producer level.
15.   Training is deficient and the technical advisers show lack of conviction in the
technological application of irrigation and soil management. C. Victora (see Annex) shows
how the technical advances in soil conservation start in 1940 with Ing. C. Finn, as soon as
he airives from the United States, at the time when terracing was being applied in that
country; in 1970 Ing. E. Marchesi, also coming from the United States, introduces the
system of belt contours; and finally Ings. R. Puentes y F. Garcia, returning from their
graduation in the USA introduce with great energy the covered soil system and minimum
working of the soil.
Rsearch Program Objective
16.   The program would help to generate and adapt soil and water
management/conservation techniques, providing the technical groundwork for a coherent
natural resource management strategy in Uruguay.



- 82 -                            Annex D
D. TECHNOLOGY TRANSFER
17. Within the public sector, the enterprises with technology transfer responsibilities in
Uruguay are MGAP, INIA, School of Agronomy, AUSID. Other private companies looking
after communities with specific crops also have an important role in technology generation.
The private independent technicians in agricultural extension cover a great variety of
activities and techniques. Technical advisers of private producing companies handle
management related aspects and not always do their objectives adequately weigh the
importance of natural resource conservation.
18.   The possibility of improving natural resource management as an integral part of
agricultural production programs in Uruguay will depend to a Large extent on the level of
support to specific tmnsfer projects of the already established institutions, and the
government's support will create a favorable climate for the development of new private
consultancies in soil and irrigation management.
E. USE OF-THE PROGRAM's FUNDS
19.   The purpose of the Applied Research Program is to serve as an instrument to support
technologies adapted to the new demands of the private agricultural sector. In the preceding
context, in order to achieve the fulfillment of their role, the research and the technology
tansfer units must possess at least the same direction and speed established by the demand.
The Program's available funds must meet the demand of the research and transfer institutions
in order to make possible the achievement of their technological objectives.
F. RESEARCH PROJECTS
Prioritizaton of Submitted Projects
20.   The Technical Team for Program Preparation received an orientaton regardig
prioritization of the research projects submitted to be executed in the 6 years of the Program.
The Soils and Waters Directorate and the FAO/World Bank Technical Team partcipated in
determining the priorities. During project preparation, 40 research proposals were selected
according to farmers' needs and the country's overall natural resource conservation strategy.

or generated by the research. It will quantify the level of

and those of productivity related to the more resticting
factors, or to a management necessarily more efficient of
the farms selected for the evaluation.

H. TECHNOLOGY TRANSFER
24. Technology Transfer activities will include:
(a)   meetings with Working Groups. Working Groups act as advisers to the
Regional Advising Councils (CAR), take note of progress achieved and
propose research actions at the project level for the mid and short term
programs;
(b)   meetings with regional technicians. This is an activity oriented to technical
up-dating with presentation and discussion of results;
(c) meetings with regional technicians. These are meetings in which generally
producers and technicians participate, reviewing progress achieved and visits
to places where tests are being performed or of interest regarding development
of the technology under investigation;
(d)   written dissemination. INIA disseminates written information at several levels,
ftom collectible loose information sheets to technical-scientific publications. It
covers practically all training levels required by the technological acceptance.
Each Experimental Station has the equipment for less formal written
dissemination to assure a faster treatment of the subject with the producer and
the technician requesting such information;
(e) oral and televised dissemination. INIA participates in radio and TV
dissemination with different subjects of interest. Normally each rtsearch stage
is registered on video-tape for its later massive dissemination in specifically
contracted for radio and TV space; and
(f)   publications and documents. INIA renders a dissemination service of
international research documents to regional technicians. The infstructure is
made up of a communication system via PC, CAB Abstracts, CRIS,
AGRICOLA, Current Content and other subscriptions to publications of
greater interest to each experimental station.
25.   The School of Agronomy has in its role the basic component of knovwledge transfer
for a modern educational background in national research. It is expected to disseminate the
resuts of the different applied research subprojects through technical notes, information
bulledts, summaries of thesis and reports on research progress. Another transfer mechanism
will be through Technical Field Symposiums, Seminars and Exhibitions on Research Results.
26.   AUSID will prepare research progress reports, that will be submitted to members of
the Association and the general public. It will publish partal and final research results.
AUSID will be the seat for School of Agronomy students preparing their thesis on research
rearding soil management m sowing with reduced soil working. The basic transfer method
will be through extension and technical updating symposiums and meetings.



-94 -                              Annex D
27.   The Soils and Waters Directorate has a system of publications to disseminate
information. Technical updating meetings tabe place all over the county. Through
PRENADER specific projects will be developed in the areas of farm, agriculture, irrigation,
etc. While applied investigation is the technological support of project development, the
research institutions may prioritize summoning the organizations executing the projects on the
subjecXt of dissemination and transfer.
28.  It is proposed that the UEP establish as a requisite to the participating research
institutions in the stage of definite formulation of their respective projects, a comnmitment to
prioritize calling or convening the executors of productive projects to technical meetings,
semnars, etc. and to the distribution of publications related to research projects integrating
the Research Components Program. It will also be established for new projects and
institutions whose results, in opinion of the IJEP, will be of national benefit. Without
excluding the possibility that a given research project may be exclusive of the agriculuural
enterprise developing it, only the UEP will have access to the follow-up without its results
becoming public domain.
