A. BACKGROUND: PRESEN1 SITUATION OF MAIN GOVERNMENT
INSTITUTIONS AND UNIVESITY DEPARTMENTS
INVOLVED IN GIS9-RELTED ACT
1. There are about one million cadastral parcels in Uruguay of which about 200,000 are
in the rural areas. The information about the rural area parcels are already entered into a
computer database at CONEAT. The information about the remaining 800,000 urban area
parcels awe available on microfiche.
Available MaMs
2.    All the cadastal maps at 1:20,000 scale for the whole country have already been
completed. They consist of 1200 map sheets. The maps are in Gauss-Kruger Projection.
Only about 20 sheets were digitized to carry out some tests. About 30-70 m. gaps were
found between the sheets. It is believed that the sheets have about 50-70 m absolute error.
This error can be fixed, because the triangulation network is already in place.
Military Geographic Service (Servicdo Geofrfico Militar)
Abfale -MaD
3.    The Military Geographic Service within the Ministry of Defense has completed about
3/4 of the 1:50,000 and all of the 1:100,000 topographic maps. It also has 14 LANDSAT
photographs covering the whole country, although digital imagery has not been processed.
Directorate of SoiLs and Water (Direcci6n de Suelos y Aguas)
4.    The following Soil maps were digitized:
(a)   Using ILWIS: Northern zone of Uruguay (Departments of Artigas, Salto and
part of Paysand, Rivera, Cerro largo and Tacuaremb6) at 1:1.000.000 scale,
carried out as part of the SOTER Project with ISRIC.



-96-                               Annex E
(b)   Using IDRISI: Soriano and Colonia Departments at 1:1.000.000 scale within
the framework of INIA Project.
(c)   Southern part of San Jos6 Department at 1:100.000 scale as an ILWIS taining
exercise.
Available Maps
5.    The DSA has prepared soil maps at 1:1,000,000 scale for the whole country and at
1:100,000 scale covering about 15% of the country, mostly in the south, plus many thematic
maps mostly at 1:1,000,000 scale.
Directorate of Forery (DMeccd4n Forestal)
6.    Several forest maps have been made so far. The first one, which was published in
1980, is at 1:200,000 scale and is based on 1:40,000 scale aerial photographs taken during
1966-1967. On this map, new plantations were plotted manually. It contned tiree classes:
natural forest, artificial forest and no forest.
7.    Another map for the whole country was made at 1:100,000 scale in 1987 by visual
interpretation of satellite photographs. Most of the work was done at INPE in Brazil. The
classes were: Pine, eucalyptus, natual forest and no forest.
8.    A third forest map was made in 1989, mainly by updating the 1987 map, again by
visual classification of satellite imagery with the participation of Arianna Sorrentino and
Maiz De Los Angeles from the Agricultural University.
CONEAT (Comisi6n Nacional de Estudios Agroecondnmcos de ia nerra)
9.    Within CONEAT, the commission responsible for agro-economic surveys in MGAP,
there is an Information Systems Unit.
!hXaboas
10. The Information System Unit of CONEAT has a tabular database, which contains a
large amount of information about the rural cadastal units in Uruguay. For each unit (parcel)
the database contains the following information:
(a)   Cadastral unit mmber;
(b)   Surfice area;
(c)   Percent areas of soil types covering the unit;



- 97 -                             Annex E
(d) Productivity index (an index also referred to as CONEAT index indicating
productivity of the unit based on the surface area and percentages of its soil
cover, etc.); and
(e)   Owner's name, etc.
11.   When requested by the owners of a rural cadastral unit, CONEAT prepares a report
which contains anl the information about the unit, a description of its different type of soils,
and a sketch of the field which is manually prepared from the available charts described in
item below.
Available MaMs
12.   There are about 1200 cadastral charts at 1:20,000 scale which were prepared by the
Directorate of Cadastre. In addition to cadastral information they also contain soils
information provided by the Directorate of Soil and Water (Direcci6n de Suelos y Aguas),
MGAP. The soils information were obtained by interpretation of aerial photographs at
1:40,000 scale. These charts are available only in hardcopy, that is not digitized and not
linked to the tabular database electronically.
National Directorate of TonotEaphv (Direcci6n Nacional de ToNoqrafa)
Hardwre and Software
13. The Directorate of Topography in the Ministry of Transport and Public Works
(MTOP) has at present a DECstation 5000/125. Additionally, they have 4 Digitizing table, a
Hewlett-Packard 8 pen plotter, and a Laser printer.
14.   The software that they are currently using includes a Multiuser workstation, a
database management software, and PATHWORKS software for connection of PCs to
DECstation. They plan to digitize all the 1:50,000 topographic maps prepared by the
Military Geographic Service.
Diectoate of Hydrographv (Direcci6n de Hidrogandf
Database
15.   The DNH has a tabular database stored under RDB, which contains the data
periodically obtained from the 200 meurement stations located along the steams. The
stations measure both the level of water and the speed of flow, which are used to calculate
amount of discharge. The measurements are made 2-3 times a day by automatic data loggers
and transmitted by portable equipment. For some parts of the country, the data is available
since the beginning of the Century. At present, the total amount of data is about 450 MB.



- 98 -                            Annex E
16.   This database is used mainly for making projections of the amount of available water
and plan water allocations to the users. The database is also used by other agencies for
designing dams, bridges, and irrigation systems.
17.  Using a graphics software available on the PC terminal, they often do evaluations of
changes in the measured parameters within a given period, comparisons between stations for
simultaously measured parameters and preparation of frequency curves.
18.   The only maps which are available are those indicating the locations of measurement
stations.
B. ANALYSIS OF THE PRESENT SITUATION
Available MaMs
19.   As indicated in the previous section, topographic and cadastma maps are available at
the scales needed. In fact, the Directorate of Topography plans to digiize 1:50,000 scale
maps without the elevation contour lines. Topography maps at 1:1,000,000 scale, including
rather coarse contour lines (every 300 m), will be soon available in digital form at FAO GIS
Center for all of South America and can be provided when needed.
20. Most of the thematic maps needed are already available, at least at 1,000,000 scale,
but need to be digitized. Moreover, it would probably be necessary to digitize 1:100,000
scale topo maps including the contour lines of the areas where the future projects will be
implemented.
A^ddit,iona MMp Required
21.   Since the cost of making new maps is rather high, no new maps should be made
unless needed for a specific application. For making maps like temperature and rainfall
isolines, the data needed are already available at the Meteorology Institute in the form of
point measurements. Most GIS software is capable of making isolines maps using such point
data. Therefore, such maps could be made after the GIS becomes operational. This
technique is not limited to meteorological maps only but can be applied to any type of isoline
map using point data.
22.   One thematic map that is currently needed is a land-use map which can probably be
made for most of the country using the infmation available from satellite imagery. It
appears that two sets of LANDSAT photographs are available at the Military Geographic
Service and the Directorate of Forestry. It may not be possible to make the land-use map by
visual interpretation of the satellite photographs, especially if a large number of classes need
to be distinguished. Therefore it would definitely help to supplement visual interpretation
with digital image analysis in any case. A few digital LANDSAT scenes are available
already. For improving the classification accuracy, it may be necessary to use digital images
obtained in two different seasons. Unuguay is covered by 14 LANDSAT scenes. Therefore,
28 images would be needed if the whole country is to be covered for two different seasons.



-99-                               Anmex E
The actual number needed would probably be less than that, because in some areas seasonal
vaiations may not be important or quarter images may be sufficient. The PC ERDAS VGA
image analysis system available in the MHELUP has limited capabilities and could not be
used for processing a large number of images. Despite this, upgrading this system to a
workstation version or acquiring additional satellite imagery is not being processed at this
time, for the following reasons:
(a)   The staff of the Mistry of Housing and Environment have not yet evaluated
the available satellite photographs and the digital data. They should first find
out what can be done with them and detennine what is really needed at least in
terms of additional data. Also, it seems that they have not evaluated the
satellite imagery classification work done for making the forest maps either;
and
(b)   Upgrade of ERDAS software to the workstation version and the satellite data
even for 14 scenes would cost a large fraction of the total CGIS budget. It is
not certain that capabilities provided by them can be used effectively during
the first phase. However, this upgrade should definitely be considered for a
second phase of the GIS program.
Available Iardware
23. Directorate of Topography is setting up a large and modem GIS faciilty. The
Computer Center at the University of the Republic also has modern computer facilities
altbough not exacdy geared for GIS. The Directorate of Cadastre has two new powerful
VAX computers but they are not in use yet. Moreover, this Directorate is using only one of
three PC ARC/INFO systems they have for lack of qualified staff. The VAX system
available in the Directorate of Hydrography is an outdated system and needs to be upgraded.
Other available systems are small PC based systems.
Availble Software
24. Several institutions already have ARC/INFO. However, they all have only the basic
module of the software. No institution has the GRID, TIN, NEIWORK and the COGO
modules. The Directorate of Topography is the only instittion with a multiuser workstation
level ARC/INFO. There are also 9 other PC level ARC/INFO at four institutions: 4 copies
at the Directorate of and Topography, 3 copies at the Directorate of Cadastre and one copy
each at the Ministry of Housing and Enmironment and the Surveying Department of the
Republic University.
25.   The Computer Center of the University has the GRASS software on a workstation.
This is a raster type GIS software available in the public domain from several institutions in
the U.S. at a nominal fee.
26.  ILWIS raster PC GIS software was acWired by two institutions in March 1992,
namely by the Directorate of Soil and Water and the Directrate of Foresty. The former
also has another raster software for the PC called IDRISI.



-100-                               Annex E
27.  It is estimated that almost all of the GIS applications that have been done so far are
limited to some attempts to experiment with the system and to do training. Only the
Directorate of Cadaste has digitized about 20 maps and even there the only work that has
been done with the digitized data is checking for map positional errors. The real power of
GIS is its capability to overlay maps and carry out modelling studies. However, during
project preparation it did not appear that such studies have been done in any of the GIS sites
so far. The Directorate of Topography plans to digitize all the 1:50,000 topographic maps,
which is a substantial task, but their system was not operational yet.
Avalabl Staff and Const_ants for the Development of a GIS
28.   There ar only a few government officials with some training or experience in GIS.
In act, some institutions like the Directorate of Cadastre is not able to use most of the
available equipment, for lack of qualified staff. Experience of most of the staff is limited to
some training projects on PC systems. However, there are a large number of staff with
sufficient background in computers and related areas such as remote sensing, some of which
have already d3ne some GIS work with the PC GIS available. Consequently they are well
aware of the limitations of their systems. Similarly, they are likely to know their
requirements for furher training. This staff can be easily tained to form the core GIS staff
needed. It should be noted that the Directorate of Topography has already started to train
several staff in ARC/INFO and other software- needed for operation of a GIS.
C. A POSSIBLE STRATEGY FOR TIE DEVELOPMENT OF A GIS FOR
NATURAL RESOURCES MNGMN
Appkicaton of GIS in the Mana _ment of Natral Reources
29. Management of natural resources requires the collection, analysis and dissemination of
massive amounts of different kinds of information: information about topography, land-use,
soils, water, climate, vegetation, animals and infrastructure; figures on population, income,
health and nutrition, just to name a few of the many types of information involved.
30.  In general, this information is in one of two forms: attribute data (e.g. statistics,
numerical or texual information) contained in tables, lists, catalogues, etc.; and geographical
information, contained in various Idnds of maps.
31. The resources planner's problem is how to compare and combine selected information
from these two kInds of data base, in order to relate a given geographic location to its
specific attnbutes (such as slope, soil type, vegetative cover type, etc.) or to assess the status
of one particular area in relation to other areas, (which of two areas would be more suitable
for a given crop, for example).
32.   Computer technology has long since made it possible to handle and analyze statistical
infrmation, but only recenly has technology been developed which can conveat maps into a
computer-usable digital format and allow the simultaneous operation of both the geographic



-101-                               AnnxE
spatial data and related attribute data. This now makes it possible for the resource planner to
tapidly prouce a combination of maps and tables that show "were gand "what".
33.   The ultimate use of GIS lies in its capability for modelling: constuctin models of tbe
real world from digital data bases, and usg tese models to simulate the effect of a specific
process over time for a given scenario. Modelling is a powerful tool for analyzng trends and
identing factors that affect them, or for displaying the posslble consequences of planning
decisions or projects that affect resource use and manugement.
Cenkwhomn of the Databases and Xt  G   Falith
34.   The Directorate of l[opogrphy ad the Diretorate of Cadstre already have
ARC/INFO based GIS and the capacities of their systems are probably sufficient for the next
sever years. Although their activities are not directly related to management (if naural
resources, the maps they digitize can be used for that purpose.
35.   The instittons which are directly related to management of natural resources are:
(a)   Diretorate of Forestry in MGAP;
(b)   Directorate of Soils and Water in MC'AP;
(c) Directorate of Hydrography in MTOP; and
(d)   Land-Use Plaming Unit in the Ministry of Housing.
36.   These institudons have only limited GIS facilities. Therefore, the project would
prov  the hardware/software facilites they need and also provide support to train their
staff. A Cental GIS (CGIS) would be set up for these four institions. The CGIS would be
managed, operatd and used by the staff of these institutions.
37.   The project would also assist:
(a) the Directorate of Topography within MTOP in upgrading the outdated VAX
II computer they have. This system cannot be maintained any longer and its
failure can cause serious problems. The cost of this upgrading has been
etimtd at about US$40,000; and
(b) the Directorate of Cadastre (decentralize institution) in putting the two unused
VAX computers and two PC ARC/INFO systems to use by providing taining
to their staff.
The Rationale for Cealizng GIS F4i0lbt  and   ties
38.   Most decisions affecting natural resource mnagement are iery cross sectoral.
They require trade-offs between sometimes conflicting sets of priorities. The use of ntural
resurces should not only be economically efficient but also sustainable. Therefore,
rsource-use planning has to integrate information from many sources such as diffrerent types



-1 O2 -                             Annx E
of maps and statistical tables. This integated approach has to be implented staing from
strategic planning at the national level down to the departent or even project level. The
flow of information should be not only between fthese te levels, but also between different
sectors.
39.   This requireent of the integration of multsectoral information is the best
justification for centralizing GIS databases and activities. Only a ctal database, where all
the data are integrated in the same strucure, format and the coordinte system, can
efficiendy serve the needs of moCern auto,nated resource use planning.
40.   Other advantages of centraizing the GIS activides and databases could be sunmard
as follows:
(a)   Improvement of the communication between the sectors, decision making
institutions and the institutions which are responsible for making maps or
collecting statistical data.
(b)   Sharing expensive equipment. If the facilites are decentralized, then each unit
woud need its own tape drive, plotters, digitizers, high capacity disks,
prnters, etc. Sharing these equipment would reduce the costs.
(c)   Sharing systems and database management and pm ing staff. These are
highly techimcal staff and cannot be trained easily. Each GIS site requires at
least one such staff. Therefore, centlizing the GIS facilities would reduce the
number of such staff needed.
41.   Most of the argumems against centalizing a GIS are related to administrative matters
involving decisions regarding the location of the GIS, the resonsibility for its manageme
and development, and the allocation of the various workstations and the digitzers between
different insiutions. However, these potenal problems do not outweigh the advantages of a
centalized GIS.
42.   As a result of the analysis carried out during project preparation, it is ommnded
that the CGIS be located at the Directorate of Soil and Water witin MOAP, given their level
of specialization in mapping techniques. However, this is a subject to be analyzed further
(dunng annual and mid-term reviews) in the light of Govermment strateg regardwg GIS
operators and users.
43.   The CGIS would be developed in two stages. During the first two-year phase, basi
hardware/software and trainiDg would be acquited and the available 1:1,000,000 scale maps
would be digitized. Also modalides of data exchange with Directorates of Topography,
Cadastre, Meteorology and CONEAT would be explored and some pilot projects would be
completed. At the end of the first phase the needs of CGIS would be evaluated in terms of
new maps required and new hardware and softwar to be acquired. Additionally, the
evaluation would determine the need for furher training and techimcal assistance for:
(a)   Makig  new maps;



- 103 -                             AnnexE
(b)   acquiring new hardware/software;
(c)   futher training of the users; and
(d)   futher consulacy.
On the basis of tWs evaluation, the activities to be carried out during the second phase,
which would take two years, would be defined.
eletbion of the CGIS Softw
44.   At FAO GIS Center the main GIS software is ARC/INFO Other software used in
FAO are PAMAP, ILWIS, IDRISI and TYDAC/SPANS and ERDAS. Among these
ARC/INFO is the most comprehensive and is available almost on aU hardware platforms. But
it is also the most difficult one to learn and use. PAMAP is also a very good software and
available mainly on UNIX platforms bcaue  they discontinued upgrading the VAX version).
SPANS used to be arailable only on PCs. Now it is available also on IBM 6000 and some
other UNIX workstations. However, its digitizing and plotting capabilities of the previous
version had some limitations. ILWIS and IDRISI are available only on PCs. IDRISI and
ERDAS are image procesing software rather tman GIS because they camot be linked to a
database.
45.   Even though the selection of any major software is a complicated and highly techical
task, this is not the case for the GIS software for the centralized system in Uruguay. The
recommendation has been to use ARC/INFO for the following reasons:
(a) It can meet the functional requirements of the project;
(b) It is the most widely used GIS software in Uruguay already, therefore, many
persons are already familiar with it;
(c)   Despite what the software companies say, data exchange between different
types of GIS is a difficult and time consuming problem, not to mention that it
often degrades the data. Therefore, there are many advantages to havmg the
COIS software compatible with the GIS software used at the Directorates of
Topography and the Cadastre, which are both ARC/INFO; and
(d) It is the main GIS software used at the FAO GIS Center at HQ and the FAO
Regional office for Latin America and the Caribbean.
46. Concerning the potential difficulties of learning ARC/INFO, these have been largely
overcome by the innovations introduced by its author, ESRI, to the latest version (ver. 6.1).
These innovations include:
(a)   a sophisticated dashboard type menu system;
(b)   an entrely new raster component called GRID;



- 104 -                            Annex E
(c)   an easy to use inquiry module called ARCVIEW; and
(d)   a tutorial software for teaching ARC/INFO.
47. Moreover, the Directorate of Topography has already started a training program in
ARC/INFO. Therefore there will be at least a few experts in government offices soon.
48.   Selection of ARC/INFO for the CGIS would not imply elimination of other software
available e.g. ILWIS, IDRISI in the govermment offices. These software can still be used to
complement ARC/INFO.
Selection of the CGIS Hardware
49.   FAO experts have recommended a RISC/UNIX type workstation as the main
computer for the CGIS. According to experts, the PC version of ARC/INFO has many
limitations compared to the workstation version and is quite slow when processing large
maps. Secondly, the TIN and GRID modules of ARC/INFO are not available on PCs yet;
and finally, workstations provide much better graphics capabilities, such as the X-windows
which is required by some ARC/INFO modules.
S0.   Among the various workstations available, the project should consider one which is
compatible with the latest version of ARC/INFO.
51.   Non-RISC and non-UNIX type workstations are not considered here because of the
higher performance/price ratios of the RISCIUNEI machines and the recent trends in GIS
software which highly favor the latter.

F. ORGANZATION AND MANAGEMENT OF THE GIS
76.    The CGIS Would be managed by a board which would meet about every six  monts.
Members of the board would be appointed by the CGIS user institutions.
77.    The CGIS should have the following staff:
78.    Manager. This is a part-time post, which would be filled by one of the senior staff
members of the four insttutions using the CGIS. The Manager does not need to have
raining in computer science, but he/she would have to have a background in natural
resources. The Manager would report to the CGIS board. The post can be held on a
rotation basis by the staff of the four institutions. During the development phase the manager
would be assisted by the consultants hired.
79.      yms Analyst. Would be responsible for the technical maners related to operation
of the CGIS and would provide technical support, as well as training on the use of the
systm, to the application staff. The person responsible would have a background in
computer science and would be fhmiliar with the operating system software of the
workstion. He/she would report to the CGIS Manager. If not initially familiarized with
ARC/INFO, he/she would be trained by the project.



- 110 -                             Annex E
80.   Because of lack of staff, the post of the System Analyst could not be filled by the
staff of any of the four user institutions at the moment. However, the possibility of hiring
one of the staff of the University on a half-time basis for at least one year should be
considered.
81.   Database Manager. Would be responsible for development and maintenance of the
database. He should have a background in natural resource management, but would be
trained in ARC/INFO along with the applications staff.
82.   Applications Staff. They are the main users of the CGIS. They would apply GIS
techniques to the solution of natural resources management problems. These staff would be
selected from the GIS or Information System staff of the four user institutions and the project
would provide the training needed by them. They would act as liaison officers between the
CGIS and their respective institutions. Initially, only one staff member would be chosen
ftom each of the institutions, to reduce the office space requirements at the CGIS, although
more than one should attend the training courses. Applications staff would be trained to
digitize their maps if a digitizing operator is not available.
83.   stemdigitizin          . This is a general service type post. The operator would
assist the Systems Analyst in the day to day operation of the system and in making data
backups. He/she would also assist the application staff in digitizing maps. This is not an
essential post and its duties can be split between the Applications staff and the Systems
Analyst.
84.   The CGIS Manager and the Applications staff would be identified and appointed as
soon as the CGIS is approved by all parties. This staff would keep their current posts at
their respective institutions.

URUGUAY
NATURAL RESOURCES MANAGEMENT AND IRRIGATION
DEVE LOPMENT PROJECT
FORESTRY DEVELOPMENT SUPPORT COMPONENT
I. EXECUTIVE SUMMARY
1.    The Forestry Directorate intends to develop a 5-year program that would continue the
activities of the forestry component of Loan 3131-UR and that would include complementary
activities aimed at addressing the needs of a rapidly growing forestry sector. Implementation
of the technical assistance. training and institutional strengthening components progressed
well, partally due to FAO's management of the funds. Therefore, in this follow-up project,
that would follow along the Imes of the original project, would aim to complete the technical
assistance, training and institutional strengthening activities during the early phase of the
follow-up project, followed by the implementation of the applied research and
complimentary studies, as described below.
treugthening the Forestr Directorae
2.    The proposed institutional strengthening activities would involve a taining program
for the Forestry Directorate (FD) technical staff within MGAP, which would include 10
scholarships of up to six months each to improve expertise in Forestry, Pests and Diseases,
Forestry Management, Forest Economics, Timber Technology, and Forest Mensuraton, and
10 short-term scholarships (up to three months each) in complementary areas. The project
woud also provide:
(a) Technical equipment ar-d construction needs for the Toledo Technical Center
(specialized laboratonies, training center, germ plasm bank) and for the
Forestry Directorate regional offices and Montevideo headquarters;
(b)   Internaional Technical Assistance to strengthen the Forestry Directorate
technical units and the applied research program. This would involve 16 man-
months of select international consultants in areas such as Forest Grazing,
Forest Inventories, Pests and Diseases, Forest Fires, Forest Industries,
Genetics and Improvement, Land Reclamation and Environment Inpact; and
(c) National Technical Assistance to act as the counterpart of the international
consultants and to support the regular program of the Forestry Directorate and
the Project itself. For this purpose, 9 specialties with a total of 16 person-
months and 5 one-year scholarships would be provided during the length of the
project.



-112 -                             Annex F
SVrengthen  of the Ap-Iled Research Prorm
3.    This activity aims to provide the technical information needed to facilitate the
decision maldng process of the private sector and of the Forestry Directorate. Research
would be implemented by national and interational research agencies following the
adminisrative procedures set out by the first project. The main research areas would include
yield assessment with permanent plots, pests and diseases control, agro-sylvo-pastoral
systems, weed control, and timber technology and tansport.
TeWnical Assistance for Private Investors

(a)   Short-duration (i.e, 15 day) taining tours for 31 mid-level supervisors and

small-scale industrial units, wood harvesting techniques, and wood
preservation and drying.
(b) Overseas training of 25 specialized workers and 7 training courses in various
technical areas of the timber industry.
(c)   Local training by international instuctors giving short courses (5-6 days) for

inventories, forest evaluation, quality control, industial safety, mechanized
timber farming, pruning and tinning.

and assistance of private missions coming from abroad. While only project costs are
inclhded in the cost tables, additional resources would also be provided by the private sector.

Pject

would therefore be the national counterpart agency. An expert with international experience
will serve as the principal technical administr of the project.
I. INTRODUAICION AND BACKGROUND
8.   In 1990, under the World Bank's Second Agricultural Development Project, an
instittional strenging activity was initiated to help promote the establishment of a
critical mass of tree plantations in the country. That project (Loan 313 1-UR) was designed



- 113-                              Annex F
to: (a) assist forest owners to export forest products; (b) initiate the financial incentive
scheme provided in the forestry law; and (c) provide credit assistance for plantation
establishment and for the development of small industries. The project was also designed to
help facilitate costs of plantation establishment and improve the sector's technical and
economic understanding through technical assistance, training and applied research.
9.    The current administration formulated a forest development strategy that includes a
target to establish 200,000 ha of plantations in 5 years. The incentives provided for in the
current legislation-i.e., partial reimbursement of reforestation costs, tax and fiscal
incentives, two promotional credit lines, and a reforestation investment mechanism through
debt conversion-also became effective.
10.   The technical assistance, traing and technological transfer activities of
Loan 3131-UR got started through a project co-financed with UNDP and executed by FAO.
Since the beginning of this project, private investor response has been very positive: the area
planted annually has been increased as anticipated, from an historical average of 2,000 ha/yr
to 8,000 ha in 1990 and 10,000 ha in 1991. The estimates for 1992 and beyond appear to
confirm an increasing trend in activity. At the same time, the industrial sector has been
stimulated by the creation of a consortium of sawn timber exporters, the opening of new
markets for domestic sawn wood and logs, and the establishent of new industries.
11.   The Bank Project sponsored intational and national consulta to prepare research
programs, establish infonnation systems, produce technical discussion papers and manuals,
and participate in technology transfer seminars. The project also sponsored training in the
form of 29 scholarships for Umguayan professionals and 13 training courses for 500
workers. Under the project, the FD has acquired needed vehicles and other technical and
office equipment.
12.   After 26 months of effectiveness, roughly the entire program has been completed.
Those targets for research and training activities associated with wood processing and
industrialization were not completed due to the lack of trained specialists. Because of their
importance, these research and training activities will be carnied out through this follow-up
project.
13.   With the incentives and credit facilWies provided for by the Forestry Law, there has
been increased interest in the establishment of new plantations and in forestry sector
investments in general. To maintain its role as promoter and supporter of forestry
development, the Government must contnue to obtain more information on the economics of
forest farming and on wood processing technology, as well as on the real possibility of future
markets for wood and some of its byproducts. The Forestry Directorate must also improve
its capacity, including the regional offices to support this rapidly expanding sector and the
technical assistance demands made upon it. This accelerated development pace in the
forestry sector also makes it essential that training, technology generation and transfer,
research and dissemination of information be contued and expanded.
14.   With the termination of the UNDP/FAO Project in December 1992, and with it the
training, technology transfer and technical assistance activities sponsored by Project 3131,
the Government approved a new budget provision under which UNDP contributes



- 114 -                            Annex F
US$70,000 to allow for the continuation of the technical assistance, training and
dissemination activities for a period of 6 months, starting January 1993.
15.   Other essential forestry development activities, not included in project 3131-UR
would also need to be included in a follow-up project. Some of these activities correspond to
a contnuation of those being carried out to date under the Project, and new ones which begin
to appear as a consequence of the increased level of development of the forestry sector.
16.   The original Bank project comprised three basic lines of activities: (a) Institutional
Strengthening of the Forestry Directorate, (b) Private Sector Promotion and Assistance, and
(c) Applied Forestry Research. The continuation project proposes a group of activities
along similar lines to those of the first project, with the conviction that they lie within the
public sector jurisdiction for facilitating the development of the forestry sector.
mI. PROGRAM ACTIV1     UNDER THE -NATURAL RESOURE
MM-AGNfLENTE ANIRG~ATION DEVELOPNMENT PRJ_ECT
A. Strengthening the Foresty Directorate
17.   Under the first Bank Loan (3131-UR), the Forestry Directorate was highly successful
at attracting private investment in the private sector. Foreign promotional efforts were also
successful at attracting productive investments to Uruguay through the foreign debt
capitalization mechanism. Specifically, five reforestation projects to plant 60,000 ha at a
total cost of US$48.5 million-of which US$23.5 are covered through the exchange of
external debt securities for Treasy Bonds or BCU debt instruments-were approved in
1991. But because of this success, the demands for technical assistance from the Forestry
Directorate has increased enormously. A group of well-trained and properly equipped
professionals, with access to information sources, are needed to answer the technical
questions and to fulfill the duties that the Forestry Law requires of the Fortry Directorate
for this dynamic sector.
Tchnical EAuipment for the Foresry Directorate
18.   The project would provide for technical equipment for the Toledo Technical Center,
the regional offices and the Montevideo headquarters, as follows:
(a)   Toledo Technical Center. MGAP's Toledo Technical Center is located 18
km from Montevideo (Alejandro Gallinal Nursery). The Forestry Directorate
manages 50 ha and the following activities: forest seed distribution,
germplasm production, cold storage, and forest pests program. A technical
development plan has been outlined aimed at strengthening this program and to
improve the delivery of services to the Private Sector and carry out joint
activities for genetic improvement (seed bed gardens) and applied silviculture
with the National Institute for Agricultural Research (INIA). The project
would provide for a laboratory for seed analysis and certification, a forest
entomology and pathology lab, a training center, and experimental field



-115 -                              Annc F
equipment and material. Because this Center is an adequate place to offer
specialized training courses and demonstrations, technical workshops and field
symposiums at a lower cost and with greater facilities than those in other
places, it would be equipped for training. A total of US$158,450 has been
estimated to support the Toledo Technical Center.
(b) Strengthening of Regional Offices. With the increasing demands placed on
the Forest Directorate, the present regional offices, which stared operating in
February 1992, would need to be strengthened to cover the region (which
includes an area of 70,000 km2, one million ha of priority forest area, and a
radius of 250 km from Tacuaremb6). In particular, strengthening is needed
for technicians and equipment for the offices already established (Young -
Tacuaremb6 - Montevideo), thus avoiding the need to open new Regional
Offices. The Project would reiforce equipment for mobilization,
dissemination of information, audio-visual aids, processing and fles, library,
communications and technical equipment for on site measurement, and offices,
for an estimated total cost of US$60,000.
(c) Main Headquarters In Montevideo. In accordance with the Forestry Law,
and in consideration of the rapid increase in area planted (8,500 ha regisred
in the last season) in all parts of the country, a systemized national registry is
needed. The existing data bank does not satisfy the planning and decision
maaing needs of both the Public and Private Sector. To meet these needs, a
National Forest Inventory Office would be established to register and map the
locations and basic information (volumes, ages, growths and yields by species,
etc.) of plantations. The Project would support this Office through the
provision of initial basic equipment. The central headquarters would also be
equipped for extension and promodon activities (seminars, workshops, field
symposiums, talks) aimed at the private sector. Accordingly, costs of some
US$54,000 haver been estimated for the cental headquarters.
In total, the cost for equipping the Forestry Directorate is estimated at US$272,450, as
detailed in Table 1.

B.  9rengthin the Applied Parcb Program
26.   Uruguay is far from having sufficient information on applied technologies for site
specific production and planting systems, plantation management, wood processing, and
transportation and marketing. And the technological advances that have been made are not
reWadily available to the public. The objectives of the Applied Research Program therfore
are to: provide the technical information needed for the decision making process of the
Directorate itself, assist the private sector with key production information and build a data
bank to alow for technical improvements, both at the level of forest farming and at the wood
utilization level. In general, this Program intends to provide a readily accessible database,
capable of delivering locally verified information and rtco endations, thus avoiding
unproven "foreign prescriptions" as much as possible.
27.   The first project under Loan 3131-UR initiated a series of silvicultural reseach
initiatives. Currently, ten species selection and production trials are being implemented with
the participation of local research agencies. The PD has prioritized lines of work where the
epected information may be useful to the greatest mnmber of users and in the minimum
possible time, where possible using participation and cooperation mecbanisms with the direct
beneficiaries of the infbrmation (i.e., private sector). For this, a close Forestry Directorate:
Private Sector working relationship was initiated (as summarized in the following table), and
which will be strengthened by the continuation project.
28.   Division of Responsibilities for Rsearch Activities:
Directorate of Forestry                   Private Sector

- 118-                              Annex F
29.   In addition, the project would include a series of studies (product transportation,
forestry directorate and markets) designed to support the research program. This would also
include research activities related to permanent plots, pests and diseases in Pines and
Eucalyptus, drying of sawed eucalyptus and pine lumber, frost resistance in eucalyptus
varieties, effect of plantations on soil, and support of graduation thesis in the University of
Uruguay School of Agronomy. National capacity would be sought for the implementation of
these activities, however, the bidding would also be opened for international experts. The
total cost of this activity is estimated at US$175,000.
C. Technical AssIance for the Producer Sector
30.   During the development of the first project, Tecbnical Assistance activities to the
private sector focussed on the grouping of companies for commercial goals (e.g., creation of
an export consortium) or as producer groups with shared management, once the development
stage was achieved. Service companies (production of plants, planting services, fire control,
germplasm center) were also strengthened, visits abroad were organized, and field days and
technical seminars were conducted. These activities were designed to support the training
needs of mid-level leaders in the primary industrial sector, transfer technologies and search
for external and complementary supports. But the private forestry sector is made up of
actors with very different characteristics and needs. While some international companies are
setting up operations using high technology, the majority of operations are medium and small
size entrepreneurs just stardng this activity and others who historically have worked towards
the local market only. These operations are now faced with the need to incorporate new
technologies that would make them competitive with international markets. The continuation
project would provide the needed technical support to these evolving forestry enterprises,
under a cost sharing arrangement.
31.   Specifically, the project would support:
(a) Training of mid-level swpervisors and technicians. This would involve study
tours (of approximately 15 days) organized and scheduled by the project in
coordination with local companies and host enterprises (e.g., Wood
Corporation of Chile, Wood Industrialists of South Africa, Brazil, etc). The
project would organize the study program in coordination with the host
enterprises and would finance the travel expenses. The Uruguayan company
would pay per diem (including health insurance and contingencies) of the
trainee during the stay abroad. The project would train 31 participants at an
estimated cost of US$11,600. Training subjects would include: operation of
medium sized sawmills, harvest management, drying of sawn timber,
chipping, box and pallet plants, and other medium and small industrial
processes;
(b)   Technical training of select manpower. The project would support the
technological upgrading of industial units through the training of operators.
Specific technical skills would be acquired in specialized training centers.
Seven training courses of a duration of about 40 to 60 hours each would be



-119 -                               Annex F
provided, at an approximate cost of US$28,300. The Uruguayan company
would cover the expenses for the travel and per diem costs. Training would
involve bandsaw use, crosscut saw use, sharpening and maintenance
workshops, dryer operation and maintenance, and operation of planers;
(c)   Training of personnel in Uruguay. It is expected that some training activities
will be conducted in-country, with the assistance of international instructors.
This would be short- term courses (5-6 days each) in subjects (e.g., aerial
photography for forest inventories, forest inventory, quality control in forest
products, industrial safety, chain saws operation, and pruning and thinning
techniques) of interest to professionals, mid-level technicians and workers
directly involved with the producer sector. An attendance of 8-10 persons per
course is expected. The project would finance the participation of the
instructor, help with the production of teaching material, and the coordination
and administration of the course. Producer groups or companies would fund
transportation and per diem for their participants. The cost of this activity is
estimated at US$20,000;
(d)   Study Tours. The project would sponsor study tours to familiarize producers
with the experience in other countries, facilitate contacts with similar
enterprises, and facilitate the participation in specialized seminars, exhibitions
and equipment shows. A total cost (if US$34,000 is estimated for this activity,
which includes 2 industry shows, 4 specialized seminars and 2 scheduled trips;
(e)   Extension aid Dissemination. The project would support the preparation of
technical publications and the dissemination of technical manuals, booklets and
leaflets, prepared by the Technical Units of the FD. These publications would
provide instructions on current technologies useful to producers, technicians
and professionals in the sector. Material related to pine, eucalyptus and poplar
silviculture, wood technologies, products markets, ferdlizers, pest control, fire
prevention, and industrial safety would be prepared and disseminated. The
periodical "Uruguay Forestal' would also be strengthened and videos and
audiovisual aids designed to promote domestic and foreign investments. The
total esfimated cost for this activity is US$50,000;
(f)   Local Seminars with Aericultural Producers. The project would sponsor the
preparation and coordination of reforestation seminars for traditional
agricultural producers, designed to interest them in forestry-for-profit activities
on their land. Through seminars, round table discussions and workshops, a
substantial number of landowners and livestock owners in each Department
would be provided with information on: the Forestry Law, characteristics of
available credit, international forest products market, and rate of reurn on
forest investments. The project would provide the relevant written material,
audio-visual aids, transportation and per diem allowance for international
personnel. There would be at least one meeting per Department dunng the
life of the Project. The estimated cost of this activity is US$19,000; and



-120 -                             Annex P
(g) Private missions from abroad. Along with the growth of the Uruguay forest
sector, its promotion abroad and PD's attendance at several international
forums, there has been a rapid increase in the number of technical and
commercial missions from abroad who wish to be informed on what is going
on in the country. In most cases, the FD acts as host and, because this
responsibility is expected to increase, then will be an increasing need to hire
services, prepare briefing kits and provide ad-hoc counterparts. At an
estimated cost of US$10,000, the project would support this activity.
D. Promotion Abroad of the ForestR Sector
32.   A balanced development of the Forestry Sector requires a promotional effort to
interest foreign companies to invest in plantations and industries and to purchase Uruguayan
forestry products. To facilitate this process, the project would contract specialists to:
(a)  identify key countries to receive advertisement information;
(b)   prepare a marketing strategy for key counties;
(c)   prepare meetings and promotional materials; and
(d) train FD staff on public relations methods.
33.   A consultant would evaluate the situation in different countries and identfy those
having a nartual financial or market advantage for making joint investments. Based on the
results of tis first stage, a markedng strategy would be developed to reach each of these
markets. For this, promotional materials and audiovisual aids would be prpared, and
seminars and meemngs would be coordinated. The project would also sponsor
communication skills training for staff of the FD, the Foreign Service and the Foreign Trade
office (of the Ministry of Foreign Affairs). This acdvity is estimated to cost US$120,000.
n. PRQJECR MANAGEMENT
34.   This 5-year program would be implemented by the FD, which would nominate a
national coordinator. A senior technical advisor would be contracted for the duration of the
project to help the PD National Coordinator manage the technical and financial aspects of the
program. The current agreements with the School of Agronomy (URU) and the National
Forestry Corporation (Chile) would be continued and strengthened, both of which have
proven to be very effective. Likewise, similar mechanisms of cooperation should be sougbht
to incorporate other national and interational institutions (e.g., IBDF/Brazil, SF/Canada,
SF/South Africa, ICONA/Spain, INIA-LAT/Uruguay, and IF/SERCOTEC/CIDERE/Chile)
qualified and willing to assist the project.

URUGUAt
NATURAM RESOURCES MANAGEMENT AND IRRIGATION
DEVELOPIMENT PROJECT
SELECTION CRIERIA FOR I&D SURPROJECTS
A. GENERAL FEATU
1.    The proposed Natural Resources Management and Irrigation Development Program
would finance: (i) minor I&D works (i.e., with an investment under US$250,000), and (ii)
medium size I&D works (with an investment above $250,000), up to an aggregate amount of
about US$12.7 million equivalent.
2.    The present project manual refers to the selection criteria to be applied to those
subprojects conaining construcon and/or rehabilitation of minor and medium size civil
works (SAR paras. 2.26-2.29), thus:
(a)   Selection of subprojects with access to pre-investment funds to finance
feasibility studies. Said studies will be a pre-requisite to possible financing of
subproject design and execution; and
(b)   Selection of subprojects which would involve the constuction of new or the
improvement and rehabilitation of existing I&D infrastructre, and whose
design and execution will be financed with funds from the Natural Resources
Management and Irrigation Development Project (PRENADER).
3.    All I&D subprojects would be prioritized according to the potential beneficiaries'
interest and their commitment to have an active participation, even cofinancing, in the
execution of the studies and the construction of I&D infrastructure.
4.    The Project Executing Unit (UEP) will prepare and standardize the necessary
background information of all subprojects meeting the application requisites, to be submitted
to the Intministeral Coordinating Committee (CCI) for further consideration by this body.
Based on the selection criteria described in the present document, CCI will approve and the
Bank will review, the proposals to be executed annually within the project. For that purpose
the Bank and CCI will hold annual programming meetings as part of the annual project
reviews.



- 126 -                            Annex H
13. SELECT10 OF PROIECT PROPOSALS FOR  RE-INVESTMENT FUNDS
5.    The Regional Offices (OREMI) of the Ministries of Transportation and Public Works
(MTOP) and Livestock, Agriculture and Fisheries (MGAP), will receive the investment
proposals for l&D subprojects to be submitted by both, the private sector and the regional
offices of govertnment agencies requiring pre-investment funds to carry out the cotresponding
feasibility studies. Following a technical report from the Regional Irrigation Boards
(regarding technical and engineering aspects and agricultural matters), the OREMIs will
jointly analyze the proposals and send them with their report to CCI through the UEP for
further consideration and analysis. All investment proposals will include a project profile
with the basic data as indicated in the rules which the UEP will publish to that effect. Under
the overall coordination of UEP, the Regional Offices of MGAP and MTOP will have
updated information on average I&D infrastructure construction costs, production costs,
yields and agricultural prices in order to make a fast verification of the project information
submitted by the interested parties.
Pre-Requisites for Pre-Investment Funds
6.    Application for pre-investment funds may only be submitted for those proposals
having a project profile approved by the UEP, with the advice of the Regional Irrigation
Boards, and a feasibility analysis of the water use rights performed by MTOP (i.e., DH),
also advised by the Regional Irrigation Boards. Environmental impact assessment would be
carried out under the supervision of UEP in collaboration with DGRNR and DINAMA (SAR
paras. 2.69, 2.70 and 2.87). Projects in the protected area committed by Uruguay in the
1971 RAMSAR Treaty, may not apply.
Selection of Suboroect Proposals
7.    Based on the background information submitted by the interested parties, CCI will
decide every year which sub-projects will receive project funds to carry out the
corresonding feasibility studies. To make that selection, CCI will apply the criteria for
assignment of points and selection defined in the Table attached to this document. Rejected
proposals may be re-submitted, if appropriate.

C. CRITERMA FOR THE SELCION OF PROJECTS TO BE EXECUTED WT
FUNDS FROM TE NAUA RESOVRCES MANAGEMENT AND
IRRIGATION DEVELOPmENT PROGRAM
PiVedures
8.    The UEP will submit to CCI annually the background information on subprojects
competg for the investment resources.  The proposals selected from  that group will be
financed with project funds duing the following year. This financing will include the final
design and the execution of the work.



-129-                               Annex H
(a)   Only those sub-projects with a feasibility study and a study on environmental
impact may participate in the selection process for construction and
rehabilitation funds. Both studies must be duly approved by MTOP's
Hydrography Directorate and MGAP's Soils and Waters Directorate, with the
technical assistance of the Irrigation Boards;
(b) All sub-projects must be economically (social prices) and financially (private
prices) feasible. The economic (social) return rate must be equal to or higher
than 12%;
(c) Only those sub-projects whose designs and operational and maintenance plans
meet the requirements and propositions contained in the corresponding studies
on environmental impact, will be considered;
(d) Only those sub-projects having the support of the potential beneficiaries, tacitly
expressed in a letter of intent and with a good organizational level of the users
expressed in some form of juridical existence, will be considered; and
(e)   Only those sub-projects whose feasibility studies clearly specify the degree of
orgnization of the beneficiaries and their capacity to manage, operate and
maintain the anticipated works, identify the possible limitations they may
encounter and propose possible solutions, will be considered.
ldon Cdteda
9.    The net benefit/inversion ratio (N/K)1 will be calculated for those sub-projects
meeting the previously stated pre-requisites. Thereafter, sub-projects will be ranked by
decreasing number of points, accepting all those that may be financed with investment funds
available for the construction period of the projects. The availability of investment funds
will be established by CCI in accordance with the mles developed by the Planning and
Budget Office and the Ministry of Economy and Finance.
10.   The Government may exempt from the fulfillment of these rules those sub-projects
located in poor rural areas.2 However, these exceptions may not involve more than 30% of
the investment funds allocated to Small Farmers' I&D Schemes.
1.    The esumate of values will be made with the socia pces defined by MGAP on the occasion of the
MERCOSUR Project, updated according to what may haWen staring in 1992.
2.    The poor nura areas will be detrmined by MGAP, based on the criteria defined by the RIDA mission
of August 1992.
