URUGUAY
NATURAL    OURCES MAND IRRIG4TION
DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
INSTITUTIONAL RMEWORK
A. PUBLIC INSTITUTIONS
Mnisftr of Agriculture (MGAP)
1.    The Directorate of Soils and Water (Direcci6n de Suelos y Aguas (DSA) within the
General Directorate of Natural Resources (DGRNR) of the Ministly of Agriculture (MGAP))
was created in 1967, under a different name, and slightly modified in 1981. Its main
objective is to promote and regulate both utilization and conservation of soil and surface
water involved in the process of agricultural production. The most important activities of
this office have been: a) Soil and water evaluation: (i) in 1976 a soil map at 1:1.000.000
scale was produced for Uruguay as a whole, and since then several parts of the country were
mapped at 1:250.000 and 1:100.000 scales, (ii) special cartography maps were prepared at
1:20.000 scale for the country as a whole with soil associations, these being the basis for a
land tax system applied since 1978; and (iii) as a result of the Soil Conservation law (Ley
15,239) passed by Parliament in 1990, the registry of land under cropping, the evaluation of
erosion problems in cropping areas and the organization of a soil and water conservation
campaign, have been the main actvities undertaken. b) Research and extension activities: (i)
some research on water management has taken place in the Research Station of Ags
Blancas; (ii) there has been very little research on soil management; several extension
activities, mainly related to soil conservation and soil management have been undertaken
tbrough TV programs, field days and short seminars. c) Soil. water and plant analysis: there
are laboratories providing services to any farmer or institution requiring this kind of analysis.
d) Quality control: imported and domestic fertlizers are checked once they get into the
commercial system; and irrigation equipments are controlled through the granting of import
permits. The total staff of DSA includes 35 agronomists, 9 of them with post-graduate
degrees, 15 technicians, 29 field workers and 31 administrative staff.
2.    The Foresty Directorate (Direcci6n Forestal (DF)) of MGAP deals with the
promotion of forestry development, the planning of forest resources use, the promotion of
forest plant and seed production, the provision of technical assistance to private and public
institutions, the administation of public forests and the protection of forests against any kind
of damage such as fires, pests and others. Law 15939 in 1987 created a Forestry Fund to
finance forestry development and a Honorary Commission for the Administration of the
Forestry Fund, integrated by the Diretor of DF, one delegate from MGAP and one delegate
of the Ministry of Economy and Finance. In 1988 when loan UR-3131 with World Rank
was signed, an executing urit was created within the DF for the implementation of the
project. This executing unit has the main office in Montevideo and three offices in Young,



- 132 -                             Annex I
Tacuarembo and Montevideo, each of them staffed with director, 2 agronomist and 3
clercal workers. The total staff of DF including the Executive Director, consist of 22
agronomist specialized in forestry matters, 9 technicians and 191 clerical officers.
M1nitr of  nort and Public Works
3.    The National Directorate of HEdrography (Direcci6n Nacional de Hidrograla (DNH))
of the Ministry of Trnsport and Public Works (Ministerio de Transporte y Obras PNblicas
(MTOP)) has the power given by law 14.859 (C6digo de Aguas) to issue, modify and
withaw, water utilization rights. Water rights are handled by the Division of Hydrological
Resources (Divisi6n de Recursos Hidricos (DRH)). The DRH office has 4 departments, one
of which is the Department of Water Administration (Departamento de Administraci6n de
Aguas (DAA)) whose main responsibilities are the analysis of water utilization, the provision
of permits and authorizations, and the monitoring of water utilization rights, per basin. This
department has 5 regional offices in Artigas, Tacuaremb6, Cefro Largo, Rocha and Treinta y
Tres, and has 7 agronomists and 13 administrative staff. The Department of Hydrology of
DRH (Departamento de Hidrologia (DepH)) is responsible for the inventory and evaluation
of water resources and is staffed with 12 employees some of them at professional level. The
Divsion of Hydraulics and Rivers (Divisi6n Hidriulica y Fluvial (DHF)) through the
Deparment of Public Works (Departamento de Obras (DO)) is responsible for the execution
of works projected by any public office in the field of water management. Its staff includes
2 civil engineers and 7 technicians and administrators. The DRH collects no money from the
private sector. The DO collects money from farmers using water facilites which are public
property for irrigation purposes.
M-initryof Itdusbgy and Energy (MIE)
4.    The NationalDirectorate of Minin and Geology (Direcci6n Nacional de Mineria y
Geologia (DINAMIGE)) is institutionally located within the Ministry of Industry and Energy
(ME). There are two divisions, one responsible for ground water and the other for
geological studies and drilling activities. DINAMIGE bas a small laboratory for analyzing
water quality, mainly chemical aspects. They can't perform complete water quality analysis
for irigation purposes. The total staff amounts to 25 persons, 5 of them having university
degrees. There are 7 complete drilling equipments which are used for water exploitation.
nt  of Hou
5.    The National Directorate for the Environment (Direcci6n Nacional de Medio
Ambiente (DINAMA)) is part of the Ministry of Housing, Territorial Organization and
Enviromnent (Ministerio de Vivienda, Ordenamiento Territorial y Medio Ambiente
(MVOTMA)) recently created by Law 16.112 of 1992. DINAMA has responsibility in the
control of water resources quality and industrial liquid efluents. Protection of Ecosystems is
also under DINAMA responsibility. DINAMA is still poorly staffed and total budget is very
small compared to other ministries.



- 133 -                             Annex I
Natioal Commision for the Development of the Sant Luia River Bai
6.    The National Commission for the Development of the Santa Lucia Basin (Comisi6n
Honorara pan el Desarrollo de la Cuenca de Santa Lucia (CHDCSL)) is a honoraiy
commission created on December 1990 by the President of the Republic through Decree
581/990. Its objective is to promote sustainable regional development through coordination
of activities with the various public offices taking action in the Santa Lucia Basin. This
basin is very important in terms of water supply for human consumption and location of
major industrial plants. Also most dairy and horti-fruticultural activities take place within
this basin. The Honorary Commission is integrated by a staff of young professionals, and is
technically supported by the Office for Planning and Budgeting and the Ministry of
Agriculture. There is no hired staff and no budget supporting the Commission's activities.
The main office is in Montevideo and there is an additional office in the city of Santa Lucia.
B. REGIONAL IRRIGATION BOARDS
7.    There are no water users' organization apart from the Regional Irrigation Boards
(Juntas Regionales de Riego (JRR)) where public delegates, water users' representatives and
delegates from the non-irrigation farmers organization, deal with provision of annual water
right demands by farmers. They were created in 1970 by Decree 442 which establishes that
the presidency should be held by the delegate from MTOP and there should be one delegate
from MAPPI two delegates of the water community and two delegates of farmer unions.
The main tasks of JRR are to coordinate fair distnbution of water rights, be the arbiter when
problems related to water use are present, assist MTOP on the provision of water rights to
new demanders, assist both MTOP and MGAP about any investment involving creation of
new water supplies, help MTOP in keeping good records of water management projects in
the area under their responsibility. There are 6 JRR in Uruguay: Artigas, Tacuaremb6,
Yaguar6n, Treinta y Tres, Rocha and Montevideo; the latter covers the southern part of the
county. JRR have no budget at all and they do not take any action apart from advising
public and private institutions on issues under their responsibilities.
C. IPRODUCIION SUPPORT SERVICES
Institute of Aricultural Research (INIA)
8.    There are several public and private institutions involved in agricultural and forestry
research. The most important of them is the Institute of Afgcultural Research (Instituto
Nacional de Investigaci6n Agropecuaria (INIA)), involved in agricultural research and its
appLication to the Uruguayan agricultural sector. INIA is the successor to the various
research instutes that have worked under MGAP on agricultural production matus since
the beginning of this centry. In the early stages, these institutions gave priority to research
programs on crop production, with partcular emphasis on wheat production. Ia Fstanzuea,
located in the best cropping area in the country, was the only research ceter at that time. In
1958 research was radically reorganized and several new programs were started mainly in the



- 134 -                              Annex I
field of crop-pasture rotation and animal production. In 1968 another reorganization took
place and new research centers were created which started working with new products, such
as the rice in the Treinta y Tres research station, horticultural and fruit production research
in Las Brujas, and citrus research programs in Salto Grande. INIA was created in 1989 by
law 16065 as a public institution without being part of the public a ation system
(Entidad P6blica no Estatal). Both staff and research centers formerly belonging to the
Center for Agricultural Research (CIAAB) of MGAP, were transferred to INIA.
9.    The main objectives of INIA are: (i) to formulate and execute research programs that
generate new technologies and adapt them to the Uruguayan agricultural conditions; (ii) to
promote scientific and technological activities within the institution and in coordination with
other institution involved in agriculural research; and (iii) to effectively transfer new
knowhow to farmers through public and private extension and technical assistance agencies.
At present, INIA has 5 Research Stations, spread throughout the various agricultural regions
of the country. Research programs are classified in four areas: crop production, aninal
production, horti-fruticultural production and forestry production. In each area there are
several programs. In the crop production area the programs included are winter crops,
summer crops, rice and evaluation of "cultivares". In the animal production area the
programs are pasures, beef, dairy, sheep and goats and small farm animals (pigs, poultry
and others). In the horti-fruticulture production area three programs are included,
horticulture, fruticultue and citrus production. Total staff comprises 134 university
professionals, 55 technicians and 294 field and clerical workers. The 489 employees are
spread out in the various research centers and central offices. Montevideo office has 7%, La
Estanzuela Research Center (RC) 40%, Tacuaremb6 R.C. 13%, Treinta y Tres R.C. 14%,
Las Brujas R.C. 19% and Salto Grande R.C. 7%. La Estanzuela deals mainly with daily
and mixed cropping activities including pastures and beef; Tacuaremb6 with livestock rearing
activities; Treinta y Tres with rice; Las Brujas with horticulure and fmits; and Salto Grande
with citrus. INIA is fumded through a specific tax (4 0/00 of value of agricululral goods
marketed), a similar or bigger amount of money provided by Public funds, money gathered
through provision of services or selling of produce, and any kind of donation, gifts or other
kind of provision of money. There is no specific irrigation research program in INIA nor
any full time researcher specialized in this area. There is one full time professional dealing
with crop irgation matters and many irrigation research lines within programs such as rice,
summer crops, horticulture, fruit production and beef and dairy programs. INSA is involved
in the creation of water reserves in many research stations and very much interested in
expaiing research on irrigation items. The total annual budget of INIA amounts to
approximately US$5 million.
Facultr of Agronomy
10. The Faculty of Rural Science (Facultad de Agronomia (FA)) is part of the University
of the Republic. It is a public institution with independent administration. The main
functions of this faculty are to provide education at university level and carry out rsearch to
complement its educational programs. The main building is sited in Montevideo and there are
3 research centers: Paysandi, Salto and Cerro Largo, all of them in the nortern part of the
country. There is the intention to start a new research center in the southern part of the
coutry, near Montevideo. F.A. has a specific area dealing with soils and water with 40
university professionals, 5 of them working on irrigation subjects. Most research is



-135 -                              Amnex I
undertaken through comracts with public and private institutions such as citrus producdon
firms and MGAP programs.
Other ReParch hLtitutions
11. The Uruguayan Association for the Promotion of Zero Tillage (Asociaci6n Uruguaya
pro Siembra Directa (AUSID) was created in 1991 and is a private institution for research
and technical assistance purposes. It is funded by private firms and is located in the western
part of the country in Soriano.
12. Research in forestry has just started as a separate program in INIA. Research in the
Faculty of Rural Science (Facultad de Agronomia) was quite important from 1963 up to
1974, when the military goverment fired many researchers as a result of their political
action and cut down University spending. During that period the research center in
Paysand6 carried out severl important research programs, especially in cropping and animal
production subjects. Not much research was done in horticulture, fruit production and
forestry throughout that period. Some research in aninul health and animal production is
undertaken by the Vet    Research Center Miguel Rubino, of the MGAP. This
institution is based more towards animal health problems and it pays minor attention to
ni  management items. The Forestry Directorate (Direcci6n Forestal) of MGAP has
traditionally been, jointly with the Faculty of Rural Science, the only institution carrying out
forestry research in Uruguay. There are now a few research programs going on in INIA and
LATU (Laboratorio Tecnol6gico del Uruguay). The Forestry Directorate (through the
support obtained from the loan 3131-UR with the World Bank) has expanded the applied
forestry reseach program on forestry issues, mainly plantation systems and genetics.
13. There are some private research initiatives in particular production activities. Beer
production plants carry out some research programs on barley production. Cooperadves in
the north-western part of the country have several research programs on sugar cane
production under irrigation conditions; part of these programs work on a contract basis with
INMA. Many private institutions have been supporting joint research programs with INIA,
Faculty of Agronomy or other research institutions. Rice has been a typical example where
the main milling plants, rice producers and INIA support jointly, very successful research
programs on rice production with irrigation.
Extension and Technical Assistance
14. Extension and technical assistance services are provided by a wide range of public
and private institutions. Despite that, only 25% of farmers declared having received
technical assistance of any sort in 1986. There has been a clear increment in extension
activities with farmers during the 80's. In 1980 there were less than 20% of farmers
receiving any assistance from extensionists or technical advisors. Private assistae accounts
for 56% of total assistance received by frmers and it has grown sharply (95%) from 1970 to
1980 while durng the same period public assistance decreased 34%. There are big
differences between the 19 departments in terms of extension activities with farmers. In
some of them, such as Rivera in the northern part of the country only 8% of farmers
declared having received some assistance, while there are many other departments such as
Florida, Rio Negro, Paysandd and Soriano, where more than 30% of farmers received



-136 -                              Annex I
Florida, Rio Negro, Paysand( and Soriano, where more than 30% of famrs received
technical assistance. According to research results by CIEDUR market oriented faums
receive more technical assistance (49%) than family farms (15%).
15.   Technical assisance received by farmers varies with the kind of production in which
they are involved. Rice farmers are probably the ones receiving the highest level of
technical assistance in a more stable way followed by Citrus and Tobacco farmers. Daity
production fiamers receive a good standard of assistance but in a less stable and permanent
fashion. Livestock farmers receive very little technical assistance. In general, the larger the
farm the higher is the level of technical assistance in most production activities.
16.   There are about 21 different institutions involved in extension activities. MGAP has
8 different agencies providing technical assistance to farmers. Other government institutions
with certain extension activities are the Bank of the Republic, the Institute for Colonization
stituto de Colonizaci6n), LATU and the municipalities. Within the private sector the
cooperative movement is very important regarding extnsion and technical assistance
activities. Other important private institutions involved in assisting farmers are FUCREA
(Federaci6n de Grupos CREA del Uruguay), SUL (Secretariado Uruguayo de la Lana),
INAVI (Insdtuto Nacional del Vino), and SAMAN (Sociedad An6nima de Molinos
Afroceros).
17. The total mnuber of technical assistance personnel throughout the country and
intitutions is esfimated at 720. Most of them are civil servants (68%) of which more than
half are MGAP employees. The cooperative movement with about 140 technical assistance
agents has a strong technical assistance program. The southern region is better provided with
extension and technical assistance activities, than the rest of the country. There are 326
agents in the south, followed by 127 agents in the cropping area of the west and far fewer
agents in the rest of the country.
18.   There has been a trend to form group of farmers as a means to organize technical
assistuae. This has been the case of FUCREA and CONAPROLE (Cooperativa Nacional de
Productores de Leche). In 1990 there were about 240 groups of farmers. Dairy farm groups
accounted for more than half of total groups, followed by horticultural and fruit production
groups, crop fanming groups and finally livestock production groups. JUNAGRA (Junta
Nacional de la Granja) has developed a network of about 31 groups dealing with intensive
production activities. Generally each group has about 10 to 13 farms but in certain cases
groups can be much bigger in numbers. Each group has its own technica! assisne agent
working on a part time basis. Estimates of technical assistance coverage through groups of
farmers are the following: about 10% of livestock farms, 46% of dairy farms and a small
proportion of intensive production farms.
19.   The PENTA (Programa de Promoci6n de Exportaciones no Tradicionales
Agropecuarias) was created by Decree 552/989 in 1989. The honorary commission
responsible for PENTA activities is integrated by delegates from the MGAP, Ministry of
Economy and Finance, OPP, and JUNAGRA. The Coordinating unit is staffed with the
Gener Secretary, who is an agronomist, and 3 clerical staff. Its main objective is to
implemet the non thadional export component of Loan 3131-UR with the Bank, basically



- 137 -                            Annex I
through the promotion of new export projects, training of farm managers and market
research.
20.   Agricultural Credit is provided by private and public banks, agroindustries and by the
Cental Bank through external debt bond exchanges. Most of it is short-term credit, as no
medium- and long-term credit is provided by private banks, with the exception of recent
lending activities involving Loan 3131-UR with the World Bank. Only 12% of credit in
private banks goes to agriculture, 11% to finance livestock activities and 1% for cropping
purposes. Agricultural credit has decreased compared to other sectors of the economy. In
1980 agriculural credit accounted for 28% of total credit. Credit for livestock decreased
67% and credit for crops 39%. Total amount of agricultural credit from private banks was
US$147 million in 1989 against US$139 million from the BROU (Banco de la Republica).
21. Private bank credit interest rates have traditionally been higher than public bank rates.
In 1991, top, average and minimum rates were 180%, 159% and 140% respectively, while
BROU loans charges about 122%. On the other hand private banks have been much more
efficient in providing credit. Public banks, BROU in particular, have a traditionally negative
performance with enormous quantities of prerequisites and delays.
22.   BROU is the most important source of agricultural credit of any kind, and one of the
few sources of medium- and long-term credit. Almost any medium to big town or city in the
country has a BROU agency in it. The most important development programs, financed by
the World Bank and IDB, have been channeled through BROU. The evolution of
agricultural credit in BROU has experienced a sharp decrease since 1980 similar to the one
[decided?] for private bank credit. In 1990 agricultural long-term credit accounted for 30%
to 40% of total agricultural credit. The IDB Global Loan and the Plan Agropecuario
accounted for most of this type of loans. About 40% of total long-term credits were adjusted
through a product value basket system with low rate of interest. Long-term credit has been
normally geared towards investments in livestock activities. Very litle of it has been used in
more intensive production activities such as horticulture, fruit production and forestry. The
main items financed have been pasture improvement, water facilities, fencing, machinery and
replacement livestock. Since 1988 there have been special long-term credit lines for forestry
and non-traditional export crops but there have been hardly demanded by farmers.
23.   Some agroindusties such as CONAPROLE and SAMAN, involved in dairy
production and rice production respectively, have been important suppliers of credit to dairy
and rice farmers. They usually give credit for inputs and capital goods financing and then
recover the principal once famers market their production through them. They charge
higher interest rates than the banks but provision of credit is very efficient and timely.
24.   There have been three special programs of exeral debt bond exchange for long-term
projects since 1987. Many projects prented were agriculural ones. Forestry and citms
projects have been presented in almost all exchange plans.
25.   Credit ganed for water m agement and imigation through the Plan Agropecuario
has been less than 3% of total money granted. In three years, from 1990 to 1992, only



- 138 -                              Annex I
US$1.7 million were provided for water management purposes. In the case of the IDB loan,
from 1989 to 1991, almost US$1.5 million were granted for irrigation purposes during the
whole period, mainly for the financing of irrigation equipment. Since 1989 there has been
an important increase in credit demand for irrigation projects.
MEkeng
26.   Agricultural Marketing is basically handled by private firms. Direct Government
intervention is very rare. The Government's role in marketing has been, since the last
decade, the determmation of quality standards and satary restrictions, external tariffs and
tax reimbursement policies. The prices of virtally all agricultural products, with the
exception of milk for human consumption and grapes for wine production, are set by a
completely free market. International marketing has no restrictions apart from the
prohibiton on importng bulk wine and importing wheat when particular market conditions
prevail. There are no import or export quotas with the exception of garlic and soybean
imports from some ALADI countries.
27. External import tariffs for agricultural products are in the process of being reduced.
At present, the highest tariff for any product is 20%, the intermediate tariff is 15% and the
lowest one 6%. These tariffs are supposed to disappear, for imports from neighboring
countries, once MERCOSUR is fully enforced in 1995. The maximum extemal tariff
negotiated within MERCOSUR will probably be 20% in the near future. External export
taxes are very few. Apart from raw wool, in bone beef and hides, which are lightly taxed,
agricultural products pay no export tax at all. There has been various tax reimbursement
schemes, mainly directed to reimburse farmers for indirect taxes paid while producmg and
maketg products.
28.   Agricultural products are sold partly straight to consumers, either domestic or foreign
consumers, and partly to agroindustries. Every product has a particular pattern of
marketing. Beef and mutton animals are rarely exported live and most part of the products
goes to slaughter plants for meat processing. About 60% of wool goes to various
industrialiion processes before reaching the final consumer. Cereals and oil seeds are
partly processed in the country and partly is exported raw to foreign markets. Fruits and
vegetables are mainly consumed fresh by domestic consumers and exported with minimum
conditioning to world markets. A small proportion of fruit and vegetables, with the
exception of grapes, is industialized.

NATURAL RESOURCES MANAGEMN AN) IRRIGATION
DE rLPMN PROJEC
PROJECT ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
A. GENERAL INSaITUTIONAL FRAMEWORK FOR ROJEC EXECUION
1.    The proposed Program is multfsectoral and covers aspects related to natural resources
management and irrigation; agricultural research; forest development; exports promotion and
institonal strengthening. Execution of this complex Program would require a high level of
organization and coordination between different participating institutions.
2.    Although MGAP and MTOP will have a fundamental role in Program execution,
there are otber Government institutions, such as OPP, for its responsibility in budget
allocation and control, foreign cooperation coordination and national planning, which will
also have an important participauon in the decision-making process during Project execution.
3.   It is deemed necessary to create a coordination and decision-making body for the
Natural Resources Management and Irrigation Development Project (PRENADER) which
would be made up by the Ministers (or their representatives) of MGAP, MTOP and the
Director or Assistant Director of the OPP. To perform their functions this Interministerial
Coordinating Committee (CCI) would have a small high level technical team (the Project
Execuing Unit - UEP). This Unit, responsible for overall project management, will
coordinate the acivities of the different departments and directorates of the MGAP, MTOP,
MIE and MVOTMA that may have responsibility in the execution of the various project
activities.
4. CCI will meet at least three times per year, or at the request of the UEP's Executive
Secretary. The insitutions resonsible for the execution of the Project and not represented in
CCI, may attend the meetings but without the right to vote when matters of their concern are
being considered. The Executive Secretary would act as the committee's Secretary.
5.    Project execution would be carried out through specific agreements between UEP (by
delegation of the Minister of Agriclture as head of CCI) and the different responsible units.
6.    CCI functions, among others, would be to:
(a)   assume the responsibility for executig the proposed Natural Resource
Management and Irrigation Development Program. To discharge this
responsibility the committee may delegate implementation to UEP or the
Government institution it may select for that purpose;



- 140 -                             Annex J
(b) coordinate and supervise Program execution, evaluate the progress achieved
with the disbursements of the different components in terms of the established
goals, and adopt the necessary corrective measures in case of important delays
or deviations with repect to the original objectives;
(c)   approve the selection and contracting of studies, works, lines of action and
projects to be developed under the Program, which may be proposed through
UEP;
(d)   overview contracting of evaluation stdies of the Program's environmental
impact and to adopt when needed corrective measures based on the UEP's
proposals; and
(e)   hire, in agreement with the Bank, UEP's Executive Secretary and approve the
Program's execution plans and the contracting of the UEP's remaming
personmel.
7.    V&P will be presided over by a high level agricultural economist with experience in
the preparation and evaluation of investment projects, who will act as the Executive
Secretry. In addition to its Executive Secretary, the Unit will be made up of a
mulddisciplinary team composed of an Agriculturalist specialized in irrigation agriculture, a
civil (irigation) engineer with proven experience in procurement, a natural resource
management expert, an Accountant, and supportig secretarial staff. The main functions of
this Unit will be to:
(a)   execute and coordinate CCI resolutions;
(b)   receive and analyze the projects' background, programs and lines of action
submitted to it by the public and private sectors;
(c)   prepare and present the necessary background for CCI and the executing
agencies (i.e., MGAP and MTOP) to adopt the decisions for Program
execution;
(d)   prepare, together with MGAP's DGRNR and MTOP's Hydrography
Directorate, MlE's Mineralogy and Geology Directorate, and MVOTMA's
National Environment Directorate, the terms of reference for the feasibility
and enviromental impact studies and for the final designs of the irrigation
projects to be financed within the framework of the proposed Program, and
submit them to CCI for approval;
(e)   propose to CCI (i.e., MGAP and MTOP) for approval, the consultants who
will carry out the feasibility and environmental impact studies and would
prepare the final designs;
(f)   once the terms of reference have been approved and the consultants have been
hired, supervise the preparation of the feasibility and environmental impact
stdies and the final designs. In addition, together with MGAP's DGRNR,



- 141 -                             Annex J
MTOP's DH, MIE's DINAMIGE ai.d MVOTMA's DINAMA, it must
evaluate the quality of said studies for their final approval;
(g) cooperate with MGAP's DGRNR and MTOP's DH in the supervision and
monitoring of the environmental impact of actions executed within the sphere
of the proposed Program, and to inform CCI and the Bank periodically on the
subject;
(h)   secure the efficient coordination of the different program components;
(i)   centralize all information on natural resources management supplied by the
different institutions and organizations in charge of executing the different
components related to the subject; hire the required consultants who will
interpret the information; and to issue, together with the DGRNR, periodic
reports on the progress achieved;
(j)   monitor the execution of the different program components and send periodic
reports to CCI and the Bank;
(k)   recommend the adoption of the necessary measures to secure the required
technical, financial and institutional resources;
(I)   assume responsibility for the management of the Program funds and the
application of accounting procedures compatible with Bank guidelines, without
prejudice to the pertinent authority of CCI or other public entities; and
(m)  perform any other functions CCI may assign.

NATURAL RESOURCES MANAGEMENT AND IRRIGATION
DEVELOPMEN1 PROJECT
MONITORING PROGRAM ON WATER USE EFFICIENCY AND
SOIL CONSERVATION
A. BACKGROUD
1.    Expansion of irrigation has incorporated new productive areas into the national
domain in a sustained manner, amounting to some 160,000 ha from 1992-1993. This
expansion resulted from an increase in the area devoted to rice cultivation, and from the
miorporation of irrigated fniit and horticultural crops, com and forage.
2.    Uruguay has suitable land and adequate water resources to support this growth;
however, it is of the utmost importance that any possible impact on natural resource
conservation be minimized.
3.    The Water Code (Law 14,859 of 12/15/78) and the Law on Soil and Water
Conservation (Law 15,239 of 12/15/81) contain regulations favoring the efficient and
conservative use of water and soil, and penalizing practices and habits that result in poor
mnanagement of these resources. There is a regional decentralization mechanism continuously
under improvement by the Hydrography Directorate and MGAP's Directorate General of
Renewable Natural Resources, consisting of the installation of regional offices and promotion
of the establishment of additional Regional Irrigation Boards. In turn, every use of water for
irrigation must be preceded by an application to Hydrography and the Directorate General of
Renewable Naural Resources setting out the legal and technical infonnation on the land
where the water will be used, irrigation systems to be employed, soils to be irrigated and
production and rotation systems to be incorporated.
4.    Due to the dynamics of irrigated land development, where foreign companies
increasingly participate, and the need to adapt the available experimental information on the
subject, it is essential to have precise and updated technical information for each region.
according to real conditions affecting water and soil use efficiency. Knowledge derived from
this information will improve the decision-making process both at the level of Government
(i.e., MGAP and DU) and Regional Ilrigation Boards.
B. QB1&IE
5.    The main objective of the monitoring program is to determine water use efficiency
and impact of soi conservation practices in areas under irrgation, representative of each



- 148 -                               Annex K
region of the country. Preference will be given to lands with projects already registered in
MTOP and MGAP files (water use permits for irrigation), and representative of the different
facts regarding water sources (surface water, dams, perforations), irrigation systcms used (by
gravity, sprinkler, localized), soils and topographies of the irrigated farms, and crops or
production systems to benefit (rice, fruits and vegetables, industrial crops, extensive crops
and forage).
6.    The monitoring program will also provide, disseminate and demonstrate the results
obtained in each region, through the Regional Units of the Directorate General of Renewable
Natural Resources and the Regional Irrigation Boards. The intention is to promote a
balanced and environmentally sound use of water through the granting or cancellation of
permits, based on technical competence, and through dissemination and demonstration
activities addressed to technicians, producers and interested population.
C. ACTIVITIES
7.    The following activities will be carried out:
(a)   selection of 6-12 irrigation systems representative of each region and basin:
(i) Central and Northeem Littoral [Uruguay river basin]; (ii) North
fTacuaremb6 river and Rio Negro river basin]; (iii) East [Laguna Merin and
Atlantic Ocean basin]; and (iv) South [River Plate and Santa Lucia river
basin]; and
(b)   lab and field studies and determinations for each irrigation system, including
the following:
(i)  characterization of water source as to quantty and quality (vohlme of
surface streams or wells, volumes in dams, quality of available water)
and characteristics of works and structures (volumes of earth and height
of earthwork, flood areas, depth and diameter of perforations);
(ii) characterization of water consumption and extraction patterns,
conveyance and application systems (volume and quantities used,
pumping stations, canals and/or pipes, equipment or structures for
water application to the crops);
(iii) characterization of the irrigated land by vegetation and prior use (farm,
natural land, pasture, others), soils (description of properties and
infiltration speed measurement, apparent density and structural
stability), topography (slope and surface regularity, grading practices
and farm plot arrangements);
(iv) survey of crops and production systems to irrigate (species and
rotations, farming practices and yields per harvest); and



- 149 -                            Annex K
(v) determination of the relationship between production and the following
factors: quantity of water used and effect on quality, soil conservation
parameters, parameters of affected natural vegetaton (pasture and
natural woodland) and operational costs and depreciation of works and
equipment. An attempt will be made to quantify environmental
externalities (effect on water quality and soil conservation).
8.    The results of the studies and determinations will be used as follows:
(a) publication of results;
(b)   dissemination in meetings and field symposims;
(c) taken into account by Regional Irrigation Boards in granting the permits for
water use; and
(d) proposing the establishment or updating of technical standards on water use
and conservation in the areas under irrgation.
D. INSTTUTONS IN CHARGE
10.         The monitoring program to be supervised by UEP will be under the direction
of the Water Use and Management Division, with the cooperation of the apropriate
Divisions of the Soils and Water Directorate and the Directorate General of Renewable
Natiual Resources. The execution and implementation will be carried out by the Regional
Units of the Soils and Water Directorate (DSA). The Regional Irrigation Boards, the
Regional Offices of the National Hydrography Directorate and the Experimntal Stadons of
INIA will participate as cooperating agencies.

FISCAL IMPACT
ESTIMATE OF COST RECOVERY, THROUGH FISCAL ACTION,
OF INVESTMENTS FROM THE NATURAL RESOURCE MANAGEMENT AND

1.    The amount of fiscal recovery was estimated on each of the projects for which
feasibility studies are being made, as well as the recovery value for water sources
rebabilitation and generation works, which will be included in the Program in the next years.
2.    Separate calculations were made for the recoveries corresponding to the works
themselves, and those resulting from the productive processes.
3.    Prtial information was available on the recovery levels of works corresponding to
two of the five projects under study, therefore the levels of recovery are approximate. In the
case of the productive process recovery, information was available from the models of three
feasibility studies. This information was complemented with the results of an agriculhural
taxation report prepared by OPYPA (MGAP) in 1991.
4.    To estimate the Present Net Value of investment flows and fiscal recoveries, a
discount rate of 6% was applied on annual values.
5.    Within the framework of restrictions for a refined estimate of the fiscal recovery, it is
considered that the latter may amount in some cases to approximately 43% of total
invesunent.

URUGUAY
NATURLm RESOURCES MANAGEMENT AND
IRRIGATION DEVELOPMENT PROJECr
ENVIRONMENTAL ASPECTS
1.    Itrduction
The Natural Resources Management and Irrigation Development Project
(PRENADER) aims to enhance productivity and efficiency in agriculture through sound
management of soil and water resources and minimization of adverse effects on natural
ecosstems. Biodiversity is not expected to suffer any significant degradation since the
project would not finance any irrigation and drainage activity within areas defined for
protcon and preservation under natural conditions.
In the development and operation and maintenance (O&M) of irrigation projects, a
nber of standard environmental issues have been identified, such as soil degradaton and
water contaminaton from agrochemicals under intensified agriculture, wastefil use of water,
deteriorating quality of irrigation water, sedimentation of reservoirs and damage to
biodiversity.
As a result, it can be stated that the project is essentially oriented to support a
susinable development of irrigated agriculture which implies a careful environmental
management, with particular emphasis on water and soils. Within such a conceptual frame
the project should be regarded either as positive or neutral from an environmental point of
view, since most activities are designed to improve environmental quality or, at least, to
avoid enviromnental degradation.
Yet, the project component covering the irrigation sector includes irrigation schemes
which might potentially generate adverse impacts on the environment, as a whole or on some
of its components. Accordingly, a screening system for classifying irrigation schemes to be
financed through PRENADER has been incorporated into the operation plan. The screening
procedure will determine the type of environmental assessment required prior to approval.
Additionally, project components related to the dissemination of soil conservation practices,
water quality and research on irrigation techniques and natural resources management have
been stuctued to help solving potential environmental issues derived from intensification of
land use and expansion of irrigated agriculture.



- 156 -                             Annex M
2.    ProJect Desription
The proposed project aims to:
(a)   increase and diversify sustainable agricultural production and exports through
expansion of irrigation and improved water use within a context of sound
enviromnental practices; and
(b)   substantially enhance natural resources management in rainfed and irrigated
agriculture.
As designed, the project would contain the following components:
(a)   natural resources management, comprising (i) pilot development schemes in
micro-catchment areas of the Santa Lucia River Basin, to gain experience in
the management of ecologically fragile zones; (ii) a pilot program for
development of water catchment works at farm level in Yaguari basin;
(iii) demonsaion farms in other agro-ecological areas suffering soil
degradation and erosion problems; (iv) applied research in natural resource
management and irrigation techniques; (v) a computerized geographical
information system; (vi) support to forestry development; and (vii) studies on
natural resources management related issues;
(b)   Trrigaon development, comprising (i) the rehabilitation and modernization of
selected public irrigation and drainage works and their transfer to the private
sector under appropriate operation and maintenance arrangements; (ii) of
feasible medium-scale irrigation works proposed by private farmer groups;
(iii) small farmer sponsored construction of small-scale tubewells and pumping
stations and other kinds of surface water catchment works for irrigation;
(iv) feasibility studies of possible irrigation investment projects; and
(v) technical support for the promotion of non-traditional exports.
3.    Basfelin Data
Irrigation is practiced in Uruguay almost entirely in the eastern, northern and southern
regions which are defined and differentiated fru  each other on the basis of soils pattern,
water resources, crop types, kind of farmers, producer organizations and existng
infrastuctur. Climatic regimes are about the same throughout the country, although some
regional differences might be described: sunlight increases to the north, whereas cold spells
in smmer are more frequent in the south.
The three regions account for 95% of the national irrigated area and all relevant
irrigated crops belong to at least one of them.



-157 -                             Annex M
The Eastern Region
The eastern region comprises parts of the departnents of Cerro Largo, Treinta y
Tres, Rocha and Lavalleja and covers approximately 12% of the national ternitory. Beginning
in the early 30s, private initiative developed the region as the main rice growing area in the
country where 95.000 ha of rice (75% of total national area) are cropped annually on flat
lands where low to very low hydraulic conductivity soils prevail. This feature makes
irrigation more feasible since infiltration losses are extremely low on paddy fields and in
conveyance structures.
The soils of the central and northemn parts of the region (Lavalleja, north of Rocha,
Treinta y Tres and Cerro Largo) are mainly Planosols and Solods, low in organic matter,
with a loamy topsoil 20 - 30 cm thick underlain by a heavy textured, almost impervious
claypan. The terrains are flooded for short periods of time, once in a number of years, by
river spill water, notably from the Olimar and the Cebollati rivers, during exceptionally high
floods. As a consequence, no important drainage structures are needed for safe cropping.
The southern part of the region includes mostly the lowlands of Rocha with
dominance of Gleysols, high in organic matter (local peat) and heavy textured (clay loam or
clay) throughout the soil profile. This zone comprises the enclosed flatlands that are
temporarily or permanently submerged by standing rainwater unless artificially drained
and/or flood protected. In fact, extensive aras were partially or totally reclaimed for rice
cropping since the late 70s by the combined action of government and private initiative. As a
result, extensive flood protection, drainage and irrigation infrastructure has been built during
the last decade.
Environmental conflicts, not yet solved, make it advisable not to consider the
lowlands of Rocha eligible for further irrigated agriculture development under present
conditions. Since the mid 80s, about 435.000 ha are supposed to be protected under the
Ramsar Treaty making part of the wetlands of international importance, especially as habitat
for migratory or resident waterfowl. Enviromnental conflicts derive from inconsistent
goverment actions: promotion and/or direct execution of drainage and irrigation
inrastructure, and, almost simultaneously, adhesion to the Ramsar Treaty inscribing as
protected wedands a large tract of territory which comprised the areas already reclaimed in
1984 or proposed for reclamation. The result has been an escalation of conflicts between
agriculture and environment protection, between different government agencies, between
government and environmental NGOs and between either government or NGOs on one side
and either land owners or rice growers on the other. In addition, conflicts have also arisen
within the agriculture sector (upstream versus downstream land users), although they concern
hydrological rather than environmental issues since they relate to private drainage and flood
protection infrastructure built with no consideration of a regional plan for water management
(even if environmental issues were neglected).
Other predictable environmental issues during development of the project in the
eastern region will be adequately treated and prevented since most of PRENADER
components are geared towards improving resource management and enhancing productivity
and efficiency in agriculture in the long term (sustainable production), which implies
reduction of soil erosion and degradation, conservation of water quality for diversified use



- 158 -                            Annex M
and preservation of biological diversity. This means that prevention of water pollution by
agrochemichals (fertilizers, herbicides, insecticides, etc.) will be effective and soil erosion
reduced to a minimum. The latter point is easy to achieve since present and future agriculture
will be located on the flat lands where potential for soil erosion is niegligible.
The Northern Region
The northern region comprises part of the departments of Artigas, Salto, Tacuaremb6
and Rivera and covers almost 25% of the national territory. On the flat lands of the
Tacuaremb6 and Uruguay river basins, 30.000 ha of rice (25% of total area) are cropped
annually under cropping systems similar to those used in the eastern region.
Dominant soils in the northern fladands have mostly a loamy topsoil of variable
thickness and a claypan or claypan-like subsoil; hydraulic conductivity is generally low or
very low. Since the region has extensive areas of good soils not yet under agricultre, the
potential exists for increasing the rice area in the near future, provided enough irrigation
water is made available. Increase of the area under rice is very likely because irrigated land
in the eastern region has already been almost completely developed and little land might be
added in the future, at least under present cropping system of long rotations.
Because there is no shortage of suitable land for rice cultivation in the northern
region, no attempts have been made for reclaiming wetlands since the latter require
additional investments in drainage and flood protection. However, extensive areas of
weedands of relatively unknown importance as habitat for native fauna exist along the main
steams. At present, no legal protection affects these northern wetlands although at least
some of them might reasonably be declared protected areas in the future, either within or
outside the Ramsar Treaty. Yet, only one ill-defined area has been identified in the northern
region wetlands as valuable for protection (Riverine forests of the Tacuaremb6, Negro and
Yaguari rivers) and, moreover, it is not included within the selected 16 priority areas out of
a total of 36 that have potential for protection (Estudio Ambiental Nacional, OPP/OEA/BID,
1992).
Sugar cane, citrus, deciduous fruits and horticultural crops are also cultivated under
irrigation in the northern region, totalling more than 13.00 ha. Most of these crops are on
gently undulating lands; lowlands and wetlands are avoided because of (micro) climatic
reasons, unsuitable soils or reducing investment in infrastructre for drainage and flood
protection.
The area under non-rice crops in the northern region has not been a source of
environmental conflicts and they should not likely arise in the future. No drainage of
wetlands is predictable and soil erosion and degradation and soil and water pollution should
be under control because of general and specific objectives of the project.
As a general recommendation it can be stated that no drainage of wetlands in the
northern region should be financed or encouraged under the project because of two reasons.
On the one hand, there is a high availability of suitable land for expansion of irrigated or
rainfed agriculture which makes unnecessary reclamation of permanently or temporarily
flooded additional land for such purposes. On the other hand, the possibility exists that part



- 159 -                            Annex M
or all of those wetlands might be officially designed in the future as natural reserves,
particularly if some of the reclaimed wetlands in the eastern region are removed from the
Ramsar Treaty area. The Ramsar Treaty states that any site removal should be justified by
urgent reasons of national interest and should be compensated by inscribing additional
wetlands of similar importance located elsewhere. The wetlands along the Tacuaremb6,
Negro and Yaguari rivers might reasonably qualify as a Ramsar site and designated in the
futare for substitution of lost wetlands in Rocha. Even if that is not the case, the riverine
wetlands are already proposed by the Estudio Ambiental Nacional (EAN) for protection,
mainly because of the ecological value of their natural forest cover. The fact that protection
of these riverine forests was not considered of the highest priority by EAN is a minor point
if it is accepted that pressure for natural protected areas designation and development is
increasing within the civil society, mainly through the activities of environmentalist NGOs
and legal decisions promoted by the Congress.
The Southern Region
The southern region comprises the departnents of Colonia, San Jose, Canelones and
Montevideo where about 11.000 ha are irrigated for horticultural crops and orchards.
Intensive land use for 100 years or more under rainfed non conservationist agriculture has
caused extensive erosion and degradation of soil physical properties. Soil losses and non
careful use of agrochemicals are further causes for loss of water quality in rivers and
reservoirs affecting drinking water supplying plants (need for more intensive treatment of
water, eutrophication, etc.).
The project will contribute to correct or mitigate some of the major environmental
issues detected in this region, particularly those concerned with soil erosion which is the
main source of suspended particles and soluble ions in nual areas that contribute to sildtg up
of streams and dams and eutrophication of lakes and reservoirs. With this scope, the project
is obviously beneficial from an environmental point of view and complements current
programs concerning soil and water conservation (MGAP, MTOP), water quality monitoring
(DINAMA), pesticides use and control (MGAP, DINAMA), which are under way or in the
initial stage of implementation aside of PRENADER.
Environmentl conflicts derived from possible wetlands reclamation are not likely to
arise since only relatively minor areas of marshlands or flooded lowlands occur within the
southern region, namely the lower Santa Lucia river and other small rvers floodplains next
to Rio de la Plata. Even though wedands drainage in the southern -egion appears not
probable, the project should not support or fund actions outside them that might lead to
environmental detenoration through modification of their hydrologic regimes, considerng
both the volume and quality of runoff reaching the wetlands.
The Estudio Ambiental Nacional (see above) did not select the Santa Lucia and Rio de
la Plata wetlands either with high or low priority for protection. Yet they are included in the
list of Uruguay's important wetlands published by IUCN (International Union for
Conservation of Nature), one of the best-known international NGOs, and working with a
solid scientific basis.



- 160 -                             Annex M
Again as a general rule, prevention of damage of the limited area of wetlands in the
southern region should be one of the rationales of the project. This contributes to
conservation of biological diversity through preservation of representative natural ecosystems
and does not result in constraints for the project since land availability is not a limitation for
development of irrigated agriculture in the region.
4.    Expced Environmental Imnats
The major fundamentals of obvious or probable environmental impacts expected from
development of irrigated agriculture promoted by PRENADER, have been set out in para 3.
A deepe: analysis of the issue leads to the separate consideration of three different sites for
the occurrence of enviromnental impacts, at least for most of the irrigation schemes to be
developed, which are based on gravity irrigation from dams: (a) upstream issues1, (b) on-site
issues, and (c) off-site (downstream) issues.
Upstream environmental issues in most of the projected schemes are related to
contribution of sewage or industrial effluent to reservoirs, build-up of nitrogen and
phosphorus in them (eutrophication), likelihood of contamination by agro-toxins and silting
up of dams due to soil erosion. It is expected that most of the mentioned factors will not play
a significant environmental (negative) role in the schemes to construct or that might be
executed in the future. Since the dam catchments are mostly located in rural areas of low
density, dispersed population, both domestic and industrial discharges will not represent a
threat to water quality entering the reservoirs. However, in some cases it will be essential to
monitor the location of new industries in the catchment, particularly in the few reservoirs
located at short distance downstream of medium or large towns, to prevent water pollution.
Control of already existing industries or other possible sources of contaminating effluent is
under the authority of DINAMA and legal instrumnents exist, and are actually applied, to
enforce the construction and habilitation of the industrial treatment plants required to satisfy
the standards accepted for such effluent.
Soil erosion and subsequent sedimentation in the reservoirs may be significant when
inadequate agriculture is practiced in the catchment. This will be the case in projects located
in rural areas of higher population density (e.g. Canelones) and intensive land use.
Provisions will be adopted by the project with the purpose of reducing erosion rates and
amsociated sedimentation; extension and financial support will be of great help to succeed in
erosion control efforts.
On-site issues relate to water logging, threat to endangered species due to flooding in
the reservoirs and conversion of land to intensive agriculture, salinization, destruction of
wetlands or other sensitive ecosystems (through reclamation or drastic changes in water
regimes, clearing of unique vegetation types, etc.) contamination of ground water, conflict
with alternative uses of the reservoir or the land, accelerated erosion due to intensified land
use and increase in water-borne disease.
Many of the on-site potential impacts tentatively identified are not likely to occur
everywhere or are easy to deal with through appropriate engineering designs. So is the case
for salinization hazard, so far unknown in existing irrigation systems in Uruguay and easy to



- 161 -                            Annex M
control with adequate drainage. It is proposed that increased soil erosion and contamination
by agrochemicals will be dealt with, when necessary, through "high technology". For soil
erosion control, this means contour cropping, low flow systems for water application,
carefully planned rotations according to soil erosion hazard, minimum soil tillage, use of
organic fertilizers and amendments and incorporation of green crops in the rotations to build
humus and nitrogen (the latter by N-fixing legumes). The risks derived from use of
agrochemicals resulting in residues on fruit and vegetables and contamination of aquifers
would also be offset by "high technology't: use of new selective herbicides, use of
insecticides and fungicides with minimal residues, careful use of nitrogen fertilizers based on
proper soil test and assessment of precise crop requirements in order to miniimize nitrogen
runoff into surface or ground water. Water-borne disease should be regarded as a very minor
or negligible threat since expansion of rice area to 120.000 ha throughout the country over
the past 15 years has not given as result any signs of malaria or schistomiasis. The problem
had already been analyzed in the mid 70s while assessing environmental impacts of the
construction of hydroelectric Salto Grande dam. So far no signs of water-borne disease have
been identified in that area and consequently, neither are they expected in comparable areas
under the project.
Rservoirs usually become attractive for waterfowl, at least temporarily and especially
during drought; this impact should be considered beneficial from an environmental point of
view.
Environmental conflicts associated with the threat that irrigation activities may pose
for the wetlands officially protected by the 1971 Ramsar Treaty have been already discussed.
Steps will be taken in project implementation to ensure that no irrigation activities will be
supported within the Ramsar site or other protected areas. Further, in the environmental
assessments of specific projects proposed in the eastern region, attention will be given to
potential downstream impacts on all officially reserved wetland areas or those proposed for
protection. The original area of the Ramsar Treaty is at present under revision and a
technical mission of the Convention Bureau has been very recently assisting the Government
to re-define the site and design sound plans and rules for the management of the redefined
area. Obviously, one of the main guidelines of such rules will be not to alter the water
regime of the Ramsar wetlands in order to ensure the conservation of the ecological
characteristics which justify its definition. Though no accurate figures can be advanced, it
seems reasonable to assume that: (a) the future Ramsar area will be smaller than the original
one, at least in the short term, and (b) it will not cover a continuous though irregular area,
but will rather comprise separate individual areas which, independently from each other,
qualify as Ramsar sites. Some significant tracts of land that have been drained, flood-
protected and put under irrigation in the last 10 years no longer qualify for the Ramsar
Treaty requirements, as already stated in 1988 (Monitoring procedure mission for the
Bafiados del Este; P. Duggan & A. Diegues; report to the Government of Uruguay). These
areas should be removed from the Ramsar site. On the other hand, some wetlands not yet
included in the site might be added, since they clearly qualify as Ramsar wetlands and have
been recommended for such purpose by different national and international specialists.
Litoral lagoons and associated marshlands and sandbars along the coast of Maldonado and
Rocha are good examples of potential Ramsar sites. The same might be true for some
wedands in Paysandfi, along the Uruguay river, and in Tacuaremb6 along some major
streams mentioned above.



-162 -                             Anmex M
Besides the Ramsar Treaty, protection of areas of particular value from the ecological
point of view has been established by legal or administrative ways. The law NO 16.170 of
November 28, 1990, mandates the Ministry of Household, Environment and Territorial
Planning (MVOTMA) to delineate the boundaries of a number of areas (e.g., wetlands along
the coast of the Merim Lagoon, Negra Lagoon and associated wetlands, and Jos6 Ignacio,
Garzon, Rocha and Castillos Lagoons). The delineation was finished and the areas so defined
were approved by the Government (Decree N 527 of October 1992). Some of these areas
are included in the original Ramsar site whereas others (most of the littoral lagoons) fall
outside of it. Whether included or not, its legal status makes them not eligible for irrigated or
raided agriculture as well as for any intensification of land use affecting its hydrology, its
natual vegetation and fauna and its scenic and cultural values.
With wetlands excluded for irrigation development, it is evident that rice cultivation
on non floodable flatlands will result in an increase in the use of fertilizers and pesticides
(excluding perhaps insecticides) and so risks exist of increase of the chemical content of
surface and ground water. No evidence of contamination has so far been detected in runoff
and water tables of the traditional rice areas of the country, though it may be argued that no
specific research has been conducted with that purpose. Monitoring of surface waters is
recommended to be considered and in fact DINAMA is already worldng on this issue, based
on a nationwide (though not dense) network of water sampling stations. The intensification of
field and laboratory work on water quality control is under way, and the eastern rice area is
a target region for water monitoring; yet, the use of "clean technologies" (as far as possible)
as descnbed above should contnbute to offset environmental negative impacts. Actually, the
long rotation of rice followed by 5 to 6 or more years of pasture has as a main purpose to
keep the use of expensive herbicides as low as possible. This purely economic objective
means that the rice cropping system in Uruguay might be considered ecologically 'soft" as
compared with more intensive rotations or even continuous rice, where the amount of
herbicide used is higher and the area treated every year is much larger. These considerations
do not imply that water quality monitoring might be ignored; it will be a need.
Off-site, downstream issues are in many instances the same as those listed for on-site
coflicts, but risks to population, infrastcmture and natural resources due to dam failure must
be added. This last point is to be considered with different weight for different specific
projects, because the number of settlements, individual homesteads, roads and other
infrastructure components is significantly different in each one. However, it has been assured
that engineering design standards and supervision of construction by the PRENADER
executing unit will meet international dam safety requirements.
Downstream changes in the timing, quality or quantity of water flows below dams
will not be significant in most cases, both for population and for wetlands and endangered
species. However, some exceptions to this general statement may be identified and should be
adequately considered. If a significant population settlement occurs downstream, some form
of consultation with the community will be necessary on this issue and on the advantages and
disadvantages of the whole project. Similarly, an irrigation scheme to be developed upstream
of an important wetland might be prejudicial if, as a result of its operation, the volume and
qualiiy of water reaching the wedand are negatively affected. This situation should be
managed with caution to avoid damage to natural mrsorces and habitat conditions in the off-
site wedands. Yet, it may be assumed that such a situation would occur only very rarely if



- 163 -                            Annex M
ever, given the territorial scope, the production aims and especially, the overall sound
management of natural resources pursued by PRENADER.
5.    Analvsis of Alternatives
The process of environmental screening and site selection of sub-projects is largely
included in chapters 3 and 4. The existing protected areas or those officially identified as
deserving some degree of protection will not be considered eligible for development of
irrigated agriculture. The basic information concerning these areas is available at different
government agencies involved with the subject (MGAP, MVOTMA) and any sub-project
proposal should be checked against the list of protected areas to identify and assess
environmental conflicts before any decision is undertaken. When a sketch map of location of
all potentially protected areas (included those already defined as such) is observed, it is
realized that most of them are situated in terrains where irrigation development is not feasible
or advisable because of many reasons other than those of environmental nature. Such is the
case for many areas on hilly and rocky soils (sierras), on coastal sands and beaches and on
river or ocean islands.
Environmental conflicts would arise in areas of wetlands and riverine forest lowlands
(wetlands of Rocha, littoral lagoons and associated marshes and forest lowlands of
Tacuaremb6). Since PRENADER will not fund or support sub-projects affecting such fragile
and environmentally valuable ecosystems, it is inferred that an effective screening is available
for avoiding conflicts between production and environment when taking decisions concerning
these subjects.
Other environmental issues to be dealt with are concerned with the design of the
projects themselves in order to consider, timely and adequately, its objectives, its strategy
and its consequences both in output and in environmental impact. This is so because the
project itself provides for sound natural resources management and promotion of sustainable
agricultural production through inclusion of these issues as major components (16% of total
cost).
The current capacity of central government agencies and local authorities to carry out
environmental impact assessments and monitor the application of corrective measures that
might be suggested by those assessments is rather weak at present. Yet, the project will
provide for strengthening of institutional capacity of MGAP on this subject (3 % of total
cost). At the same time, DINAMA will be receiving in the near future grants from IDB and
from EEC to strengthen its capacity to deal with environmental questions, environmental
impact assessment being one of the major fields to be covered by the technical cooperation.
As a result, a significant increase in technical capacity within government agencies
concerned with environment issues can be expected over the next two years. This will be a
significant contribution to environmental screening of projects of different nature and scope
(not only PRENADER).



-164 -                             Aimex M
6.        M;gtigain Yans
General environmental mitigation measures have been enumerated and discussed
under chapters 3, 4 and 5, with particular reference to specific projects and related measures
aiming to mitigate adverse impacts. Even if a sound screening of proposed sub-projects is
carried out, there will be the need for mitigation plans and specific actions to deal with some
inevitable consequences of irrigated agriculture.
Environmental impact assessment (EIA) is the basis for defining the need and extent
of mitigating measures. At present, a law of EIA is being discussed at the Congress; once
approved there will be a legal frame regulating the need of EIA for specific projects, the
terms under which they should be executed, the way they should be evaluated by the
responsible government agency and the timely incorporation into the project of the mitigation
measures that might be needed, but are not included in the project proposal.
It is expected that when PRENADER operations begin, the EIA law will be approved
and effective.
The environmental profiles for specific identified projects take into consideration the
necessary prevention or mitigation measures to keep environmental impact at acceptable
levels of international standards. The measures include upstream, on-site and downstream
issues that lead to the recommendation that the projects be enviromnentally classified as "C"
(i.e., no additional environmental studies required as prerequisites to final design). Only one
profile project is recommended for further work before definite environmental classification;
the additional work is conditioned by a ETA of flood control benefits, multiple-use reservoir
management options and a survey of the views of the downstream population with regard to
the possibility of dam failure.
Mitigation of environmental issues are part of the specific projects by inclusion of
agricultural "clean technologies"; soil conservation practices, when needed, to control
erosion and physical degradation; reduced inputs of agrochemichals and priority given to
those accepted as enviromnentally "soft"; water quality monitoring; assurance that
engineering designs satisfy international safety requirements.
7.    Environmental Monitoring and Evaluation
The physical environment of sub-project areas is generally well known since enough
information exists about the natural resources throughout the country. Yet additional
information concerning a specific resource might be needed for a specific area in some sub-
projects eventually proposed or for areas where the physical environmental conditions have
substantially changed since previous surveys. This will not be the general situation, but it
will be the case in areas modified by human action as mentioned for some former wetlands
in the eastern region.
Physical environmental conditions in sub-project areas will be monitored by technical
assistance agents working closely with farmers and supplied by DGRNR through its water



-165 -                             Annex M
and soils specialists. Additionally, DGRNR staff will receive international technical
assistance through PRENADER in the field of soil and water management and conservation.
The monitoring and supervision of institutional performance will be dealt with by the
Project Executing Unit (UEP) which will have resources for contracting specialists in
environmental impact assessment.
8.    IlLstitutional Strengthening Needs
As mentioned above, institutional capacity to deal with environmental issues -
including monitoring, evaluation and eventual penalization - is still rather weak in the
country. MOVTMA is acting just for the last 2 years and other government agencies show
similar structral weakness as well. Insufficient funding and quantitative lack of qualified
human resources contribute significantly to institutional weakness. Thus it can be concluded
that institutional strengthening is fundamental for success of the project.
The main responsibility for project implementation would be within MGAP, with
MTOP, through its Directorate of Hydrography, playing an important role in the allocation
of water rights and the construction of main irrigation works. An analysis of the capacity of
the various insdtutions was carried out as part of the preparation of the project to determine
the ability to implement the various components. As a result, some gaps were identified in
the institutional capacity, particularly in the General Directorate of Renewable Natural
Resources (DGRNR) within MGAP, for which technical assistance and training programs
have been defined. These include the institutional frame for the execution of the project, the
operation of a Geographical Information System (GIS) for centalization, compilation and
combining all the information generated and for periodical diffusion of available information
with the purpose of improving natural resources management.
National and international consultants and experts will be engaged for training
DGRNR personnel in the application of GIS and for assisting the institution and its technical
staff in planning of investments, defining the variables to be monitored and the way they will
be measured.
Additionally, to improve coordination between the main ministries involved in project
execution, an Inter-Ministerial Council headed by MGAP would be created and supported by
a strong Project Executing Unit.
DINAMA will also benefit from technical assistance for institutional strengthening
financed by grants of IDB and EEC. The main components of the program include the
development of an Environmental Information System, training of technical staff in
monitoring environmental variables with emphasis on EIA.
Institutional strengthening of MGAP and DINAMA are important contributions to
assure success of PRENADER and improving environmental management either within or
outside the project area.
